THE RED PAGE .
PUZZLED,
I’m a bachelor of thirty and a wild, erratic cove,
And I’m rather sorely puzzled by the number that I love.
I’d marry quick, but, hang it all ! my brain is in a whirl,
For in each one of these places I’ve a true and waiting
girl.
Bnregurra, Burrumbeet, and Badaginnie too,
And Barnawartha, Berrigan, and Bungaree and Whroo,
And Darlimurla, Derrinal arid Dandenong and Dean,
And Toombollup, Toongabbie, Tooradin and Toolleen.
I’ve been a bit promiscuous in loving, and I know
In common garden justice I deserve "a little woe ;
But Pin simply flabbergasted and my senses are at strife—
To which one of these places will I go to get a wife ?
Gheimghap or Goorambat, Goyura or Gritjurk,
Gerangamite, Glenrowan, or Pannooboomawm or Bourke,
Or Moorabool, or Mooraduc, or Montajup, Mcryhu,
Myaniyn or Murtoa, or Mysia or Kew ?
You think I’m only joking, and account my conduct
queer, .- . -
Or put it down to shiftiness, or shallowness, or—beer ;
But you'd do a lot of thinking ere you’d end a rovinglife
If in each one of these places you could find a willing wife.
Natimuk, Nathalia, and Nooramunga, too,
And Nar-nar-goon and Narracan, Nyora and Mirboo,
And Pomborneit and Piggoreet, Purdeet and PyalOng,
And Wooragee and Wycheproof, and Waubra and War-
rong.
The moral’s on the surface ; if you long for wedded bliss
Just take the first girl willing, and a/lot of worry miss.
And a willing maid is winking, and you ’re sure to find a
flaw,
Although you cull the lot from Cue to Boomahnoomoonah,
v. Ben Sun.

PERCIVAL C. COLE.
QUEENSLAND BUSH TO TOWNSHIPS
I sing pot to-dav of lands far away,
-Of cities all bowered in green ;
No reason to roam—niv song is of home,
Where our plucky bush townships are seen.
There’s mostly a drought that lingers about,
And shortens the water-supply ;
A bath’s a rare treat—don’t dream of fat meat l
And the bare town-reserve makes one sigh.
At — ' --
Allendale, Adavale, Alice and Annerley,
Alderley, Abingdon, Ascot and Anthony,
Ari'ilalah, Argentine, Asmus, Altken Vale,
. Augabhella, Aucbenfipwer, Ay ton, Avondale.
How the good folk will chat on this thing and that i
And there’s music wherever they dwell :
Aecordeon, flute, Jew’s harp—no orie’s mute,
They can sing, play, and step-dance as well.
Their races and balls, my memory recalls
With happiest thoughts in their train,
But life was a treat, and time all too fleet
Just after six inches of rain.
In
Ballandean, Burdekin, Banana, Birralee,
Bogantungan, Bingera, Bororen, Bingaree,
Bouidercombe, Boolburra, Barcaldine, Bundamba,
Blythedale Bluff, Bloomsbury, Buderinj, Birthamba.
Don’t label me “ cracked,” for sad is the fact
(Here Washington courage affords);
Unequalled my pain—but over-much rajn,
Like the drought, leaves them all drunk as lords!
Not teetotal mokes, but pluckiest folks,
Bright, hopeful, and kindliest towns,
The best mates must part—’tis the wish of my heart
You may prosper’midst Fortune’s worst frowns !
Hound —
Tambourine, Tungamull, Turallin, Torbanlea,
Thargomindah, Tamaree, Talgai and Torquay,
Tangorin, Tewantin, Tiriaiia, Te Kowai,— .
Brave Queensland townships, may you hold your own
for aye I

MARY HOUSE.
Alphabetical Verses—the Letter C.
O Love builds on the azure sea,
And Love.builds on the golden sand ;
And Love builds on the rose-wing'd cloud,
And sometimes Love builds on the land.
O if Love builds on sparkling sea,
And if Love builds on golden strand,
And if Love builds on rosy cloud—
To Love, these are the solid land.
O Love will build his lily walls,
And Love his pearly roof will rear,
On cloud or land, or mist or sea—
Love’s solid land is everywhere.
e. 1860-S5. Isabella Valancey Crawford.
Audrey knoweth naught of books,
Naught to captivate the wise ;
But the soul of goodness looks
Through the quiet of her eyes.
‘ She can bake and she can knit,
Handily she wields the broom,
All her pleasure is to sit
In a neatly ordered room. , .
’ *
Touchstone, shaping a career,
Shines at each exclusive house :
“ Such a clever man, my dear,
Tied to just ‘ a country mouse.*
“ Married ere he dreamed of us,
, Ere he knew what gifts he had
St-range that Fate should yoke him thus,
% And very, very, very sad ! ”
Touchstone—(let them mark it well)
When the social round is trod, /
Bored by dame and demoiselle—
Goes home softly, praising God,
T. W. H. Crosland.
Listen to tho water mill '
All the livelong day—
How t he clicking of the Wheel
Wears the hours away !
Languidly the autumn wind
Stirs tfte greenwoed leaves,
From the field the reapers sing,
Binding up the sheaves} j
And a memory o’er my mind
As a spell is. east—
. The mill will never grind
With the water that is past.
Take the lesson to yourself,
Loving heart and true,
Golden years are fleeting by,
Youth is passibg"too.
Strive to make the most of life,
Lose no happy day,
Time will never bring you back
Chances swept away.
Leave no tender word unsaid,
Love while love shall last—-
The mill will never grind
With ; tfte water that is past.
Work while yet the daylight shines,
Man of thought and will ;
Never does the streamlet glide
Useless by the mill;
Wait not till to-morrow’s sun
Beams upon your Way,
All that you can call your own
Lies in this To-day.
Power, intellect and health
May not always last—
The mill cannot grind
With the water that is past.

Our “Future.”
STRAY spars still drift to show where the
“Austenle” storm lias passed. Here is
“ J.C.R.” on the topic of This Great (in
the future) Country :
The pessimistic views of “ Onlys,” and others of his
kidney, result from the misanthropic error which dis-
torted the. mental vision of Mr. Godlin. “ When you
played the ghost in the reg’lar drama in the fairs, you
believed in everything—except ghosts. Biit now you ’re
a universal mistruster.” Thus Short to Codlin. “ Aus-
terileJ Ausfertile?” Mere phrases !—conveying, it is
true, some shadow of an idea ; but, after all, only pedan-
tic peddling in the philologin department of visionary
literature. This Island Continent is denounced by one
writer as “ a withered hag, with the ribs of the world
sticking through her skinny sides.” Boor literary Codlin '.
Practically speaking, Australia is fertile in parts, and
the other portions could be utilised for various productive
purposes by exercise of due intelligence. The physical
features of the country are very good. But, at present,
the aspect of much of the country is very bad. Ravished
of much treasure, impoverished by a constant drain upon
its resources, plundered by a long-continued
system of political thriftlessness —or something
worse the infant Commonwealth issues from
the womb of Time suffering from marasmus. The
nation itself the people of Australia has as yet
developed no sane national instincts. It has enjoyed I?)
representative government by suicidally returning to
place and power eorrupt and incapable tyrants like——-no
matter who. It enjoyed peace and howled for war—and
has now tasted blood at the cost of many kicks and much
waste of treasure. The land has been alienated and the
price wasted, and the gold won rests in Bull’s pollers.
The wool shorn is also gone; while our people wear
shoddy clothes, and sleep under blankets that are half
cotton. But though we have received so little in return
for exported wealth, the country is ruined by,overstock:
ing; whole areas of pastoral land are trodden flat by
myriad hoofs; and on every reserve burr, thistle, cape-
weed, and other imported noxious growths' luxuriate.
Rabbits and hares are on the increase ; so ar6 hum
gry, desperate swagmen with boxes of matches to light
fires in summerand still the weed?; increase. Seldom
now are men- employed to wield a hoe, so Ausfertile is
turned into Austerile, instead of the reverse resulting
from intelligent control—which may prevail some day
when, for example, the present N.S. W. State Government,
faces the dawn with its coat-tails; and-“Onlys” and'
others of his literary standing use their faculties to other
purpose than disguising homely truths (or falsehoods) in
ornate phraseology.
The future of Australia depends upon intelligent home
rule. At every risk, even at the risk of menaced
coercion, Australians should endeavour to resist that
dominance of the Imperialistic toady element which has
proved so very disastrous under provincial rule. Not a
river locked,; .not a stream of any magnitude diverted ;
neither irrigation canal, nor other canal; and drought
after drought finds us in the same helpless case, after
lakes of water have flowed down our mountain slopes
tosoak away into deep subterranean channels thousands
of feet beneath the great infrferior plains.
Ausfertile ?. Yes, nq doubt about that I If only the
brains of the coming Federal Legislature prove fertile
with common sense !
“Onlys” shouldn’t blame the county for its impover-
ished condition, its sterility, and the consequent ignor-
ance and misery of some of its inhabitants. He > should
place the saddle on the,right horse—blame those incom-
petent administrators responsible- for the enormous losses
the country has sustained mainly through corrupt
Parliaments.
And “ A.B.T.”—
If Central Australia ever becomes a great mining coun-
try—as in all, probability it will—and carries a large popu-
lation, there is hope for the development of all that is
best, intellectually and physically, in the people who may
settle there. I was awaiting a "train at the Port Augusta
(S.A.) station the other morning when I heard the regular
tread of bare feet approaching. I looked-up and saw six
as fine men, physically speaking, as anyone might wish to
behold —tall, broad, and deep-chested, straight as arrows ;
limbs symmetrical, strong, lithe ; heads haughtily posed,
with a physiognomy far superior to that of the depraved
East Coast blacks ; features pleasing and intelligent.
They were “ niggera ’’who had just completed a term, of
punishment for interfering with cattle up Alice Springs
way. The trooper was physically the worst, man in the
procession and he was.no sloven. Central Australia is a
splendij breeding-ground, 'whether for stock or man.
Geologically, this part of the continent is distinct from
the eastern side. Climatically, it is hot, dry, healthy,
rarely enervating. It does not breed melancholiacs, and
pessimists who write verse and pfose damning Australia
because their "livers happen to be out of order. In the
near--future it may be pro veil’to the- satisfaction of.
“Onlys”, that Australia has astout heart bound in metals
and set in jewels, ready to throbmightily to the tread of
a vigorous, lusty, progressive race of white Australians.
Other remarks from P.B. ;. Zadig: M.G. ;
H.M. ; 0.8.
From a notice of-Hauptmanns newly-printed
four-act play,. “ Michael Kramer ” ’
Michael Kramer is a painter whose strong, pure nature
has overcome his bodily defects and enabled him to de-
monstrate the beaufy of his ,soul in his art. His soil
Arnold possesses the spark of genius lacking to .the
father ; Arnold can create what the other only dreams of,
but he fails because he has inherited the bodily defects
without the strength of mind- and will to conquer them.
This Kramer well knows, and the chief interest of the
drama lies in his struggle against his son’s weakness and
depravity, a struggle only ended by Arnold’s suicide.
Death becomes the great mediator and reconciler who
teaches man to understand life, and the fourth act is an
elegy, as fine as any we know in the world’s literature,
over the dead man, over his ineffectual life.
Hauptmann here shows himself in a new light; he is
not only the keen observer of human nature, the skilful
prober of the human soul, but the proclaimer of the
higher truths concerning man’s destiny. As a drama the
composition is full of technical faults, which caused it to
be doubtfully received by the Berlin public as a stage
play ; but as lofty imaginative literature, as the heart-cry
of the idealist in all ages, in all lands, it stands far above
anything Hauptmann has yet produced.
Pity that the idealist, of necessity, nearly always
belongs to the ranks of the sick-at-heart, who say,
“ Come, let us die quickly.” The bounding pulse
which never says die belongs to the action-man,
not to the dreamer. If only Viking hearts had
the brains to write the Praise of Life as they feel
it, how gladly would we spare the most admirable
elegies !

A field for speculation seemad to be opened by
news of the “ Loan Association ” formed by Mme.
Jeanne Robin, in Paris. The business consists in
issuing loans of from 5 to 20 francs to indigent
writers. No security is required, no interest
ciTarged, and no time specified for repayment.
The borrower just promises to pay “when con-
venient,” and the losses were reported as “almost
nil.” An appeal to Bulletin commentators, how-
ever, disclosed a lamentable dearth of ideas and
epigrams. “Edson” objected that “the idea
might kill the gentle art of borrowing, an art
based on the urgent thirst of the artist. No writer
who could borrow fifteen shillings from an Asso-
ciation, established solely to afford its promoter
the delight of lending writers fifteen shillings,
would ever be impelled to bring into operation
those subtle and delicate diplomacies necessary in
successfully playing the emotions of sordid persons
who positively ioathe lending fifteen shillings to
anybody. And Robin is not necessary to the true
artist. I have known one. so accomplished that he
could borfiw money from a usurer.” The remarks
of other contributors were unsuggestive of Crisis.
Only “ J.S.” touched the topic with a gold pen in
the lines appended—
American Book World recently offered a prize
of ten dollars for the best sonnet on America-
moved by some echo, maybe. And the prize went
to a “ sonnet ” beginning—
* America ! Not wielder of a power to wrong,
But ruler of a strength to right:
Thy force almighty, calm, uplifting, yet unseen ;
Thy ways are sentinelled by .Peace;
Thy progress heralded by Truth.
Think of—
Last sea-thing dredged bp sailor Time from Space!
—and exult ! ,

Remarks by Frederick Wedmore, who has
attained some critical celebrity in England:
If there is 'one fad in particular that it behoves the
painter,' and-the public to steer clear of at this time it is
the notion that any dramatic interest in a picture is a
diversion from a picture’s legitimate aims ; and that the
first and last business Of the painter is to be decorative;
that it is his function to be concerned only with what
they call “paint.” Paint, however, the thoughtful
person who is outside the studio will at once recollect, is
a medium of expression—does not suffice in itself; or,
rather, when it does suffice, the artist has grappled,
beautifully it may be, successfully it may be,
with only one of the problems with which it
was. his right, if he chose to exercise it, to concern
himself. The truth about the introduction of dramatic
incident in painting seems to me to be that it may add
immensely not only to the width but to the depth of the
appeal which the picture may make. At the same time,
the too great reliance upon dramatic or literary' interest
does obviously open the door for the neglect of qualities
that are a painter’s alone—beauty of line for its own
sake, beauty of colour for its own sake, richness or gaiety
of tone for its own sake. . . . . .
Just what we ’ve been telling you ! “It is n’t the
business of a picture to tell a story.” Not neces-
sarily or exclusively ; nor is it exclusively the
business of a picture to be paint well laid-on and
nothing more. Once again : technique is merely
the means >• th ot.end is the expression of artistic
emotion; and if you can get more emotion into
'your picture by putting in Balzac’s novels or
Shakespeare’s ..plays,- then put them in and
triumph:
PERCHANCE!
When having slain the Charlcote deer
He trudged to London town,
Perchance some- kindly yeoman near
Lent that poor tramp a crown.
The poet in the after years
Repaid that loan so free
With young-eyed Juliet’s passionate tean
For golden usury. , '
Then Portia to the world he willed:
Now, for her sweet appeal,
Poor craftsme-d of his own great guild
Are freed from Shylock’s steel.
J.S.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Does 1 lie English Democracy shrink
appalled before the vision of King Edward
through the plate-glass of the State or stage coach,
30ft. long, or does it regard the concern as a Lord
Mayor's show ?
“Eller”: The irony of Fate! Talking
some time ago about the money that was being
raised for the injured Imperial artillerymen, I
heard a man say that he would willingly lose the
sight of one eye for £lOOO. Now (he works in a
foundry) he hus lost the sight of one eye for
nothin<j !
For some time past Vic. Govt. has been
busily prosecuting Hindoo hawkers for perjury,
with very limited success. When, as often hap-
pens, the evidence of the colored gentleman’s
compatriots is depended on for a conviction, the
result is generally a batch of perjury-convictions.
The Hindoo lies from preference, and prosecution
for such a thing is a hopeless mystery to him.
Macaulay (“ Essay on Warren Hastings”) wrote :
What the horns are to the buffalo, what the paw is to
the tiger, what the sting is to the bee, what beauty (ac-
cording to the old Greek song) is to woman, deceit is to
the Bengalee. . . . elaborate tissues ot circumstantial
fiGehoods, chicanery, perjury, forgery are the weapons,
O f.*nsive and defensive, of the people of the Gower
uanges.
And he might have added “ of the Upper Ganges ”
—where the hawkers come from !
T.C.W. : The gay doings on last Q.
immigrant ship recall history. In ’74 (Palmer
time) a mob of male and female immigrants were
sent on from Kockhampton to Townsville and
billeted, the men in a large empty store and the
women promiscuously—very promiscuously. The
men, supposed to be navvies, were all costers and
Whitechapel bird-catchers ; the women were
lydies. They all got jobs quickly, beer-jobs and
queer-jobs, just the sort that would quickly
populate Bobby Towns’s old selection. It’s
like the case when the old woman in Scotland
was running down the Irish harvest hands. Per
crony said : “ But, Elspeth, ye ken they poj u-
late the Ivintra.” “’Deed, that’s true, as oor
Janet kens, three times.”
In Motley Mood!
Tlie deft and downy son of old Japan,
Tlie almond-eye from sandy Pee-chee-leo,
The earnel-drivers’ dour and swarthy clan,
The black gallant from Tanna fair and free,
The lordly Briton fresh from over-sea,
Tl:e Syrian suave, the lean and lank Hindoo
I call the land where these united be,
A motley country with a motley crew 1
lie swears by Allah, yonder rogue in tan ;
While gentle John a gentle Joss must fee ;
The heathen runs the race his fathers ran
And hugs a hideous idol secretly ;
But Europe’s son, a noble shrine hath he,
Wherein he weekly drops the threepence due ;
In varied style they bend a various knee,
The motley country with its motley crew 1
The lingo from Beersheba trips to Dan ;
The liquors range from tangle-foot to tea ;
The ladies vary—like their master, Man,
And piebald is the sorrow and the glee ;
A piebald shadow lies on hill and lea,
A queer concoction is the beer they brew,
In hybrid joy they blend alow-bred spree—
The motley country with its motley crew !
They oped the door and threw away the key,
And so a Speckled Grief they slowly grew,
The scorn of peoples pure in each deiiree —
This motley country ivith its motley crew !
Queensland.
P. LUFTIG.
The Wind on the Hunt.
“ Get up and come out! ” said the \\ iud on the
Hunt,
“I’ve been after the leaves all night;
There’s a rout in the sky as the clouds speed by,
And at sea is a royal fight.
“ It was all so calm that I could not rest,
So, out of my den and away !
llow T've chivvied and raced and hunted and
chased,
You'll learn by the light of day.
“ I met with a lugger just coming to port
And turned her about with a surge.
And she speeds back north for all she s worth,
With a sea at her heels to urge.
“ Get up and come out! it’s the fun of the world
Much bettor than lying abed—
To watch how I lift the roof-trees and shift
The trees by the hair o’ the head.
“ I am playing a game on a dark lee-shore
(Where no man hears her guns),
And with thrust and din I heave her in—
A ship of a thousand tons !
“And one fight more I must fight to-night,
’Tis a pine-tree great o’ girth ;
And with lion leap I shall break his sleep
And bend him to the earth.
“ His roots shall groan as I sway him back,
This ancient foe of mine ;
And the man-world start with a beating heart
At the crack of his riven spine.
“ Ships and trees! ” cried the Wind on the Hunt,
“ And screaming seas on the sand !
I’m a scourge to-night on the seaways white
And a pack o’ wolves on the land.”
N.S.W.

SATURDAY, APRIL 13, 1901.
The Theory and Practice of “Drink.”
All “ stimulants ” are bad. To “ stimulate ”
is to lift yourself above your plane of
vitality : it is flogging the sick horse, or the
dull horse, or the tired horse. And for the
temporary gain of energy, not earned by
your physical machine, you surely pay in a
temporary or permanent loss of energy.
The more you flog, the more you pay ; the
“stimulant” doses have to be continually
increased to secure the desired reaction ; and
presently comes a time when brain and body
fail to respond : you have exhausted the
power of the “stimulant,” and probably
wrecked your physical machine.
* * * *
All “stimulants” are good. For the
majority of human beings, this is a very dull
earth indeed—because they are dull earth-
animals, with a dull set of nerves, or placed
in a dull set of circumstances, or among very
dull people. Beer represents to them the
reaction from Bore. Without consciously
reasoning that a short, fast life is better than
a long, slow life, they have an instinct that
a vegetable existence is the very worst kind
of existence a man can lead. Yet their
inferiority of physical organisation, or of
circumstances, or of company, condemns
them for the most part to a vegetable exist-
ence. So they stimulate themselves with
alcohol, or opium, or hashish, or fifty
other things, in order to justify their claim
to Humanity. They understand that, in
their case, Philip drunk has a better time
than Philip sober. And with all their
brains they recognise that the sole object of
living on this earth is to have a good time—
soberly, if possible, but drunkenly if neces-
sary. They are quite right.
* * * *
The only human way of having a good
time is to get emotions, impressions, sensa-
tions—the most and most varied and most
intense sensations that your brain can give.
Every human being tries instinctively to
live the most intensely conscious kind of life
that he is capable of living, and to remain
conscious for the longest possible time. If
Minerva sprang full-armed from the cradle,
a wise man would deliberately set himself to
improve his brain and its attached body to the
utmost limit of the cosmic and hereditary
tether. He would get his sensations as he
extended his capacity for sensations, but he
would always look forward to the time when
his brain would be as keen and full as he
could make it by normal vital pro-
cesses. Then, when his brain was
full, he would start to absorb fully the
world of sensations. Joy, grief, pleasure,
pain, natural beauty, artistic beauty, the
delights of knowledge, the delights of
power, love, fatherhood, war, peace, the
light of dawn and the light in woman’s eyes,
books and friends, music and mystery ; —he
would welcome them all to the limit of his
power to receive them all, when considered
u.her with his mortality, his chance
ot continuing to receive them all in the most
intense measure. Deliberately he would
milk the world of possible sensations into
the bucket of his brain. And deliberately,
if he understood that there was an intensity
of sensation which transcended the normal
power of his brain, he would take “stimu-
lants,” counting the cost, and realising that
he was giving perhaps a day of normal
life for a moment of transcendent life.
Deliberately, a wise man would know excess
and fatigue, intoxication and abstinence—
for the pleasure of knowledge, and for the
pleasure of excess and intoxication. And
his motto would be, not “Nevertoo much,”
but “ Rarely too much”—“ Too much ” ac-
cepted with the knowledge of his power to
refuse if he so willed, - “ Too much ” wel-
comed because, on a calculation of chances,
“ Too much ” paid.
• * * •
Every wise man gets drunk at least once
in his life ; and it would probably do most
men good to get drunk four times a year or
so, if they were sober meanwhile and drunken
deliberately on quarter-day. Several Sydney
doctors recently united to publish a manifesto
on the side of total-abstinence. It was a
very mild manifesto ; and they contented
themselves with declaring, among other
things, that total-abstinence from “ liquor ”
is compatible with perfect health. In many
cases, it may be. But, as has been philoso-
phically pointed out above, perfect health is
not the desideratum of existence. We only
desire perfect health as a means to perfect
life, and perfect life depends upon the quan-
tity and quality of possible brain-sensations—
some of which cannot be enjoyed by a total-
abstainer. And even doctors’ intelligence is
only a relative thing. The number of little
things which the average doctor does not
know about modern science is astounding.
For example, in Sydney the other day, it
was reported that chloroform was adminis-
tered to a woman at eight o’clock in
the morning, and that the woman
died under the chloroform. Very likely
the operators were skilled and careful
as far as their knowledge extended; but
(unless the operation was one which
could not possibly be delayed) possibly
they did not know that chloroform
should never be administered at eight
o’clock in the morning when that can be
avoided. For the simple reason that the
daily rhythm of life in nearly every indi-
vidual is at its lowest towards daybreak,
and gradually ascends till early afternoon—
falling again towards sunset to rise towards
midnight. The time to medically strain a
weak heart or a weak organism is obviously
the time when the vital force is at its highest
point for the day ; and, when possible, the
seasonal rhythm should be considered as
well. Similarly, in regard to “drink,” many
doctors disregard most men’s periodical
need of physiological explosion. In many
cases food, however varied, does not
seem to satisfy this periodical need
of leaving the alimentary rut. Then
the wise man deliberately gives his system
the tonic outburst which it needs. If he
can secure his tonic without alcohol, he is
possibly all the better. If he secures it with
alcohol, und*er those circumstances and with
those reservations, he is probably none the
worse. This, of course, is doctrine for
sensible men, with the Power to Refrain •
not for fools or weaklings.
* * * •
Unfortunately, the average man, when
there is question of “ drink,” is usually a
fool or a weakling, or both. He can be
mistrusted to take more alcohol than is good
for him without philosophical or physio-
logical buttresses of argument. And, as
excessive drinking causes loss of the power
available for national industry, and is a foe
to national prosperity, the more influence
brought to bear on the side of temperance
the better.
* * * *
Australia is not at present a very drunken
countiy ; but it undoubtedly drinks a great
deal more than is good for it. The com-
parative national consumption of liquor is
thus tabulated from the most recent figures
in Rowntree and Sherwell’s book of
“The Temperance Problem all liquors
being reduced to their equivalent in absolute
alcohol.

The figures are probably sufficiently correct
for inference. And the tables show that
people in the comparatively-settled and
civilised parts of Australasia consume less
than half the proportion of liquor that is
drunk in Great Britain. Australia drinks
most “ where the hardest work’s to do, out
where the fires of hell burn through ”
—in Westralia and the Northern Territory,
where “ drink ” is often a man’s only solace
—where there are no theatres, or concerts,
or picture-galleries, or Sundays down the
harbor—no family life and no opportunities
for harmless amusement ; where daily labor
is performed under the most exhausting
conditions with the fewest opportunities for
recreation. And the deduction is the fami-
liar deduction that drink is probably no
more the cause of misery than misery is the
cause of drink. When life becomes as easy
and pleasant all over Australia as it is in
Sydney or Melbourne, this country will be
one of the most sober countries on earth.
* * » *
Even under present conditions there is
room and need for Australia to be much
more sober than it is. The question of
sobriety is a national question, because
over-consumption of alcohol—in addition to
causing crime and other forms of social
disease—directly reduces national productive
energy. But the power to regulate the
liquor-traffic appears under the Common-
wealth Constitution to be reserved to the
States for the most part; though the Federal
power of imposing duties could be used so as
to materially alter the existing state of things.
There are very many reasons why liquor-laws
should be uniform all over Australia—the
chief being that diverse State enactments
would defeat to a great extent anv measure
of reform ; and it may be found desirable to
place the Drink Question, as a national ques-
tion, entirely in the hands of the Common-
wealth Parliament. Since people will drink,
the thing to do is to restrict drinking
within the narrowest limits reasonable and
possible, and to give them good liquor in-
stead of the adulterated poisons supplied at
present to most consumers. Experience
elsewhere has proved that this result can
only be reached by making the sale (and
possibly the manufacture) of drink a National
Monopoly. That is a goal which should be
set well in the foreground of the Australian
democratic vista.
Revenue and Taxation.
The duty of cooking the Commonwealth tariff is
the most delicate duty which has ever fallen to an
Australian Cabinet. When the interests of only
one State have to be considered and placated in
the light of la haute politique of Keeping in Office
the job is difficult enough ; but now that we have
to compromise and harmonise the interests of six
States may the Lord have us in His holy
keeping! J
Let us suppose that The Bulletin readers have
come to the assistance of George Turner : and
that we are all cooking the tariff together.
If we are Freetraders, the thing is very simple
—we are simply Freetraders. That is, we collect
no Customs duties all. But, in revenge, we get
no Customs revenue.
* * * *
If we are Protectionists the thing is just as
simple. We exclude all foreign products that
Australia can produce, in so far as Australian
production suffices for the need of the Australian
community. But if Australian production en-
tirely satisfies Australian need, then we get no
more revenue from Protection than from Free-
trade—since excluded products obviously cannot
pay tariff. And if Australian production only
partly satisfies Australian need, then again we
get no Customs revenue, since we admit only the
proportion of foreign goods which we cannot pro-
duce ; and Protection does not tax goods which
we cannot produce.
So absolute Protection, like absolute Freetrade
means—no Customs revenue.
* * * *
If we are bastard -I reetraders, like George
Reid, we impose an arbitrary Customs tariff upon
articles of common consumption—without refer-
®nee local industries unless (as in the case of
N.S.W. sugar-duties) the industry is big enough
to be formidable to people who want to Keep in
Office. Trade is clearly not free ; but we get
revenue.
* * * »
If we are bastard-Protectionists, like George
Turner, we make a tariff in part designed to en-
courage local industries by restricting the area of
competition—as in the case of the Vic. boot-duty
of 10s. and 18s. per doz. pairs and 15 per cent. ;
and in part designed to raise revenue—as in the
case of the Victorian tea-duty of 3d. per lb. Vic-
toria grows no tea ; and rational Protection de-
clares that the articles which a country cannot
produce are to be imported freely, so that the
community can obtain them at the lowest possible
cost.
* * * *
It is as common for the bastard-Protectionist to
assert that Customs-duties do not raise cost, as
it is for the bastard-Freetrader to assert that
tj KJ absence of Customs-duties lowers cost. It is
the habit of enthusiasts on both sides to waste
gallons of ink in the profitless strife of assertions.
1 hey are both, right, and both wrong. What is
true is that it is the tendency of Customs-duties to
raise the cost of the articles which pay the duties.
Every tax tends to raise cost— must tend, since the
other economic factors of cost remain more or less
constant. Ihe lately-increased rate of Sydney
city tends to raise the cost of rent —because land-
lords other expenses are undiminished; and, to
receive their former rate of interest on capital,
they naturally strive to make their tenants bear
the new burden. The tea-duty in five Australian
States tends to raise the price of tea; since the
growers’ charge and the freight-charge remain
unaffected; and the importing middlemen natur-
ally strive to pass the tax to the consumer. You
cannot raise a.tax without taxing somebody; and
the tendency is always for that somebody to be
the consumer, because trade-competition tends to
fix the other factors of cost. But local causes,
special circumstances, may and frequently do
operate so that a tax shall not in some cases raise
cost to the consumer. Nevertheless, the tendency
remains ; and in a large field tariff-duties unques-
tionably do raise cost to the consumer.
* * * *
Thus, bastard-Freetrade, and bastard-Protec-
tion, are equally indefensible, in so far as they
operate to raise revenue. Because the tendency is
always for the tariff to raise cost, for the revenue
to come out of the consumer’s pocket; and it is
Freetrade that it does not raise cost,
the boast of Protection that it only raises cost in
return for a greater national saving.
Freetrade, Protection ; bastard - Freetrade,
bastard-Protection; they are all economically
condemned if they are used as pleas to raise
revenue.
* * * *
Yet the revenue must be raised. Then let it be
raised by direct taxation, when the burden can be
fitted to the ba.ck. There can be no true Free-
trade without direct taxation ; as Henry George
saw when he espoused Freetrade to secure the
direct taxation of land. There can be no true
Protection without direct taxation; since the
essence of Protection is exclusion, in whole or in
part; and the first penny of duty raised under
I rotection shows that the tariff-wall is either not
high enough—since it does not exclude; or that
it should be taken down altogether to admit
goods which the country is not producing, and
against which, therefore, local industry need not
be protected ; or that it should be lowered just
so far-as to admit the surplus of needed goods
which the country is not producing at the time.
* * * *
There is no reason why the whole revenue of
the Commonwealth should not be raised by a
direct taxation of the wealthy—with the poor
exempted and the wealthy taxed in pro-
portion to their wealth. No reason—except
the resistance of the wealthy classes; the
want of political brains and determination to over-
come that resistance ; and the want of intelligence
among “working-class” voters, who do not realise
that under a system of bastard-Freetrade or
bastard-Protection the most profitable duties are
usually levied on the articles of largest-consump-
tion, so that a rich man pays in no proportion to
his riches, and a poor man pays out of all propor-
tion to his poverty. m
The Cigarette Kid.
The Wesleyan Conference has denounced cigar-
ette smoking by juveniles. Even a Wesleyan
Conference may hit upon a truth'now and then
when dealing with the pleasanter vices. The
cigarette is a growing national evil. The Aus-
tralian youth is getting to consider himself in-
complete without a tissue-roll of alleged tobacco
m his face, and every doctor who has had much
experience of life-insurance work is aware of the
increasing number of “tobacco-hearts” which
figure in the lists of the rejected. In the immense
number of cases this condition is a cigarette pro-
duct, and the up-to-date youngster who smokes
his daily three-penn’orth is laying up for himself a
hideous store of physical weaknesses. Cigarettes
induce chronic tobacco-poisoning far more quickly
than the blackest cutty that ever adorned a hod-
man’s countenance.
* * * ,
The action of the cigarette differs materially
from that of the pipe or cigar. The finely-divided
condition of its tobacco allows tne butt to rapidly
become soaked with saliva which dissolves out
certain constituents. These products are either
swallowed direct or absorbed by the mucous mem-
brane of tho mouth, the “ wet smoker 55 thus get-
ting the effect of a chew in addition. In boys the
salivary glands are particularly active, and a corres-
pondingly large amount of absorption is a natural
corollary. The digestion is thus rapidly affected,
and the physical and chemical actions necessary for
the absorption of food into the system are inter-
fered with. _ Unless this digestive and absorptive
mechanism is capable of doing its duty, food in
the stomach is of no more use to the bodily re-
quirements of nutrition and growth than is food
on the top of the head. During the growing
period of life a large amount of extra work is cast
upon the delicate machinery of glands and muscles
by which digestion and absorption are effected, and
unless this works perfectly the excess of material
required for building new tissues will not be ob-
tained. A boy’s digestion differs from that of a
man in that it is required not only to supply the
actual working needs, but to provide the large
surplus necessary for increase of bone and muscle.
Ihe digestive absorption of tobacco by a growing
lad not only spoils his appetite, thereby prevent-
ing him from taking in proper supplies, but it
interferes with the processes by which these sup-
plies are converted into a usable form.
* * * *
Inhalation is the twin curse of the cigarette
smoker, and from it arise the more definite effects
upon heart and circulation. Few pipe or cigar
smokers draw the smoke into their lungs, but
no self-respecting young cigarette - puffer ’ con-
siders himself up to date unless he can inhale—
thereby bringing the more volatile products of
the smoke into actual contact with the delicate
cells wherein is effected the exchange of refuse
organic matters and carbonic acid for oxygen. The
“residual air” of the lungs becomes charged with
injurious substances and by their absorption the
nutrition-carrying power of the blood is lessened
and the nerve-regulation of the heart disturbed.
The resulting irregular heart action is apt to
throw an extra strain on the heart-valves, so that
there may result an actual and irremediable
organic derangement of the circulatory mechan-
ism. In any case the interference with the excre-
tory functions of the lungs reacts further upon
the starved blood. The musculature grows
slack and puny, the brain is deprived of its pro-
per supply of self-forming material, and the bov
becomes unpleasantly aware of the possession of
a disordered nervous system. By the time he
arrives at adolescence he will probably have de-
veloped into a trembling bundle of “nerves”
with no hopes of life beyond the crocodile sym-


pathies of some “Young Man’s Friend.” This
is no overdrawn picture. The entire list of “symp-
toms ” enumerated in the typical quack advertise-
ment does not range outside the ordinary effects
of a liberal course of cigarettes in the average boy.
The cigarette is the most powerful ally which the
quack possesses, and perhaps 50 per cent, of the
“lost-manhood” business depends on chronic
tobacco-and-paper poisoning.
• * * *
From a physiological standpoint, therefore, the
cigarette must be considered as an enemy to the
mental and physical development of Young Aus-
tralia. Although, in adults, its deleterious effects
are less pronounced than is the case with boys,
the more serious effects with which tobacco in
general is often credited, can almost always be
traced to the cigarette. “Tobacco heart, “tobacco
blindness,” and other conditions affecting impor-
tant organs, are seldom found in the cigar smoker,
and very rarely indeed in the man who sticks to
the pipe. Absorption and inhalation render the
cigarette the danger it is, and when acting at the
very period in life where such interferences are
least tolerated its capabilities for harm are worthy
of legislative consideration. If the growing boy
must smoke, he had better smoke a pipe, but he
had far better not smoke at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Cure of Consumption.
Writes a doctor to The Bulletin :
Again does the omniscient journalist assure us that
phthisis is no longer to be feared. Prof. Armand Gautier,
of Paris, is to do the trick this time with his new cacody-
late-of-soda treatment, and the usual vague “ thousands
of cases ” are quoted enthusiastically. Lazarus has been
raised from the dead by arsenious acid !
The mischief about these advertised cures lies in the
fact that they lead away consumptives from plans of
treatment which are actually doing them good. The
spes phthisica is remarkably active in most “ lungy ”
people, and medical men well know the feverish readiness
with which they grasp at any hope of relief from symp-
toms, regardless of consequences. There is not the slightest
doubt that the publicity given to the Gautier “cure”
has already caused scores of consumptives to rush post-
haste to Paris in the depth of winter from every sana-
torium in Europe.
The element of possibility gives the keynote to all the
much-boomed r “ cures.” The tubercle bacillus is emi-
nently tractable when it can be got at with germicides,
and arsenic is a powerful germicide. The only drawback
to its use lies in the fact that arsenic, is a powerful poison,
and that the dose of arsenic necessary to kill the tubercle
bacillus would also put an end to its host. Prbf.
Gautier alleges that he has got over the difficulty
by using arsenic in a form which will not in-
juriously affect the human organism while yet retain-
ing power to kill the bacilli —although cacodylate of
sodium is an active and powerful poison in the doses said
to have been used by Prof. Gautier. In the next place, the
proportion of arsenic, even from such a dose, which would
reach the affected patch of lung would not be of sufficient
intensity to kill the bacteria infesting it with any degree
of certainty. The cacodyl salts, moreover, are unstable
and liable to various decompositions, rendering them
difficult to keep in active form for any length of time,
while their markedly poisonous powers call for the ex-
tremest care in their handling and administration. The
most powerful and rapid of all poisons, a gas of which a
single bubble would kill a roomful of people, belongs to
this group and is known as cacodyl cyanide. Already the
medical journals are publishing letters from various more
or less well-known authorities, warning the profession to
be careful with sodium cacodylate, some relating actual
cases of poisoning from its use.
It will probably die out as a hundred other “ cures ”
have died out. Our best weapon to date against con-
sumption is fresh air of equable temperature, coupled
with a healthily-planned life and plenty of good food.
For the present, antiseptics have not got much beyond
the disinfectant stage, keeping existing lung-cavities
clean and preventing absorption of inflammatory pro-
ducts. Consumption is an eminently curable disease, and
there are few of us who reach the age of 40 years who
have not been more than once attacked by and cured of
it. And the testimony lies in certain little scars or nodules
in the apex of one or other or both lungs, which show
where the leucocytes have killed or walled up the
invading bacteria. Since the majority of civilised
men recover from consumption in this way, their
bodies must possess natural resistant qualities
capable of doing a great deal towards recovery even
when the mischief has progressed much further.
One great difficulty in the treatment of phthisis
lies in the fact that the disease is generally well advanced
before treatment is begun, and the bacteria have had
time to thoroughly poison the system with their products
in addition to the physical damage inflicted on the lung.
And herein is the main example of the uselessness of
germicides.
But the public loves new “ cures ” and runs after their
inventors until a new toy is offered. Here in Australia
we have a special interest in consumption, and yet the
Nationalisation of Tuberculosis never gets beyond the
stage of Talk. We have'splendid sites for sanatoria on
the Nordrach plan, we have an equable climate within easy
reach of our cities, and we have an increasing tubercular
death-rate. Yet with all these reasons calling upon us
we neglect to spend sufficient of the public money to
build a score of such sanatoria. The increasingly artificial
conditions under which civilised man lives are lowering
his powers of resistance, and the raison d’etre of the Nor-
drach method of treatment depends upon the reversion to
a more primitive mode of life. This eternal paltering
with “cures” and “antitoxins” merely delays the con-
sideration of an ever-increasing problem. We have one
moderately efficient means of combating consumption,
and it behoves us to employ it until such time as a
better one comes along. And the “ Consumptive Vote ”
in Australia, if properly organised, would be sufficient to
force a doddering Legislature into a measure which would
advance Australian welfare by the immediate erection of
the necessary sanatoria for the efficient treatment of the
disease.

The State versus Bung.
Editor Bulletin. —What’s the matter with the
brand of Protection that consists of a national
monopoly of any profitable enterprise instead of
the nominal taxation of the private monopolist
through the custom-house? Said private mono-
polist always passes most of the tax on to the
consumer, generally robs him of a bit extra in the
process, and in the case of foods and drinks
(especially drinks) also poisons him more or less
into the bargain. Take the grog trade. There’s
doubtless a big revenue from duties on intoxi-
cants ; but there’s also tremendous leakage, much
watered or drugged liquor, heavy cost of collec-
tion, and futile inspection, and ; lot more ex’s in
inquesting the corpse of the poisoned drunk and
burying his spirituous remains, and so forth.
If we made the importation and manufacture
of spirits, wines, and beer a Federal monopoly
and municipalised their distribution under State
laws, we could have pure liquors for those who
required them and a revenue as large as, or
larger than, at present obtained through the
customs, with less cost of collection and adminis-
tration, besides many obvious benefits the
greatest of all being the destruction of Bung’s
power in politics—at present exercised in an im-
pudently selfish and utterly unscrupulous manner.
No considerations of right or justice ever weigh
for a moment with the organised liquor-dealers.
Their whole political principles begin and end
with the trade they follow, and woe to the im-
pious politician who attempts to touch that!—
Arthur Bae.
—
Re “ Backstaii’s Baker.”
The Federal elections in South Australia have
been more than usually interesting to the public.
It will be remembered that some twelve months
ago Symon, Q.C., found it necessary, in the in-
terests of the Australian public, to whip Baron
Way of Backstairs Passage, Sir Richard Baker,
and one or two other men of that sort men who
are prominent in South Australia by reason of
their unlimited assurance and capacity for
intrigue. Sammy Way’s intrigues concerning
clause 74 are ancient history, but he at least has not
forgotten his whipping, whether sitting on the
Bench or otherwise engaged. Way’s whipping
was by means of the cat; Baker’s rather by
way of the birch—an implement of correction
chosen by Symon as being more suitable for a
less-developed intellect. "Everybody is now
chuckling at the idiotic assurance exhibited by
Barer at that time to hide his uneasiness. The
S.A. Federal elections are now practically over,
and the people, recognising Symon’s services, have
placed him at the head of the poll. He secured
over 2000 votes more than Baker, and it is m this
connection that the ancient history is both
instructive and amusing. On the 19th May, 1900,
the spirit of prophecy descended upon Baker,
and in a letter to the Register, written with the
classical accompaniment of much foaming at the
mouth, he delivered himself of the following pro-
phecy :
“ He (Symon) seems disturbed in his mind about my
election. He need not worry. If I stand for either
House of the Federal Parliament I will be elected ; Mr.
Symon will be rejected.”
Symon, Q.G., in the following issue of the
Register said: —
He (Bauer) is good enough to predict that if he stands
he will be elected to the Federal Parliament, and I will
be rejected. It does sometimes happen that the worth-
less are taken and the worthy left. But the people of
South Australia are discriminating'. They will not forget
that when their self-government and the force of the re-
ferendum was at stake the President of the Legislative
Council played the part of informer to Mr. Chamberlain.
When the Federal Parliament is chosen he (Baker) Will
find I shall happen to be there. I am not vindictive,
so I do not prophesy his rejection. His election will have
one advantage : it will keep before the people the man
who sought meanly to betray them, and, by insidious
backstairs influence, to facilitate an assault upon an
essential part of their national self-government.
It is easy in this case to distinguish the false
prophet from the true prophet— Baker is certainly
a prophet who has no honor in his own country.
The verdict of the people in Symon’s favor is pro-
nounced. It is a verdict recognising his ability,
his outspokenness, and his indifference to those
clothed in purple and line linen.
♦ •
The reason given by N.S.W. Executive for com-
muting the death-sentence passed on Joseph
Baker for the attempted murder of Detective
Toomey was, according to Sydney D.T., “ that
Baker did not kill Toomey.” If that be the
reason, it would, on general grounds, be an excel-
lent one, but with the statute-law of N.S.W. as it
is, it is the assumption by the Executive of a
power outside the law. The N.S.W. Executive
has hanged numbers of persons for attempted
murder. It is, of course, a welcome thing at the
time, when the Executive dispenses with the
savage services of Nosey Bob ; but if the action be
dictated merely by the will or whim of that
body there is danger that another Execu-
tive, or the same body at a different time, will
act in a contrary way, also on will or whim.
Further, those who desire to legislatively abolish
capital punishment, oT to restrict it to murder in
the first degree, are hampered in their advocacy
by an Executive which is whimsically merciful.
It will be said, “ Your legislation is not necessary
as regards attempted murder, for the Executive,,
as in the Baker case, may commute the sentence.”
But the Executive may not. What reformers
want is not a commutation dependent on
the varying sentiments of the Executive, but
such an alteration of the law as will prevent the
death sentence in such cases being passed. Indeed,
it may be held that the shortest way to make the
Executive assist in the alteration of a savage law
is" to insist that while the law remains on the
statute-book the Executive shall observe it to the
letter. In the Baker case, the Executive having
a good reason for commuting the death-sentence
need not have given a had one. The good reason
was that Baker had been recommended to mercy
by the jury on the ground that he believed he
had been grievously wronged by Toomey.
—
Much simnler than the N.S.W. plan of crossing
out from me ballot-paper the names of the men
you don’t want to vote for is the system, first
adopted by S.A., of making a cross opposite the
names of those you do want to vote for. There
didn’t seem so much merit in this plan until a
ballot-paper appeared with 50 closely - written
names, 44 of wliich it was necessary to strike out;
but anybody who saw the bewildering procession
of blue-pencilled strokes was not likely to be sur-
prised to find that in some districts where pencils
aren’t often handled the proportion of informal
votes ran up to 40 per cent. —that is, nearly half
of the electors who did go to the poll disfran-
chised themselves by counting 43 strokes as 44, or
something like that. Gn the other hand, the man
who counts five plain crosses as six probably
deserves to have his vote rejected. S. Australia
had the N.S.W. system long ago—she tried it on
the occasion of the first election to the Leg. Coun-
cil, the whole province voting as one electorate ;
and the mess was so awful—just about as awful as
the N.S.W. mess was on Friday week —that she
promptly adopted the cross system, and hasn’t
seen any reason since to change it.

The fact that Britain is finding difficulty in main-
taining a supply of recruits to make good the war-
waste in S’Africa is an unfortunate circumstance
for the Boers. Britain, when in a tight place, has
never yet hesitated over such a trifle as employing
Black troops. She fought the American colonists
with German mercenaries and Indian levies.
Would the United Colonies have achieved their
independence had the British Govt. had 250,000,000
Hindoos from which to draw fighting material ?
The Yankee invasion of Canada in 1812 was re-
pelled by Canadian militia and Indian levies —and
didn’t the noble Indian play up with the
wounded! Savages, wearing at their belts thescalps
of white men of British descent, paraded beneath
the British flag and fought under a British
general. Over 20 years ago Disraeli had Hindoo
troops in the Mediterranean ready for a leap at
the throat of exhausted Russia had the advance
on Constantinople been persisted in. Indian
troops faced Russians at Kinder’s Siding in
Tientsin (China) the other day. If the position
in S’Africa becomes really acute through the
supply of British recruits failing, rather than face
a conscription, Britain will throw overboard the
promise to use only white troops, and finish a war
of extermination with a Hindoo Army Corps.
By which time the British reputation of being
“bad Europeans” will be thoroughly estab-
lished.

“D.G.” to The Bulletin re the wreck of tha
Rio de Janeiro
It is not quite correct to talk of the “cowardice ” of the
Asiatics. All men are pretty much on a level as regards
courage, or insensibility to danger. Serviceable courage
is the”ability to do the right thing in a dangerous emer-
gency, and that depends chiefly on men’s confidence in
themselves or in those to whom they look for guidance.
The real weakness of an Asiatic crew lies m the fact that,
whether the men are individually cowardly or brave,
they are nob perfectly under the control of the
European officers, who can only communicate with them
through a “ serang.” The system is fatal to discipline.
Take the case of the recent Karrakatta wreck on the VV .A.
coast. The Malay crew seized a boat and left passengers
and officers in the lurch. That was not cowardice, but
pure selfishness and insubordination which would hardly
have been possible had the men been under the direct
control of he white officers. No doubt shipowners will
fight tooth and nail against any attempt to change the
existing system. They think it is bad enough to have to
appoint only approved navigators and seamen to officer
their ships, and if officers were required also to know one
or two Asiatic languages the available supply of qualified
men would get short, and their market value would rise
to a figure that would make the employment of Asiatic
sailors a very questionable economy. You need not
prohibit the yellow sailor. Make him a costly luxury
and you get there just as surely!
The Bulletin believes that, generally speaking,
the white races exhibit more intelligent self-con-
trol in the face of sudden danger or of panic than
the colored races, often fatalistic in temperament.
Particularly speaking, it believes European
crews are more trustworthy than lascar crews.
And, personally speaking, when it has a colored
cat to kill, this paper doesn’t choke it with cream.

Yic. State Premier Peacock, in his desire to save
every possible shilling, presumably to spend on
the Jookoyork, is hard at work baulking old-age
pension applications on every possible excuse, and
has invited the assistance of members of ladies
benevolent societies to “prevent the looting of
the Treasury.” This enlistment of an army ot
honorary spies and gimleteers, who will no doubt
punish independence of manner and alleged want
of proper respect, and on the strength of the invit-
ation swagger and swish their tails, generally, has
moved Edward Findley, State member for Mel-
bourne city, to let fly a few remarks about the
persons to whose mercy the alleged democratic
Premier has handed over his ancient and indigent
electors. Said Findley :
“What are these Charitable and Ladies’ Benevolent
Societies ? . . . . The members of the Ladies’ Benevolent
Societies are the wives and women-folk of professional
ranters, notorious sweaters, lickspittle shire councillors,
sordid upstarts, professional ascetics, rail-sitting poli-
ticians, suave smoodgers, prurient purists, and all the
other rabble and off-scourings of that kind of society
which has been aptly named the World of Cant, lhe
more favourable specimens are usually mere strutting,
posturing and patronising Lady Bountifuls, whose
charitv takes the form of conferring as a favour
small' fractions of the money which their
husbands or fathers have stolen from the
people whom they are patronising. Numbers of them
are women whose sexual nature has withered into what
passes with the unthinking as virtue, and who gain a
sort of consolation in their passional decline from watch-
ing and retailing to cronies the love misfortunes and ad-
ventures of young girls who have, through the neglect of
society, to seek their help. Others of them are those
not uncommon distortions of their sex, who treat lllegiti-
mate children and children forced by circumstances abso-
lutely beyond their own control to become wards of the
Neglected Children’s Department, as if they were non-
human, delighting to brand them to their acquaintances,
and even to taunt the children themselves with what
these hags call their shame. . . . These types of women,
reared on the unwholesome food of compromise with
conscience and forever breathing the unchristian atmo-
sphere of church life, are especially unfitted for any duty
which involves the interests of the poor who refuse to be
hypocrites, who will not kow-tow to the owner of ill-
gotten wealth, and who instinctively rate at its true value
the cant and hollowness of heart of these women.
Melb. Table Talk hopes that the husbands and
sons of these “ ladies ” will remember Findley’s
remarks at the next election. The Bulletin
trusts that everybody will remember them, and
vote for a man who, though his denunciation may
be a bit sweeping, sticks to facts in the main. It
is a long timessince this paper saw the typical
pushing semi-society Lady Bountiful sized up so
accurately.

In addressing a meeting of the British Women’s
Emigration Association, Joe Chamberlain is
reported as “anticipating that hundreds of
thousands of men would proceed to S’Africa after
the war,” and that “ there would be a pro-
portionate emigration of women.” But, in S’Africa
there is a plethora of black-labor, and with
America and Australia competing with it for
population, there is not the slightest chance that
it will be rushed by the workers of Europe. If
Chamberlain doesn’t know that, he is a bigger
fool—and if he does know it, he is a bigger liar
than his worst enemies thought him. As for
what will happen “after the war is over”—it
isn’t half over yet, and, judging by present
appearances, it be won’t for a year or two. Mean-
while Chamberlain and co. have sucked the Aus-
tralian and Canadian oranges pretty dry, and if
the present drain of men goes on much longer
there will be none to emigrate. What then ?

“ Sailor-Man ” to The Bulletin
Your remarks (29/3/’01) on the ill-paid shipping busi-
ness call for fierce approval from all classes of “ shell-
backs.” With an average of 50 per cent, (as per official
Board of Trade returns) of foreigners employed in the
British mercantile marine, John Bull, shipowner, hasn t
much to boast about. Young England declines more and
more every year to take to the sea as a means of livings
and small wonder. The result of ridiculously low rates of
pay, combined with increasing severity of examinations,
will be that in 20 years, owners will have to depend almost
entirely on the foreigner, not only for the manning but
also for the officering of their ships. What sea-going
men think of their profession is shown by
the eagerness with which they jump at the
chance of a semi-nautical shore billet, even at
comparatively poor pay. Long hours of overtime in
port (without remuneration) coupled with Sunday work,
and the conversion of private concerns into limited-
liability oo.’s, have completely destroyed any personal
interest owners formerly had in their employes. Nowa-
days, the latter are merely so much machinery for earning
dividends on account of shareholders, 90 per cent, of
whom don’t know the stem from the stern. To these
people, whose mouthpiece is the managing owner (often
only with enough interest to “ qualify ” him for a seat on
the board) the sailor-man is a mere “counter” whose
personal feelings and interests must not stand in the way
of quick despatch and big dividends. Also as the “ ”
sailor-man has no vote, and if he had one would seldom be
at home to use it, no one bothers about him ashore, except
the bobby on the beat (who never misses a chance of run-
ning him in on the d. and d. charge) and the gent who
cashes his advance note, and the seamen’s missions,
which proceed on the assumption that the “ shell ” must
be treated, like a Sunday-school kid, to buns and tea.
And, finally, after a life of hard graft and hard fare, the
only prospect left the seaman is the work-house. If sea-
going is to be made again a profession worth taking up,
the two leading reforms must be the barring of the
foreigner- first, last and always—and a return to decent
remuneration, instead of a wage (especially in wind-
jammers) at which a corporation-dustman would turn up
his nose. —H.B.T.

N.S. Wales, though blessed with a compulsory
Education Act, and though a country where the
percentage of persons who can read and_ write is
high, came near to achieving a world’s record
when over 31,000 informal votes (about 22£ per
cent, of the total) were cast at the late Senate
election. Granting the confusion arising from
the fact that six names, and six only, had to be
selected from the fifty, admitting the Cupidity of
the man who drew up the crowded Senate ballot-
paper (he is apparently used to making out price-
lists for a cash draper), and, giving in the_ novelty
of the occasion, the number of informalities was
grotesquely high. The result must be put down
to the crass foolishness of the sovereign people.
It is strange that, though the people will put a
man like A. J. Gould before such as K. -It.
O’Connor, simply because they are told to do so
by the papers they see daily, and though they
will give a prominent place to “John Norton
and “Dick” Meagher because they hear ot them
every day, they cannot be taught how to vote tor
their chosen candidates. Most persons agree that,
had all the informal votes been counted, NORTON
would have been at least fourth on the poll, -but
a constituency that casts 13,500 votes for a rank
and utterly unknown outsider for the only apparent
reason that he is the namesake of a well-adver-
tised politician is pretty hopeless. N.b.W. is not
proud of her Senate election.

Will Commonwealth P.M.G. Drake kindly
charge a competent and impartial officer to ma.ie
inquiry into the following alleged facts ; publish
the result of the inquiry ; and take suitable action
—firstly, because it is obviously the right thing to
do ; and, secondly, because it will pay the Barton
Govt, to show that it is determined to reform
W.A.’s administrative abuses :
Dear Bulletin. —During the year ended 31st March
last the Adelaide S.S. Co.—in which Brother Alice is
concerned —held a contract from W.A. Govt, for a man
service from Albany to South Coast ports, at the sum ot
£l5OO per annum. ' Throughout the period of its contract
the co. has inflicted on the suffering population of the
districts served some of the least effective steamboats
for mail traffic procurable in Australasia —the last of these
being the Herbert, which possesses power (?) sufficient to
drive her against a moderate breeze at the speed of over
two knots, while with a fair wind she can steam over five
knots per hour. , . ' .
In anticipation of the present contract ending on
31st March last, the South Coast people asked the
Govt, to try and obtain a better boat and service by
calling for 'tenders, to be open to all shipping cos
trading in the Commonwealth. This is how Big John S
crowd did it. The usual notice was printed in the Govt.
Gazette, but was cancelled and withdrawn before the due
date of closing. On asking the reason for this, the
Esperance Chamber of Commerce received a wire from
Siioll, the W.A. P.M.G., which runs like this: “Much
regret cannot assist you, as during my absence
in Melbourne and prior to this department enter-
ing the Commonwealth, the Government cancelled
Public Notice of Tenders from this department, and on
behalf of State Govt, entered into a new contract with
the Adelaide S.S. Co. for 12 months from 27th February,
and cancelled original agreement which stood in my name
and which did not expire till 31st March inst. .As I am
now a Federal officer I fear I am powerless to interfere.
I am strongly of opinion that the obsolete Herbert is
totally unfit for the service and should never have been
allowed to become a permanency on the coast, which I
fear she now is until completion of contract owing to the
rate of speed, etc., having been reduced to her power in
existing contract.”—Yours, Native.
The clear accusation of this is that the Forrest
Govt., in order to profit a private company with
which some of its members are connected, can-
celled a contract before expiry in order to create a
fresh contract for an extended term —thus deliber-
ately evading imminent Commonwealth control,
and putting it out of the Commonwealth Govt.’s
power to serve the public interest until another
year expires. As such accusations are common in
Westralia, is it any wonder that Westralia re-
turned so many opponents of the Forrest Govt,
to the Commonwealth Parliament ?

Melbourne furniture tradesmen having lately
demanded increased wages, the combined em-
ployers, urged by the Argus, rushed to Premier
Peacock and plaintively requested him to rebuke
these contumacious workmen for “attempting to
override the Wagesßoard.” The Premier promptly
told them the Board had fixed a minimum wage,
but had said nothing about a maximum, and he
couldn’t for the life of him see what they
were growling about. The position of these
employers is truly amusing. They wail
tearfully that “they are bound by the
Wages Board and the operatives are not
bound.” How sad ! And even their beauti-
ful law of supply and demand will not help
them ! The one thing that will help them, which
will fix a standard wage —less than which they
must not pay, and more than which the
operative may not demand—is an Arbitration
Act, and that is the very thing they are
always fighting against. The Bulletin pities
these employers—but it has precious little sym-
pathy for them.
4
One has to go far to find a more pitiable exhibi-
tion of petty meanness than that of the five
employers’ delegates on the Vic. Wagesßoard for
the woollen industry. These gentlemen withdrew
because a couple of the men’s delegates were not
constantly engaged in the mills. It is obvious
that in many trades—especially those in the
hands of a few men—the employers might
so harass and intimidate any independent
workman as to force the employes as a
body to choose their delegates from outside their
ranks. It is pleasing to note that Ministers
Peacock and Trenwith, after reasoning un-
availingly with the recalcitrant delegates,
promptly appointed Mr. Justice Williams as
chairman and told the Board to go ahead. And
after the minimum wage is fixed those employers
—of whom a Liberal M.L.A. is one—will pro-
bably be sorry they held aloof.

Melbourne Age apparently lost its head over the
partial triumph of its Senate candidates, and, a
day or two afterwards, got upon stilts and shrieked
hysterically for a “ frequent and vigorous applica-
tion of the lash ” to garrotters. Five cases of
assault and robbery had occurred the previous
evening, and this class of crime has been some-
what frequent in Melbourne during the past few
months. Seeing that "most, if not all, of these
offenders remain at large, it is not easy to see
whom the Age wants to castigate so energetically.
Melbourne l5OO miles of streets, of which 90
are in the city proper ; for these 90, there are 272
constables of all"-ranks, and for the other 1410
there are 416 police. The obvious remedy for
a spasmodic outbreldc of this sort is to in-
crease the force, at least temporarily, so as to
make reasonably certain of first catching the
criminals. As for the punishment when caught,
The Bulletin thought the evidence of those who
know most about the subject—Lord Aberdare,
Arthur Balfour, H. H. Asquith, and many
other English M’s. P., as published by this paper
on 26th January last—had proved the utter
inefficady of the lash to abolish or suppress crime
of any sort or degree. Why doesn’t the allegedly
democratic Age demand the lash for the sup-
pression of, say, the defaulting lawyer who melts
away so frequently ? The Age knows well enough
that it is demanding a remedy that has ever been
reserved for the man in moleskins, the unknown,
undefended criminal—it has never been ladled
out to the well-connected, cruel thief or scoundrel
in frock-coat and bell-topper. So much for the
Age’s democracy !

French. Thirteen thousand men ! Then this
eternal war must be only beginning.
