Athenaeum Hall Genuine British Biograph.
“ Florodora,” fresh from its triumphal
career of 106 nights in Melbourne town, burst
upon a crowded Sydney house on Saturday night,
and from the moment the curtain rose was a
booming success. It is a story of love and swindle
in a gorgeous setting of tropical island and Welsh
castle. The first scene is in the Island of Floro-
dora, where the manager’s cottage has striped
blinds, and the trees are covered with red blossoms,
and through the branches the equatorial sunlight
streams down and paves the floor with an
arabesque of light and shade. Beyond the trees,
past the drooping palms, trembles a glimpse of
sky and sea of clearest blue, a stretch of yellow
sands, and above, low hills, all red with the
blossoms from which the mysterious scent is made.
In the foreground the ordinary packing-case of
commerce with the label, “ Florodora, Cyrus Gil-
fain, Proprietor,” gives the necessary real-world
touch that makes the extravaganza a romance of
the present time.
* • * .
The plot is simple and coherent. Love
and Greed are gods of everything. A number of
nicely-dressed men are mad to marry an equal
number of picturesquely-dressed girls. Hugh
Ward Gilfain wishes to marry Carrie Moore
Dolores, to make good his title to the island he
stole from her father. Manager Brownlow Aber-
coed wants to marry the same girl for cardiac
reasons. Dolores has also got something very
bad, and there is no cure for it but Brownlow.
Lady Palotta Holroyd is after Gilfain and his
cash. Kenningham Donegal and Miss Chetwynd
Angelina Gilfam are hungry for one another, but
Gilfain, having grown rich and bought Abercoed’s
Castle Hall in Wales, wishes her to marry
Manager Brownlow Abercoed and become a
peeress. Later on, the whole establishment of
Florodora is discovered at the Castle of Abercoed,
in Wales. Prof. Lauri Tweedlepunch, who had
been bribed by Gilfain to persuade everybody
in the island of Florodora that his ideas of
how they all ought to marry are correct according
to phrenology, comes along with Dolores and gives
an entertainment at the Castle gates, and para-
lyses everybody with his burlesque of the Dartos.
Gilfain is about to marry Dolores, and make his
title to all his millions sure, when Palotta and
Lauri, as a Ghost and a Druid respectively, meet
Gilfain in a dark room and terrify him into a con-
fession, and hold him to his promise of making
restitution under pain of being interviewed by
The Bulletin.
» * • *
“Florodora” is not only an interesting
story of love and swindle, but a good illustration
of the advantages of being a millionaire. Cyrus
Gilfain travels round with six charming young
Englishwomen, keeps a choral ballet of six in
orange silk and heliotrope, and, later on, when he
takes them to Wales they go about in flesh-colored
stockings—when Palotta Holroyd runs his estab-
ment —and all without a breath of scandal. Even
when he goes in for step-dancing in the face of
the retainers his family physician does not call
in a brother practitioner and a magistrate. The
general effect is one of sparkle, and although
“Florodora” is not cha,mpagne, it is first-class
sparkling hock. There is a charming love-scene
between Kenningham Donegal and Miss Chetwynd
Gilfain, in which Kenningham sings a pretty
loye-song. Brownlow’s song to Dolores struck this
writer as being a trifle long and heavy, although
its receding effect as the boat carries him away is
fine. But the glory of “Florodora” is not in
its principals—barring Lauri, who, of course, is
indispensable. Palotta, although the acme of
grace and vivacity, has no voice to speak of ; and
Kenningham’s song about the English Guardsmen
is not an overwhelming success. All the prin-
cipals might be cut out, and as long as Lauri and
the choral ballet and the six English ladies danced
and sang in full dress with the six fashionably-
arrayed clerks, the thing would still be a raging
success.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, the performance of
Adelina Sarina is full of thrills. She glitters on
a flying trapeze up among the top branches of the
Tivoli bush, with the naked, cruel-looking boards
beneath waiting to smash her if a muscle gives
way or anything breaks. A man hates to see a
pretty woman in apparent imminent danger of
death, and that is how Adelina impresses one as
she slides through the trapeze head-downwards
and comes to a dead stop and hangs by the tip of
her two principal toes. Marie Lloyd struck The
Bulletin as a star of the first music-hall magni-
tude in whose light there are occasional flashes of
brilliant blue. Of the beauty and grace which
her photos, lead you to expect, this scribe per-
ceived only a small percentage, but she requires
absolutely none—she glows and overpowers with
her energy. Her description of how 40 costers
, and their donahs descended on Folkestone and
painted that seaside town 17 different shades of
carmine is a work of art. Her voice resembles that
of Lizzie Kirk and Irene Franklin, and everything
she does is clever and energetic. On Saturday
her turn was run on a sort of sliding-scale : each
particular item being more wildly applauded than
the preceding one, till it seemed likely that
Rickards would soon be calling for tenders for the
construction of anew Tivoli. Rosie Aquinaldo
gives the same picturesquely-dreadful perform-
ance with which she astonished this continent
when with Fitzgerald’s great show. It is not
every day one can see a really handsome woman
standing on a branch by her teeth sitting on her
own head—but Miss Aquinaldo does it every
night at the Tivoli. There are other attractive
items, including Mr. Hurley’s turn, to which
justice will be done later on.
** * *
The Empire still keeps up its reputation
for weekly changes. In the Bower of Roses,
which forms the first part, Miss Eva St. Clair
sings and gestures daintily over “ The Presi-
dential Chair,’' while a mild and amiable person
expresses vocal desires to be a bandit bold,
or something to that effect. Mexican Jack still
shoots a ball off the top of his own head and des-
cribes a crinoline round himself with the common
or back-yard rope. The child phenomenon has
landed at the Empire this week. Maisie Ed wards,
especially in her rendering of the “ London Waif,”
is unusually mature and expressive; unlike the
shrill pipe of most infantile prodigies, her voice
needs toning-down rather than bolstering-up.
During the evening somebody appeared on the
stage and absurdly thanked the people for coming
out to enjoy themselves.
** * *
The Dampiers have farewelled to the tune
“ Robbei 7 Under Arms” and crowded houses.
The eternal popularity of the bushranger drama
is a mystery. Why outside robbery should have
a glamour of romance thrown over it, when the
same crime committed indoors is merely prosaic,
is a psychological phenomenon, and a brave
attempt should be made by some enterprising
playwright to show the burglar in as pathetic and
heroic a light as the bushranger. Meanwhile, the
* l R ? bb ery,” with others of a like pattern, goes to
rejoice the hearts of the long, lean Cornstalks of the
interior.
*****
At the Royal, “ Cinderella ” continues to
ride in majesty in the coach beautiful. That
coach and the gorgeous ball to which it bore the
despised maiden with the dainty tootsywootsy
simply paralysed the Billjims and their “old
women ” from the Big Bush during Easter. It
acted as a sort of Religious Revival, and filled in
the dim outlines of the problematical heaven with
glowing tints. To ride in a coach like that, with
such heavenly clothes, were paradise enough, and
every bush mamma who witnessed it will go back
with a stern resolution to take care of Mary Ann’s
feet and keep her toenails nicely rounded so,
should the opportunity ever come along, that
daughter of the Outside Track will be able to rise
to the occasion. And if George Musgrove has
created a desire for physical beauty among those
who formerly had no regard for it, “ Cinderella”
will not have been altogether a failure.
** * *
A second inspection of “ Why Smith Left
Home ” reveals a serious defect: There is a
grievous insufficiency of Arnold, as the incan-
descent Count who bursts into the flame of love in
the street, and rushes Smith with a wild proposal
for the hand of Smith’s own wife. Fred Lester,
as ze brave Generaal Billetdoux,” is a character
this scribe once knew in real life. Under the
domination of his wife, he is a painful circum-
stance, but, away from her baleful influence, the
brave general is one of the boys—one of the old
guard, who make love to every woman they meet
and never surrender except to their own better-
halves. Writer once knew such a Frenchman in
Maonland, and Lester’s make-up with the thin-
pomted moustache, and his expressive gestures
seem to have been copied exactly from that gallant
son of Gaul.
* * * *
“ Smith ” is a gospel for all men-of-the-
world. Its morals are sterling, and it impresses
several great truths of masculine life on the young
cubs who are just beginning to feel their teeth.
Never illicitly hug a girl in the house you live in
it doesn’t pay. But if you do hug her, in the
name of suffering man never allow her to think it
was by accident or mistake. Always give her the
in ?P ress i° n sb e is being hunted—women
like being hunted, and the shock of finding
out that they are not is likely to drive
them to tragic revenge. Then the great Monroe
Doctrine of Domestic Happiness is hammered
mto the audience all through the piece.
Exclude all relatives from and after the day of the
wedding—once they get a footing bad cooking is
quite powerless to shift them. Nothing but a
meat-axe or laying a strange girl on to hug the
masculine relatives will clear them out. The lady
relative always gets restive if she thinks another
cat is poaching over her preserves. Smith did
not intend to do that, but it was done all the
same, otherwise he would never have got rid of
his aunt and been able to leave home with his wife
for the long-deferred honeymoon.

Wirth’s Circus makes a speciality of its
animal rather than of its human performers
Conspicuous among the latter is Fred. El Zobeide
who stepdances on his hands while his feet per-
form similar evolutions up aloft, as described last
week. In days to come, very probably, the
ballet-dancer will double herself in this manner
and introduce a contradictory novelty by whirling
drapery round with her toes while her hands are
performing graceful steps. The only two
boxing ponies in the world perform in rather a
spiffy manner, and the knock-out blow which
reduces Corbett to the sawdust is given by Fitz-
simmons clasping two hoofs firmly round his
neck Ihe performance as a whole goes rather
slowly at times, and there is a large amount of
vague comic element which seems to end nowhere.
But the wild beasts and their various items are
capital, and suit admirably the spot where the
tram-cars all attack one another at various points
and their creator walks fearfully and cautiously
among his own works. ' y

#* # *
“The World’s Verdict,” at Sydney
Lyceum, is the usual old melodrama wherein the
marriage-certificate is given by the fool heroine to
her husband’s unlawful mash ; whereupon the
wicked husband and his illegal “ tart” promptly
try to slay the foolish matron in a stone-quarry,
but are prevented, as usual, by the hero and the
funny man. Not learning by experience, the
wife accepts her husband’s invitation to meet him
in the cell of a raging lunatic, who amuses himself
by inventing new instruments of fiendish torture.
The maniac immediately tries to practise on
the silly little wife with all his patents at
once, and she is again rescued with great
difficulty. Some of her imbecility has been
communicated to her husband, as he wrote to
the maniac requesting him to finish off the unde-
sirable wife, and signed his full and proper name
to the screed, which the lunatic left around in a
most sensible manner. The hero who does so
much hard rescuing is the usual peculiar person
who displays great ingenuity in saving undesir-
able wives, but is quite unable to live when the
family estate goes up the spout, and settles down
and starves quietly in a country mansion, while
the faithful retainer hunts the rent-collector off
the premises with a gun. Augustus Glover makes
a good maniac, and Helen Fergus faints in a most
artistic style anywhere and everywhere.

Nance O’Neil, the large lady who was in-
troduced to Australia a year ago as a famous
American actress, and achieved some astounding
notices from Melbourne pressmen—Nance, “born
to the imperial purple of the stage,” and able to
melt the heart of a stone ” when she wasn’t
carrying her worshippers off their mental balance
with “the ocean-like sweep of her emotion”—
Nance, who was then said to be about to visit
London for the first time, although she had
faced the music in that city a year previously
Nance, the interesting object-lesson in managerial
tactics, and the producer of unlimited dollars for
M‘Kee Rankin, her “ discoverer’’—all these
Nances came back to Her Majesty’s (Melb.) stage
on Saturday. Also, M‘Kee Rankin came back
and was a conspicuous figure in the dress-circle
district, greeting with especial florid enthusiasm
certain lucky pressmen: clapping them on both
shoulders m full view of the audience, even as a
father encourageth his children.
* * * *
Sardou s “ La Tosca,” as some people may
have forgotten, is the story of a prima-donna and
a cantata that never came off. The alleged song-
stress took up a cantata position in the throne-
room of the Court of Rome, but the Queen got
awful news from an Italian “Bobs,” and the
concert was postponed indefinitely, after which
Tosca rushes away to the suburban villa of her
sweetheart, under the impression that he had got
a giddy marquise on the premises. Then the
trouble began. The cantatrice’s unfounded sus-
picions led to the capture of her own best Mario
on a charge of treason, appertaining to a mild
young man with attenuated legs, who took a
deadly dose of poison at the bottom of a well,
and was subsequently hanged by the neck till’
he was deader than ever. Baron Scarpia, the Lord
High Executioner, wrung information from La
Tosca by torturing her Mario in the next room
Every time she said she didn’t know the where-
abouts of the mild young man Scarpia instructed
his myrmidons to make Mario groan horribly,
and the woman capable of keeping a secret which
drills holes in her lover’s neck and forehead does
not yet exist. Nance, as La Tosca, broke dd\vn
m the attempt, like Sarah Bernhardt and Mrs.
Potter before her, but her confession was value-
less to Mario. He met his doom in the last act.
Meanwhile, the sorely-worried vocalist jabbed
Scarpia to his torturous heart with a fruit-
knife for sundry reasons. Later on she committed
suicide. In Nance’s version, she doesn’t suicide :
she gets shot like a dawg. These Bernhardtian
plays, when produced in such a style that blud
and shrieks seem the only thoroughly convincing
items in the entertainment, made one almost sigh
for Shakespeare. A few improving remarks crop up
m every play the Bard wrote. “La Tosca” con-
tains nothing good except good acting oppor-
tunities.
* * * *
Thomas Kingston gives a very first-class
performance of Searpia’s final kicks. Perhaps he
doesrj fc pass away exactly as a surgeon would pre-
scribe the death struggle, but for all purposes of
histrionic deception Kingston’s quitting of this
life is true to nature. Hedies more artistically
than he lives. The Scarpia who tortures and
scowls and revels in his sin is clearly too melo-
dramatic a villain ; otherwise he wouldn’t revive
one’s recollections of Brough as Jonathan Wild in
the burlesque. Ten years ago the gallery might
have called upon Scarpia for a song and dance, and
expected him to pull his fingers with the noise of
a policeman s rattle. Plimmer, as Mario, is pas-
sable ; Atholwood, in two different characters is
effective as usual. The Court scene is caricatured
by a crowd of careworn supers in ill-fitting wigs
who cry Bravo !” to the Queen, Eitzmaurice
Gill, when she volunteers to read a letter, as
though she were a serio-comic at Rickards’s. The
fact that J. C. Williamson doesn’t provide the
company is writ large upon the face of the show
although the scenic fittings are satisfactory!
J.O.W. never would have put that lot of courtiers
into the Palazzo Farnese. Nance O’Neil’s preten-
sions to play a brilliant woman of fashion are
exactly what they were a year ago. She has lost
considerable flesh, and, with it, a little of her
former physical force, which is not to say
that she hadn’t force to spare. But she
has gained nothing of elegance in move-
ment, or speech, or bearing. There are
the same heavy, masculine mannerisms and lack
of refinement. The pit and gallery laughed
boisterously at Nance’s vehemence at times—three
or four times—when a more finished and feminine
artist would have kept the stupidest audience in
thorough sympathy with the character. Nance
trying to express Tosea’s jealousy and tenderness
affected them in the wrong way. The great
scene in Scarpia’s room was darkened unduly,
in the interests of the actress, at the expense of
the play. A soldier has to take certain instruc-
tions from Scarpia. This man’s face should tell
the audience that Scarpia, whilst pretending to
order a bogus shooting of Tosca’s lover, is giving
him the cue for a bona fide slaughter of the
prisoner. In Bernhardt’s production the room
was so well lighted that the soldier, standing on
the threshold of the door at the back of the stage,
revealed all the meaning of the order by his
covert smile and exchange of glances with
Duquesne, the grave and portly Scarpia of the
French co. Present writer remembers that smile
because of its significance. Now, in Nance’s
show, the military minion walks right into the
room instead of stopping on the mat, and even
when (on Saturday night) he stood close to
Scarpia’s table, the light wasn’t bright enough to
tell whether his face was acting the part.
** * #
The new tenor, Barron Berthald, is a
handsome stranger, but a most embarrassing cir-
cumstance for the little crowd of Wagnerians who,
so to speak, have been pulling the strings of
“enlightened opinion” re the merits of every-
thing and everybody connected with George
Musgrove’s opera campaign. For some reason or
other those painful enthusiasts laid down the strange
doctrine that Louis Arens, of the abbreviated nose
and enigmatical enunciation, was a charming
artist. Arens was taken to the bosom of the
Elect. Consequently, Berthald, who is almost as
sweet a tenor as a man can be after singing Wag-
ner’s hero parts for a few years, will have to
receive his full meed of praise by instalments. He
can’t possibly be rated much higher than Arens
all at once. The same difficulty will arise in the
case of Miss Ella Russell, the coming prima-
donna. Being admittedly a mortal, not an angel,
her voice is not capable of immediately out-rival-
ling Madame Slapoffski’s in the ears of that
clever little lady’s persistent flatterers. Mus-
grove’s new people are a severe strain on the
critical faculties of the Elect.
* * • *
Berthald, as aforestated, is a valuable
acquisition to the co. at the Princess’s. He
makes Tannhiiuser an almost interesting sinner
and singer, and when he presently comes along as
Lohengrin the apparition ought to prove a real
dreamy treat for the girls. At time of writing
the Princess’s programme for Saturday and next
week had not been fixed. To-night (Thursday)
the imposing tenor is to Tannhauser again,
and if Miss Lilian Coomber should happen as
Elisabeth in place of the very frequent Madame
Slapoffski, a lot of people will not feel offended by
the change. Madame repeats her bright Mignon
performance on Friday in any case.
** * *
At the Athenaeum Hall there was a large
demonstrative crowd on Saturday, in honour of
the “ Genuine British Biograph’s ” return. The
G. 8.8. is unquestionably the best Australia has
sampled up to date. The pictures are larger and
steadier, as a rule, than the works of various rival
machines that made a similar whirrsome noise
m the same building. Also the subjects sug-
gest that the photographers are favored indi-
viduals who occupy points of vantage by order
of the Lord High Boots when royal funerals and
other impressive sights are available. The
Queen’s Funeral on the present sheet is a fine
moving spectacle, but not so interesting as the
big German battleship, which looks to have been
put through a course of target practice for the
special benefit of the G. 8.8.
** * *
Time has proved that George Musgrove
was wise in his selection of the least-experienced
members of his Opera Cos. Their voices have
worn well, and the improvement in their
acting, as shown by the recent “Lohengrin”
performances, is remarkable. Lilian Coomber,
who seemed a mere amateur when she
first appeared as Marguerite, has developed
into a quite charming Elsa of Brabant.
The critics of the Melb. dailies, and their
humble. echoists on the weeklies, who have
all the time been overrating Madames Janson and
Slapoffski, are now bound to admit grudgingly
that quiet little Lilian Coomber in “Lohengrin”
is the star of the season thusfar. With her fresh
young voice and presence, and earnest, natural
acting of the part, she scores a greater success than
the heavy, stagey Carmen of Madame Janson.
Asa vocal artist Miss Coomber is unfinished, but
in that respect she has improved wonderfully
since she came to Australia.

O. March returns show a big falling off (over
24,0000 z. compared with March, 1900). Towers and
Qympie are both 5000 oz. behind on quartz results, and
Towers tailings and concentrates slumped 50 per cent..
One big Towers mine went from 635., buyers, to 4&s.
sellers, in last five weeks. Things generally a dull grey.
* * * *
A doctor is wanted for Woodlark Island, British
Papua—“ one having a special knowledge of malarial
fevers preferred.” Verbatim from the lips of a returned
Woodlarker, now a patient in a N.Q. hospital“ There
were on the island when I left about 50 or 60 men, not
counting outside alluvial miners. Of these, two-thirds
were sick with malaria. No man there gets a full month 3
pay _if they do 15 or 16 shifts it is considered good. The
medical man was not registered, and he, poor devil, was
run to death, and was left unpaid in most cases, lne
co., it appeared, paid him nothing’, nor was anything
kept out of the men’s pay for advice and medicine. The
country is all dense scrub, and all journeys have to be
done in the sweltering heat and on foot. Of course, the
head and front of all the offending is the mosquito, which
is in swarms —spotted and large like the swamp ones.
There was no house or accommodation for a doctor when
we left. A doctor should get £5OO a month to go to that
God-forsaken place ; and then he ought to have no respon-
sibilities or any care for his life. We engaged for a year,
but left, with shattered health and out of pocket, after
four months. If we had not managed to get away we
would be there yet—underground.”

Mt. Garnet Freehold : The issue of 35,000 new
shares authorised (first to be offered to shareholders),
tonnage increased by adding 2£ to 3 per cent,
coke to the charcoal, smelters now reported
running well. Funds, the directorate remarks,
should quickly accumulate, and divs. follow. Shares,
sellers under £2—about,half what they were a year ago.
Always sell before the smelting is quite as good an axiom
as when applied to the crushing. Latest reports indicate
that the mine is all right, but it is clear they did not cor-
rectly estimate cost of working without a railway.

Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

* * * *
Ina certain far Northern town are two banks, and
they were asked to tender for the local municipal body’s
business. Now, one of these banks was honored with the
chairman’s overdraft, and the manager therefore gave the
chairman two tenders in separate envelopes ; one at about
the rate the other bank was expected to quote, and the
other a good deal lower. These were placed respectively
in the chairman’s right and left breeches-pockets—
the opposition bank’s tender being put in his coat-pocket.
His instructions were to open the opposition bank’s letter
first'and then draw forth whichever of the other two ten-
ders might suit the case best ; the big one if it was lower
than or equal to the enemy’s, the other if necessary. This
little rig came-off all right, and the triumphant
manager boasted of his cuteness to his friends. The
strange part of the business is that he and the chairman
are still walking about.

Some facts and figures relating to the Equitable
Life Assurance Society of the United States :

The total funds of the Equitable, at 31st December, 1900,
were £63,457,930, of which nearly £28,000,000 has been
accumulated in the last seven years. On the usual valua-
tion, which is largely on a 4 per cent, basis, the EquiUble
has the stupendous surplus of £13,778,577, the liabilities
under policies being only £49,679,353, against the
assets (£63,457,930) above-stated. But even if the
valuation was made on a 3 per cent, basis, which
would be more stringent than that of any Australasian
office, there would be a surplus of about £8,000,000. At
present, the interest earnings are at the rate of a little
over £4 6s. per sent., which is a very handsome rate as
interest earnings go in the United States ; and expenses
are equal to £lB 3s. 2d. per cent, on total income. This
is almost twice the rate of the A.M.P. Society, and it
would be regarded in Australia as an extravagant charge
for an office of the Equitable’s dimensions. But a very
large proportion of the business of the great American
offloes is new business, which is the kind that is costly to
procure, and the heavy expense-rate seems unavoidable
m consequence.
With the Equitable, as with the New York Life
Society, alluded to in a recent issue, the only item
among the assets which is visibly objectionable is that of
“ Deferred premiums, £503,334.” This is based on the
idea that, though the society accepts half-yearly and
quarterly premiums, all premiums should be paid an-
nually in advance, therefore if a policy comes into exist-
ence on Ist November and the premiums are paid quar-
terly, so long as that policy lasts nine months unpaid
premiums appear in every balance-sheet as an asset. It
is a clumsy and purposeless idea, and why so many offices
keep this intangible and unproductive asset standing in
(heir balance-sheets passes comprehension. But apart
from this one dubious item—a very small one by com-
parison with the size of the surplus—the Equitable assets
appear to be thoroughly sound, and they are guaranteed by
the rigid Government inspection to which all United States
offices are subject. As to the size and energy and pro-
gressive character of the Equitable, they are beyond
question.

The Mutual Life Insurance Co. of New York —
the largest of the three American Giants —closed its 58th
year on 31st December, 1900. The record compares with
j lat of some previous years as follows :
Total „ „ Total Added to
Income. olalms - Expenses. Outgoings. Funds,
j 195 £9,978.939 £4,748,815 £2,135,762 £6,884,577 £3,094,362
J 196 10,421,359 5,262,203 2,107,705 7,369,908 3,051,451
1897 11,312,833 5,316,642 2,063,053 7,379,695 3,933,138
1198 12,116,267 5,071,029 2,157,406 7,228,435 4,887,832
! .19 12,932,673 5,540,220 2,535,825 8,076,045 4,856,628
1900 12,440,000 5,413,113 2,686,807 8,099,920 4,340,080
The total assets of the Mutual Life of New York the
biggest life office on earth—at 31st December, 1900, were
£66,889,764, and the surplus of assets over liabilities was
something considerably exceeding £11,000,000. Pre-
Bumably this is on the understanding that future interest
earnings are estimated at 4 per cent, per annum ; but the
Mutual of New York could reduce its estimates of fu-
ture interest to 3 or 2£ per cent, and still have a very large
margin of safety. The expenses last year were £2llls. lid.
per cent, of the total income, or over 27£ per cent, of
premiums ; so the Mutual, like the two other American
giants, is a very expensively managed institution.
(Curiously enough, the S.M. HeraljD declares once a year
that the expense-rate of the Maoriland State Life Depart-
ment, which is 14 per cent, on total income or 20j per
cent, on premiums, is so heavy as to prove that State
Life Insurance is a hopeless failure, yet that paper never
once complains of the privately-managed Mutual of New
York’s 27i per cent, expense-rate as casting any discredit
on private management).
The Mutual Life of New York is still another
American office which swells its accounts by reckoning
as assets half-yearly or quarterly premiums not paid and
not yet due. It varies the process a little by calling this
item “loans on policies.” When an insurer takes out his
policy on Ist November, and decides to pay quarterly,
the close of the year finds that policy with only one
quarter’s premium paid. Nevertheless, the whole year’s
premium is put down as received (though it isn’t received)
and the unpaid three-quarters of it is set down on the
other side as a sum lent by the co. to the insurer on the
security of his policy, though the co. hasn’t lent him
anything on the security of his policy. And this alleged
loan, and similar alleged loans on other policies similarly
circumstanced, appear as an asset as long as these policies
endure. It is a ridiculous and unprofitable way of creat-
ing an alleged asset that is of no possible use to
anybody. In the case of the Mutual of New York
the amount of this alleged asset is about £420,000
a sum which makes so little visible difference
to a co. with £66,000,000 of assets and £11,000,000 surplus
that it is all the more remarkable that the management
allows the dubious and circuitous thing to appear at all.
The Mutual of New York is undoubtedly a very sound
institution, with a splendid surplus and a good reputa-
tion, but its heavy expense - rate and its “ loans on
policies ” item are two misfortunes none the less.

like most concerns in the same way of business, there is
a grievous falling away from the big profits of former
years. Since 1894 it has progressed after this fashion

The years 1897,1898, and 1899 showed a dead loss, and as
1900 produced a profit of £2428 it may be fairly said that
things are looking up to some extent. But the co. has
applied a large amount (nearly £10,000) of forfeited capi-
tal to provide for losses, and if these had fallen upon
earnings they would have practically swamped the
reserves. There is now only £B9 of forfeited capital
available to meet further depreciation, and if there is
any serious additional slump in the co.’s assets it must
be met out of reserves. These are £2509 less than they
were in 1895, despite the passing of eight dividends in
five years, so though things are improving the outlook
for shareholders can’t be regarded as very bright.

Writes "Asp”: In "Wild Cat” column of a
recent issue you forecast an increase of the div. of a ce -
tain big bank. In ’94 the same bank, in common with
others, reduced its officers’ salaries 10 per cent, owing; t
the depressed state of banking business and the reauc
cost of living, at the time promising that, should times
improve, the first charge of the bank would be to rein-
state its officers at the old screw. Times have become
more prosperous, as evidenced by “profits, ana inis
institution has increased its div. and padded up its
reserves, but never a sign of that 10 per cent, is to oe
seen. The increased price of necessaries of life !8 pitnuiiy
evident when the poor sweatees proceed to pay their bins
at the month end. Other banks (at the mention of whose
names the institution in question would draw its kilts
about its fat legs and shudder) have dealt justly with
their employes, and put them back on the old footing,
though less able to afford it. A bank on this earth has
not a body to be kicked or a soul to be damned, hut on
the other side of the Stygian Stream I trust that the per-
petrators of this cruel swindle will be sifted out, ana, as
a devout Christian, I feel sure that they will not com-
plain of the plates being cold.

The wisdom of Horatio Bottomley, dropped
during the stirring of the Westralian stink in London .
“As language was given us to conceal our thoughts, so
share-registers are employed to conceal our holdings.
This was a tip to those who watch registers in order to
get a clue to market movements.
* • •
London Ckitio has at last finished its “ Direc-
tory of Guinea-pigfS.” It extended over 78 continuous
issues of the paper, gave the names of 688 guinea-pigs
directing 9219 companies, the great majority of which
should never have been born, and have since gone into
oblivion. Editor Hess now proposes to issue the list in
book-form, bringing it up to date, excluding therefrom
the names of pigs who have since died, and including
those who have arisen since the alphabetical list
started. Before squandering money in new mining or
other co.’s it would pay the average investor to get the
“ Directory of Guinea-pigs” : the chances are 100 to 1 he
would find in it someone connected with the co. into
which he was about to plunge, and in nine cases
out of ten the same man’s previous company-connections
would act more as warning than as recommendation. Ihe
index to names shows that one Douglas Arthur Onslow
wears the belt as the director of 65 different co s. Ihis
gentleman in a recent issue of the Critic is described as
the champion guinea-pig of the age—what he doesn t
know about reconstruction is really not worth bothering
about. Edward Thomas Bead has been the director of
49 co’s. ; Edmund Davis has controlled 46, and there are
several responsible for between 30 and 40. “ Half the
company directors,” writes Hess, “ are capable of nothing,
the other half are capable of anything.”

* * • *
One of Victoria’s first batch of old-age pen-
sioners at Stawell invested part of his pension in a kit of
miner’s tools, went out prospecting, and dropped on a
patch of gold. The other day the local Clerk of Courts
got a letter from the old fellow saying that, as the old-age
pensions were meant for those who were in need, “ and
as I have come on ‘ a bit of a patch,’ I don’t want to draw
mine any more, at present.” Australia’s most sturdy and
manly class is the instinctive prospector, the man
who never works for wages if he sees the remotest
show of “ going on his own.”
"On Thames goldfields, Maoriland,” writes
“Weka,” “are many old-age pensioners who prospect
under Govt, aid, thus receiving about 18s. per week.
They seldom put a tunnel far enough in to require a
candle, and often dress the sides like a plastered wall.
The value of their work is mostly nil, and Govt, super-
vision would swallow up the vote. Asked one of these
derelicts how his mine was getting on, and he told me he
was ‘ waiting for the machinery to arrive.’ He had made
a barrow, and was waiting for the wheel 1”
* * * *
Maoriland hasn’t yet extricated itself from the
Midland Railway muddle of 16 years’ standing. Both
company and debenture-holders, having been badly
beaten in the Courts, tried to damage M.L. securities in
London, and lastly the latter “ threw themselves on the
mercy of the colony,” and petitioned Parliament for
“such equitable relief as to your Honorable House may
deem meet.” The Select Parliamentary Committee ap-
pointed to deal with the petition merely recommended
Govt, to refer the case to a Royal Commission. The
Commission was in due course appointed, and, as usual,
was used to reward Govt, supporters with seats thereon.
It is now picnicing through the country at a cost to the
taxpayers of about £5O per diem. On the face of it, there
can be no claim against the public, seeing that the co.
broke its contract and caused three million acres of Crown
lands to be withdrawn from settlement for 15 years.
Nevertheless it is freely stated that something substantial
will be done to placate the debenture-holders, on account
of a supposed influence in London financial circles, which
they might use to impair M.L. credit. It is unfortunately
true that an abject feeling of dependence on London
capitalists has so demoralised Maorilanders that many of
them believe they would have to do without roads and
bridges if the benevolent oversea moneybag did not find
the wherewithal. By playing on this feeling the deben-
ture-holders may succeed in frightening Maoriland into
liberally compensating them for getting off its back.
* * * *
The projected Stratford - Whangamomona
(M.L.) railway will serve a bed of coal estimated at 800
sq." miles in extent—the biggest in the Southern Hemi-
sphere. It reaches from within six miles of Stratford to
near Gisborne, and northward from Mokau to far beyond
Wanganui River. The coal, according to experts, will
produce 25 per cent, more gas than the best Westport or
Brunner. Govt, has reserved 25,000 acres in the centre
of the bed.
* * * *
The first thing the Federal Postmaster-General
should see to when he gets his coat off is that Australia
gets her sets of postage-stamps without delay. We have
endured long enough the inconvenience of six different
sets within the country, where they could be employed
unrestricted neither for remitting, returning, nor for the
many uses where postage-stamps are most convenient.

S.A. Brewing Co., which is partly Melb. -owned
(N. Fitzgerald and Smalcum M'Eacharn are directors),
goes on improving after a rather bad slump six or seven
years ago. Last year's profit (to Feb. 28) reached £33,467 ;
from which deduct £lO,OOO interest on debentures and
£3467 for depreciation and repairs. A 5 per cent. div.
has been paid in two instalments—total, £12,500. The
balance carried forward is £22,614 ; and the shareholders
accordingly scent an early rise in divs. The S.A. beer
biz. doesn’t seem to boom much (the holy city, like most
unholy ones, is over-breweried) ; but the co. has a
brewery at Broken Hill and has a big say in the liquoring
of that droughty part.
* • * •
The Economist has an article by J. H. Curie,
its special mining commissioner, on Oobar main-lode. He
describes it as a remarkable mineral deposit from 40 to
50ft. thick, broken by faults which throw it quite half-a-
mile from its course. Gold-bearing only to a considerable
depth, when copper comes in without the gold going out;
if it were nearer seaboard it would rank with Broken
Hill in productiveness, but is handicapped by distance,
and dryness. Thinks the Great Cobar in some respects
the greatest copper-mine in Australia; developed 700 ft.,
three large lenticular ore bodies. Roughly estimates ore
in sight at 700,000 tons ; ore an ideal smelting proposi-
tion ; present production about 9000 tons per month,
which could be greatly increased, and the monthly profits
are supposed to be £lO,OOO. Of the Cobar Chesney, he
says the ore is too silicious for direct smelting, and 50 per
cent, of the copper contents was lost in the first effort to
make concentration, but if, with the altered plant, it
makes a much higher extraction, the mine will be the
means of opening up a great future for the field ; if it
fails, the smaller mines will have to finally shut-down.
Of the Cobar Gold Mines: “In a colony which has
at present not a single regular profit-earning gold-
mine, the Cobar mine is probably the best.’ Forcible
perhaps, certainly not correct. Of the Occidental, Curie
says little, but admits it to be a “promising show.
Heretofore a mine crushing with 50 stamps from an open
cut over 70ft. wide, 150 ft. deep and 120 ft. long, with a
production to date of over 100,000 tons, paying divs., and
unlimited ore in sight, has been regarded as a performing
property. Quite a mistake, no doubt, now that Mr.
Curie declares it is only promising. Curie once said that
the Associated was the biggest mine in Westralia, would
produce 15,0000 z. per month at a profit of £36,000, and so
great was writer’s faith in Curie that for a long time he
believed him. Curie is a big writer and a big man with
big feet, and when he makes a mistake it is a pro-
portionally big mistake.
Cobar Chesney (N.S.W.): Mr. Greenway, Gen.
Manager of Block 14, reports on this mine that to a depth
of 250 ft. the “ ore bodies are auriferous with little or no
copper, and below that depth are well with
copper pyrites containing a little gold ;” that with
£BOOO the mine can be put in a condition to deal with a
weekly production of 1000 tons if arrangements can be
made for the sale of picked ore from the mine and the
concentrates from the mill; but if it is found necessary
for the Ohesnev to erect its own smelting-works £20,000
will be required for that and the other purposes.”
* * * *
Great Cobar Copper Co. The action by the
minority representing 8000 shares began last week ; when
it will end Heaven knows. Perhaps within the next two
years, and when £2 per share instead of 2s. has been
called up to keep the lawyers going. The first applica-
tion for compulsory instead of voluntary winding-up was
dismissed. An equity suit will probably be the next
stage, and the Privy Council the last.
» » » *
Australia’s Gold Recovery Co. was never a
financial success in Australia, though it missed many a
big chance of making money by buying and cyamding tail-
ings on its own—instead of which it let other people step
in and make fortunes; the co. tried to enforce heavy
royalties, which in the greater number of cases were
never paid. Now the Privy Council decision has upset all
calculations; moreover, the term of the chief patent expires
in Oct. next. Decided to wind-up and reform under the
name of the Australian Mining and Gold Recovery Co.
The original co. took a prominent hand in the flotation
of the Cobar Gold Mines, in which it still holds over
16,000 shares, and at a recent meeting in London it was
incidentally mentioned that, while these shares had cost
them only 65., they were now standing at about par., and
that the enterprise was expected to settle down into a
regular div.-paying concern, and that it was by far the
best mining investment the Australian Gold Recovery
Co. had made.
* * * *
How well miners do stick together. Lately the
Broken Hill managers asked the men of each mine to
appoint two delegates to confer with the Managers’ Asso-
ciation on the mining-apprentice question. The men
met and agreed that the proper body to meet the
Managers’ Association was the Miners’ Association.
Manaeers will not, of course, recognise the latter; so
negotiations were off. Then the manager of the big
m j ne —the B.H. Prop.—tried another way. He said he
wanted to meet his miners personally to find out what
they really thought. “ Take a ballot if you want to know
what we really think;” and he couldn’t evade that.
Then the word was passed round, and “ For apprentices
2, against 826,” was the result.
’•* * * *
The Bulletin publishes for what it may be
worth this letter from the field “Re printed accounts
of close extraction at Gt. Boulder Main Reef, which
writer thinks is verv doubtful. Firstly, the stuff isn’t
half-roasted most of'the time. Next, when it gets to the
vats it is supposed to agitate for ten hours, which it very
rarely does. Also, it isn’t half-treated in the press-room.
When a press is first filled the contents are supposed to
wash with a solution for an hour, but rarely does that
happen ; five minutes is nearer the mark. Then half the
year you can’t get a cloth for the filters ; but keep patch-
ing the old ones, which become hard as bricks, and the
patches keep coming off. So. instead of taps running
clear the slimes come straight through and half-fill the
launders before press is full or supposed to be full, which
former is rarely the case. Slimes in the launders are not
treated again, but are trucked to the dump. Always
noticed when weighing a frame of slimes in press (which
they do very rarely) they pick a full frame. So if they
get their average weight of presses from this method it is
all wrong. In conclusion, all I can say is that if such a good
extraction can be got in such a fashion and the monthly
output kept up on half-empty presses, theG.B.M.R. is a
wonderful mine, and the treatment is better than any on
these fields.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia (W. A.).—Latest news by way of
London: 700 ft. level driving south on very rich ore.
Stone disclosed on this level is the richest seam in the
mine. Developments on the parallel lodes turning out
good ore, practically making a new mine from the sur-
face downwards and doubling previous estimates of
value. Yet in the face of all this, the market-value of
the mine has depreciated half-a-million in a few months,
and refuses to budge even on receipt of these boomster
cables. The cat has got out of the bag, and cable-lies
have no effect.

Associated, W.A.: Hope is now centred in
bore-holes, of which there are many. It is all up with
this mine if they all find nothing after boring several
thousand feet in different directions and depths. Manager
Hewitson, it is said, threatens to leave at end of year.
Last month’s output, 3000 tons for 2000 odd oz. After
waiting a year, the sulphide - plant is defended on
the ground that 2oz. ore was expected at the mill
and only 14dwt. rock received, and that everything was
worked'in the interests of the bears, but in other respects
the defence is rather weak. The fact is ignored that
tailings and slimes formed a plaster-of-Paris in the cyanide
vats, and had to be chiselled out and that the extraction
was so low that some hundreds of tons of tailings were
run down below for filling purposes to hide the failure.

A couple of Melbourne plain-clothes police
out on a Sunday-trading raid at Williamstown the
other Sfibbath had a mean trick
played upon them by two depraved
youths. The officers drove up to the
first pub. in a smart trap drawn by a
spanking' pony, and, leaving the
turn-out in charge of two young
men who were festooning the bar-
front, plunged in to beguile the
proprietor into dealing out beers.
When they reappeared their lovely
buggy and their high-stepping prad
had passed from the scene. After a
long wait the disappearance was
reported to the local police, and
some time later the two youths into
whose charge the fit-out was given
by the lynxes of the law were dis-
covered driving wildly up and
down, full of beer and beatitude.
One of them had recognised the
officers and the two had used the
haw s horse and buggy to streak
from hotel to hotel, warning all the
publicans in the place of the ap-
proach of the foe, for which service
they were rewarded with abundant
grog-

to The Bulletin
on the treatment
of “colonial”
officers in the
British army :
It must be re-
membered, in the
first place, that the
army officer has
always been ultra-
conservative.
Taking my own ex-
perience : I remem-
ber when a cousin
of mine got a com-
mission by bringing
100 militia-men into
the Black Watch.
He led the life of a
dog at first, al-
though there was
not much of the
novug homo about
him, his father be-
ing an officer in the
H. E. O.’s service.
When the latter in-
terviewed the C.O.
of the regt., it ap-
peared that things
had gone so far
that the complain-
ant would have had
to leave the service
for standing too
much bullying.
When Cardwell's
scheme came out,
the first sub-lieu-
tenant to join us
was named Tribe.
He had been ad-
vised not to allow
himself to be im-
posed upon. It was
then customary for
all last-joined subs,
to have an unfair
amount of duty put
on them. Mr. Tribe
bucked and wa
sent to Coventry,
and finally had to
exchange or leave.
I don’t know how
Fruit Salts and
Burton Beer and
Dublin Stout got on,
but I was at a regi-
mental mass meet-
ing at Woolwich
when an ex-captain
of the regt. was
blackballed as an
honorary member
because he had
“gone into coal.”
I never had it oast
up to me that my
father was a whisky
maker, because we
had a lot of our national shopkeepers in the army. But, after
all, I don’t wonder at the army men jibbing- at the forcing
of the Australian commissioned on them, if what is said
about these appointments is true. Have these men been
asked to pass the Woolwich and Sandhurst tests of mili-
tary and ordinary education ? I feel quite sure that
officers and gentlemen ” from Australia will be well
received by the ex-officio “o. and g.” of the most ex-
clusive British corps if they are qualified as sioh, and
do not unduly interfere with the roster.
* * * ,
In Melbourne the daily papers are not
published on Good Friday or on Xmas day, which
are, therefore, red-letter days in the hard-worked
press world. But the Sydney morning papers
come out just the same. On Friday last, S. M.
Herald published the usual sermon where the
leading article appears, and, with its advertising
columns chock full of amusement-announcements
for the leisured public, Granny went to press
with a clear conscience.
* * * .
Sydney wastes no reverence over the day
on which Christians say they believe Christ died
for the world. At the Agricultural Show on
Good Friday, 50,000 (fifty thousand) people as-
sembled {vide cash and turnstile-returns) to
witness the horse-jumping.
- * * * *
The balance of the last.N.S. W. contingent
of troops left for the Cape (where the
plague is now raging) per the ex-plague-
stricken Antilluin on Good ITrid&y. -The vessel
was berthed at the very wharf that the Surrey was
moored to some 12 months ago, when the second
contingent departed. The difference of the
scene ! No scores of thousands of spectators ; no
boats chartered by Govt, or speculating ferry-
companies ; no Ministers of the Crown to make
speeches. Just a few hundred friends and rela-
tives of the outgoing five-bob-a-days, kept in order
by some scores of policemen, a for-the-look-of-the-
thing escort of permanent artillerymen, and that
was all!
* . * * *
So far, printed reference has not been
made to this incident of the Commonwealth cele-
brations m Sydney. Whilst the crowd was await-
ing the procession at Martin Flace, the women in
front were occasionally forced, by the pressure
behind, across the sacred line, guarded by police
and civilian soldiers, who, good-humoredly enough
forced them back again. This mode of keeping
the line, however, did not please a certain
mounted officer, who rode his horse broad-
side amidst the women, scattering them
m all directions, until one irate damsel
smote the warrior’s horse over the nose
with the rim of her sailor-hat, where-
upon the steed reared suddenly up and
threw his rider literally out of his boots, 55 which
remained in the stirrups. Then Martin-place
heard the most joyous cheer of all that day, fol-
lowed by a roar of laughter at the spectacle of the
officer, covered with dust, and encumbered by
much sash and sword, pirouetting around as he
pulled on his boots again. And when the police
helped him into the saddle, and the grand stand
cheered him, it did not reassure him enough
to try any further horse tactics with the gutter
crowd.

One good work N.S.W. Premier John See
may be confidently counted upon pushing forward
with dispatch, now that his hands are free, is the
long-talked-of-asylum for inebriates. The Act
and the'Regulations are both, thanks to Dr. Creed,
M.L.C., ready, and the House alone is lacking!
See calls this his own question and there is no
doubt ; that very shortly the afflicted dipsomaniac
will receive the treatment his malady needs.

“C.M.G.”: Where T was standing when
the Hopetoun procession passed through Mel-
bourne, t’other day, public interest was centred,
not on the stem-wound Governor, bowing me-
chanically to yelling small boys, but on an unfor-
tunate horse attached to one of the gun-carriages.
The poor beast had slipped and fallen forward on
the wet block pavement, and knocked away three
of his front teeth, gum and all, and stood in quite
a pool of blood, disregarded by officialdom and the
Law, which, had it seen a cabman’s horse in the
same plight, would have furiously ri n- n the
driver for infringing all the precepts of t' e S iciety
for the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals.
V * * *
‘‘Melbournian ” sends an acid letter :
Dear Bulletin, —Doesn’t it strike you that some of the
tales re married-women-doctors and their physical unfit-
ness for practice may have been invented by their male
rivals ? I read in your last issue : “ Still another of Mel-
bourne’s better known (by the way, they are all of them
better known than the average superfluous male straggler
in the profession) women doctors goes out of practice.
Few —very few—risk matrimony and medicine together.
And The Bulletin is sorry to hear that one of the earliest
who did, the philanthropic Dr. Constance Stone, wife of
Dr. Jones, has for months past been compelled to virtually
cease practice through a most serious illness.” You will
be sorry to hear that you have been “had” by a now
quite ancient yarn, for which there was very little
foundation. Rumor-mongers have ascribed all sorts of
incapacitating complaints—from cancer down to nervous
prostration—to Dr. Constance what time she has been
looking after her patients in the ordinary way. Ap-
parently there was malice at work in the matter ; but,
whether or no, the estimable lady suffered more from
these false reports than from any indisposition that
followed the birth of her child some year or more ago.
At the present time she is taking three months’ leave
of absence from the Queen Vic. Hospital, and may be
consulted, no doubt, at Ballarat, where she has gone for
the benefit of the little one’s health, as much as for a
holiday “on her own.” Stories about the sicknesses or
forthcoming retirements of lady-doctors will soon be
epidemic, if the press accepts them without inquiry.
The male practitioners, a good many of whom are always
unfit to follow their profession, owing to drink, or mor-
phia, or the clubbish habit of being never less than two
hours late in attending urgent cases, will see that the
diplomatic lies are worked into print if possible.

The first case under Victoria's Amended
Marriage Act (why “marriage?”) happened in
Melbourne t’other day, when a putative father
was ordered to pay up £7 confinement expenses
for an expected little stranger, money to be re-
turned if the infant failed to materialise. Defen-
dant was a constable, of thrifty nature.

S.M. Herald wants May 24 made a per-
manent holiday— The Bulletin bets that it won t
be and cites Canada as an _ example. J } n fi°
loyalists are sycophantic imitators. A bell-
wether jumps over a stick, and, though
the stick be withdrawn, the flock jumps
at the same spot. Laurier, the crossbred,
omits no opportunity to prove that his
crossbreed has not impaired his loyalty,
and jumps. Though there’s no question of cross-
breeding, Australian loyalists jump m the same
way. The Victoriolaters will soon get over their
unreasoning fit of adulation : in a very few years
the late Queen will be quite as dead as is Queen
Anne. Also, York’s birthday will presumably
have to be kept as a public holiday, and a very
big section of the Australian people—both em-
ployers and employes—are utterly and thoroughly
sick of the ever-increasing number of public holi-
days.
Plague having looked in again, rat-ra,ids
and rat-exterminators are once more the question.
A very simple and more efficient rodent-killer than
half the bought stuffs can bo made by mixing equal
proportions of pollard (or other meal) and plaster-
of-paris. Leave this near their haunts and put a
vessel of water close by that they may drink.
Dead “sixpences” warranted to be plentiful
within a few hours.

Concerning an orphan :
A high Australian State official has just gone through
an experience to which King Solomon’s baby-act was
child’s play. A woman in the State capital was entrusted
by the proper dept, with the rearing of a three-year-old
orphan child, but certain representations about this per-
son having been made to aforesaid official, he gave in-
structions to have the boy transferred to the care of
another woman residing in the country, say at, A. ine

tions were signed, protests made, and finally a deputation
of M’s.P. persuaded the official that the facts about the
lady were not so peculiar as he had imagined. Further-
more the first custodian made an offer to take the hoy
free of charge and bring him up as her own, and, as the
second party was being paid so much per week, the
official jumped at the chance, and despatched a mj rmi-
don to bring the boy back from X. The emissary
was met with a flat refusal, and returned
empty-handed. Then the official was wroth, and
upon a second refusal despatched the Senior Inspector of
his staff to rea-
son with her.

found the wom-

A census-collector relates that when filling
in the census-form for a family in a boiling-down
Sydney suburb he noticed that though the parents
were of “ no religious denomination,” each of the
nine children had a different religion marked
against his name. “ What’s ya rid ear ?” he asked
the mother. ‘ ‘ Oh,” replied that prolific parent,
“me and me ’usbandere thought that we’d surely
git one of ’em on to th’ right religion if we giv
every one a run on their rown. We orta strike’t
in nine chances, eh ? What do you think ?”
* • * *
The census sub-enumerator for a certain
Vic. borough, while on his round delivering
census-papers, called at the house of an elderly
widow, who had no sooner opened the door than
she exclaimed —-“Go away ! 1 don’t want, any
man on my premises ! Go away ! ” “ But,” pro-
tested the census-man, “I must deliver- ”
“Go away I tell you ! I don’t want anything. I
won’t have a man near me. I had a man here
once—and I had three pairs of twins ! I’ll have
nothing to do with men any more.” After much
trouble she was induced to take the census-paper,
but only on the understanding that the male
monster should not come near the house again.

From Broken Hill, of all places in the
world, where there is a Labor Mayor and Council,
and Unionism prevails, The Bulletin receives a
“ report and financial statement from Oct. 20,
1900, to March 7, 1901,” of the “ Friendly Socie-
ties’ Medical Institute and Dispensary.” For this.
4| months the “financial statement” shows —
medical officer’s salary, £B6 4s. ; dispenser’s salary,
£3B Is. 5d.; boy, wages, 10s.” The membership
of the affiliated societies is 1056, and the names on
the doctor’s list 809 ; and half of these are married
men with families ! And for 41 months for
attending this crowd the doctor has received
£B6 45., and the dispenser £3B Is. sd. ! In the
circumstances The Bulletin isn’t at all surprised
to learn that in these months no less than
three doctors have been tried. The working-man
is a hard taskmaster.
* * * *
Coming out to Australia, a certain most
charming actress struck up a warm friendship'
with a fellow-passenger, who happens to be a-
doctor, and who, the other day, conducted her over’
a big hospital of which he is an honorary attendant.
During their tour of inspection a nurse chanced to.
glance into the top-hat he had left on the hall-
table. Therein were photos, of the actress in
several positions, and since that day that hospital
has worn a large, wide smile. For nobody there
had ever before seen anything in a bell-topper but
a stethoscope.

TWO POINTS OF VIEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We stood in the Park one day,
Jack Delong and I,
Watching the cavalcade
Trot and canter by.
I was in love with Maud,
Blue eyes and auburn hair,
While Jaclt would give all Tie owned
For Tom Foster’s chestnut mare.
“ The beauty! there she does!"
Said Jack, My heart beat high,
Thinking of Maud. Said he:
“ What a flank! What a fiery eye!
I turned, and hit him■ square
In the face—like an ass, of course;
For I was thinking girl,
And Jack was talking horse.
That Beastly Cat
[FOR THE BULEETIN.]
THE ancient lilac-tree outside her lattice
Its heavy perfumes flung;
I said: “ I wonder where that yellow cat is—
That cat with silvery tongue ? ”
I moved with caution o’er the crunching gravel
I looked above to see
My Mary—after weeks of weary travel
| By land and se&.
Now, Mary's cat—the household pet, the daisy—
Was ever at her side ;
Where Mary was the cat was—sleek and lazy—,
The cat with yellow hide.
I softly tapped—the silence was unbroken.
I pushed the door.
I said, “Of good or ill is this the token ? ”
And crossed the floor.
Ah! she was there—my Mary, fair and blushing.-
Her lips I pressed ;
And, all her anxious questions gently hushing, !
I took her to my breast.
But as she rested on my loving arm
Her dainty head,
I noticed with a start of wild alarm,
A HAIR op RED !
Of fiery red ! —a coarse-grained hair—a twister !
I cried, “ Why, that’s 4
Been left upon you by a man’s red whisker ”
“ No—'tis the cat’s ! ”
j “ Ah, Mary, no ! you cannot thus deceive me.
Oh, woe is mine!
iThat long hair never grew, false maid, believe me,
pv On hide feline.
“ It did! it is! ’’—and thus in wrath we parted ;
But on the mat
I paused a second, e’en though broken-hearted.
• To kick that blessed cat!
SQSUIB.
“ AUSTRALIA FACING THE DAWN."
O’Sullivan, Esquire,
Dear Sir, may I inquire
Whether you still desire
That we should face the dawn—
In bronze —from that forlorn,
And periwinkled hummock,
Whose name suggests a straitened stomach?
Remember —our Nation,
Knew nought of starvation
At the celebration
•Of Federation :
When the cry of our birth
Was drowned in the mirth,
Of picnics and spreads,
'Champagne, and swelled heads.
If your Statue’s come to stay,
Do revise your theories, pray,
Of poetic unity,
And let it reel away
Further down the bay
'To stand —as best it may—
On the greasy “ Sow and Pigs”
Where the sea foams round like shandy,
And a “ Bottle and Glass ” are handy,
And the light-ship lurches and jigs.

A WOMAN'S LETTER.

Our Easter Week’s gaieties are marked with an
?> r , Prehuiinary canter, preparatory to the
xtoyal stakes oeing run for, as it were.
At the Agricultural Show the crush has been a
record, but otherwise it is the same old show as
ever. There isn’t anything half as amusing to be
seen out there as you will find in our Young
peoples Industrial Exhibition later on, judging
by the Gazette of the latter. I find chronicled
among the interesting Plain Needlework events
of the latter: “Patched Seat Boy’s Trousers—
Holes to be ivorn, not cut.” Who is going to wear
them, I wonder, and how little do their unsus-

L ?§ er <f a y at the A.J.C. races was dressed
with subdued gaudiness—black, white, and grey
costumes keeping up the correct impression of
fashionable regret. The prettiest woman on the
lawn was a last year’s bride in heliotrope voile,
much tucked and softened with inserted lace.
Her younger sister, who has not debuted yet
also bade fair to keep up the family reputation for
complexion and good looks, in a girlish grey
frock, with a big white hat. Another youthful
maiden particularly well dressed was Miss
Queenie Levien in a pale blue crepe-de-chine and
string-colored lace with long black gloves and big
black velvet hat. Mrs. Philip Morton was in
leaf-green voile, tucked over green glace, with a
toque of shaded velvet leaves. Miss Irene Marks
wore a smart cream-cloth costume, with a little
bolero over a swathed vest, and a big cream toque.
Among the black-frocked women I noticed Mrs.
Pilcher, Mrs. Herbert Marks, Miss Carter (from
Melbourne), Miss Marie Nacelle, and Mrs
Levien.
But Monday was the scene of our really well-
considered fracking. Mrs. Ted Rundle’s straw-
berry pink cloth dress was made en Princesse at
the back, while the front was pouched and
strapped with ribbon velvet. Mrs. le Poer French
(Miss Nellie Cox, who is back on a visit to her
people in Sydney) was smartly frocked in mauve
frieze, with an Eton coat and the newest of hats—
white felt, crunched up with black glace ribbon
and a wreathing of violets. Miss Grace Palotta
already well “aegised” by Sydney society
paraded the lawn with a thin, straw-hatted
was under the tender cynosure of
the very Nicest eyes. She was frocked simply in
a black foulard blouse, inserted with ecru Irish
lace with touches of gold about it, a black skirt
and big, ecru straw hat with black feathers. A
squatter’s family was extensively represented—
the newest from ’ome wearing an ivory coat and
skirt cut in London’s latest style.
Flowing dresses were even more of a flow than
ever, trains and trails cleaning the lawn at great
length. Mrs. Ernest Davies’ frock was a pic-
turesque creation of quaint chine silk, inserted
With Irish lace, worn with a wide basket-straw
hat trimmed up with big bows of pink panne
"bbon. .Mrs. Burdekin was in lacey block.
Miss Muriel Marks’ coat and skirt was of lavender
frieze with gold buckles on the little coat, the
toque of blue satin making a snappy contrast.
A London lady, married into fashionable
Sydney society, was curiously hatted with
her hair on her neck and her hat over her
nose, and frocked in grey. Also from London
(perhaps) was a large lady in a black evening
dress with an unhappy frock-coated courtier
walking alongside her with a long white evening
cloak over his arm.
Florodora’s first house was even more of a crush
and a glitter than anticipated. The dress circle
was lined three deep with standers, and every
square inch of step had an occupant. The gayest-
lookmg box was the one that held the manager’s
wife and friends, and lots of flowers. Mrs. J. C.
Williamson, gowned in a magnificent pink frock,
farewelled her friends during the interval in the
green-room; she left Sydney on Sunday by train
to lorn J. O. W. in London, waiting first to see
what sort of a reception “Florodora” would get
from Sydney, and carry the news to its proprie-
. What an invaluable thing a little foreign twist
is to an actress’ lingo! Grace Palotta takes one’s
fancy by storm with her Viennese accent. Her
voice is otherwise quite harsh, almost cracked in
some places, but the accent mends it up and
carries it through brilliantly.
Her first dress is a lace gown over rose chiffon
with a rose-pink vest, and an enormous vivid pink
toque, toreador-shaped and trimmed across the
front with large roses in different shades of rose
Her serand creation is of nasturtium-yellow silk
the skirt tucked—if anything so small can be
called a tuck—downwards all over, bodice tucked
sleeves tucked, and heavy cream lace appliqued on
skirt and bodice. With this was worn a big yel-
low hat with two brims ; I presume the legiti-
mate purpose of two brims would be to get in a
double amount of trimming, and this hat succeeds
beautifully that way. It is heavily-trimmed with
orange-colored flowers, a paste buckle and velvet
folds. A little rosette of black velvet, with a dia-
in the centre, appears on the corsage
of both Miss Palotta’s gowns.
Maud Chetwynd is a distinct surprise to idyd-
ney. Last time we saw her she was dancing
nicely enough in the “Gondoliers,” but was very
much of a beginner. Now she has blossomed out
into a dashing, dainty little actress, in white mus-
lin, with a big white hat turned up off her face.
She moves with verve, but doesn’t do herself jus-
tice in a bright sky-blue frock and hat, that some-
how remind one of a Sunday-school picnic.
Clever little Carrie Moore taking-off Madame
Darto is Carrie at her best—so far. The dancing
dress is green velvet &la Darto, with roses round
the bottom; her shoes have white heels, and her
skirts parody the brilliant Darto underfluting.

The same night saw Marie Lloyd starting her
first turn at the Tivoli to a “standing-room-only”
She is very plump, has a jolly expression,
and brims over with “go.” She wears first a pink
brocade with a wide tunic composed entirely of
pink roses oyer a brief pink skirt, a smart coarse
straw hat, trimmed with pink and red roses, and
a , chlffon parasol. She thrills her points
at the house, and looks like scoring one of the
biggest successes at the Tiv. Peggy Pryde, Lottie
Coffins, Billie Barlow, Marie Lloyd, they are all
more or less alike, but the latter is more so. Her
songs abound in what she describes as Cayenne, but
her air of bonhomie, her long white teeth, and
her clear snappy voice are irresistible.
Marie Lloyd, the top star of the London music-
hahs, m her dressing-room at Sydney Tivoli. As
she takes her make-up off she talks, she effer-
-esces, she rattles, she buzzes, she never stops.
When she isn’t talking to anybody else, she talks
to herself in the glass. “ Marie-Lloyd-you-don’t-
look-a-bi t-pretty-to - day-as - you - usually-do-on-an-

Of its kind Marie Lloyd’s wardrobe is the finest
ever seen in Sydney. Her dresses are in dozens
and dozens. She was packing them steadily for
weeks,, and wonderful but true —she makes
them all herself, and trims all her own hats. “ I
find time because I don’t go out,” says Marie.
Don t like it; like sewing. Have a big work-
room at the top of my house at Hampstefd, and
when I want to go out, I go up and open all the
windows and sit down and spend the day making
dresses. My mother helps me. You might see
mother and me sometimes driving in of a night in
our brougham, stopping in desperation under the
lamplights to finish off a buttonhole in the
brougham, as we couldn’t trust to Providence for
the pins, me being such a slender little thing ”

scarlet foff a tii &1?d the B P ecial strips of
scarlet felt, the State carriage pulled up short of
the mark, and the front bunch of choice blossoms
missed aim altogether. Speaker Mason, prompt
and fussy as usual, got to the front, tip-toed along
the side of the carriage, and returned with the
bland expression of a Missionary Father at the
head of the party. Then he went along the line,
and made introductions: Lady So-and-So, ‘ Mrs.
Gillottthe Mayoress, ‘My Wife’ (where was
Court etiquette when Mr. M. introduced a lady
to the Gov.-General as ‘My Wife!’) ‘Mrs.
Leakin ’ —and so on. The Gov.-General bowed
arid smiled, and pump-handled where he could ; so
did the Countess. Only Wally smiled, and dropt
his all-seeing eyes—as he followed. The Countess
gave her most cordial hand-shake and limited-
liability smile to Mrs. Oeakin—the tall, slim wife
of the oratorical Senator—who, on account of her'
most recent acquaintance with Her Ex. in Eng-
land, and probably of her rare appearances in
Sassiety, was given a special place in the crowd.
To the rest of the smiling matrons, Her Ex. mut-
tered a staccato ‘How-de-do?’ and gave the
elevated finger-shake. There was just time to
take in the pale look of the Countess, the tired
smile, the quiet black outfit, the scarlet button-
hole, and she had passed through the floral
arches, and was sitting bowing (from the waist) in
the carriage. The mechanical bend is undoubtedly
an improvement on the Countess’s head-duck of
years ago.
“The Gov.-General was equal to the occasion,
and made himself genial. He laughed outright at
some of the old officials who cut their way through
the crowd for a hand-shake. * Thanks ! Thanks 1
1 hanks! he answered one after another of the
gushers’ greetings. Capt. Wallihgton, whose
whole attitude still suggests the lamented Girard’s
Of course you can never be like us—but be as
nke us as you’re able to be,’ found time as he
picked his way in the track of the Exes, to mur-
mur gently in the ear of President Zeal, and a few
other top-notchers—‘ Congratulations.’ The long,
eloquent nose dipping towards the shrimp-like ear
of the ancient bachelor seemed to actually pro-
nounce the word. The eternal Jenkins waved a
fat hand to Wally, and the astute pair exchanged
a friendly. smile. ‘ Let us be moral—and pro-
per, was insinuated in their telegraphic sign of
camaraderie.
. Mrs. Corbet, whose part in such proceedings
is tame for one so full of strong character-points,
caught her train from under grovelling heels, and
tui ned her large, handsome eyes with an indifferent
expression to the carriage. A fashionable air
ciowns Mrs. Corbet’s figure, and her admirable
black outfit, with its white-spotted panne collar
and large chin-frame of white tulle, made smart
frockers think of their local laurels.
“ Ex-Mayoress McEacharn, plump and golden-
haned, made one of her first public appearances
since her return, and looked her very best in sad
array, leaning back in her landau. Unless deport-
ment arid carriage are strings to a comely Person-
age s bow, she might do worse than develope
weak ankles and drive through life. Miss
McEacharn, who came from ’Ome with her
mother, was a pretty, fragile child ere she went
to school in England. If she has grown up as
expected, she should have a fair chance
ot tulftling the soaring hopes ascribed to her
ambitious father A pretty daughter with money
is an ideal foundation for parental castles in the
air.

came cdattenng along with a couple of aides in
attendance. Dress, as plain as possible : just a
black riding-habit and black jacket, with a white-
straw billycock hat, and a black band round it
Wfie was recognised by but few people.

, . is reckoned in Melbourne that Syme’s
friend Zeal is bound to be the first President of
the Senate. If he is elected he will (from a
. society ’ point of view) do the thing well. He
is rich, very hospitable, and good-natured. You
rarely see his name in a public subscription list,
yet few men are more benevolent. His fault is a
fierce temper. Years ago Burt called him in Par-
liament a ‘short little carrot’—he is red-haired,
so far as he has any hair. In the Convention,
Ceorge Reid used to bait him mercilessly. One
a |*P'y scene between them was erased from the
official report; but there is a very hot quarrel
reported in Vol. 11, p. 2408, of the Melbourne
sitting. It finished up by Zeal saying that Reid
had insulted him, to which Reid retorted, ‘You
are beneath insult,’ and Zeal added, as a parting
shot, ‘ You are a contemptible fellow.’
“Politicians’ female relatives are relieved that
the elections are over. Candidates themselves,
kept up by the excitement of the struggle, feel
the strain less than home-staying wives and
daughters, the latter seeming especially sensitive
to skits and caricatures directed at political kith
A/r 7?' Among local sufferers from this source,
Mr. Best’s two school-girl daughters palpably
wilted under what seemed to them the scorching
ferocity of their father’s press critics. Now
they re jubilant again, and, for the present, have
left off bewailing the folly of men in trying to get
into Parliament.
Since Marshall Hall and co. have burst their
bonds and gone on their own, they seem as joyous
as a flight of Shelleyan skylarks. The hymner
himself nas composed an opera (music and words)
described as joyous and Greek. His colleague
Mdme. Wiederman (Mrs. Pinschof) is arranging
an operatic entertainment in aid of the Alfred
Hospital, Hubby guaranteeing all expenses. The
Pmschofs, who recently outbid the Gallery
trustees got Mackennal’s Circe for their
mansion at Kew, are also the fortunate owners of
a delightfully picturesque chalet at Macedon.
“ According to Marshall Hall’s Herald article,
Tannhiiuser was an impulsive sort of flounderer—
seemingly not unlike the Hymner himself. Local
German women set Wagner’s Lothario on a pedes-
tal as a high-souled hero wrestling vainly with in-
herited flirtatious tendencies. Australiennes
apparently feel less lenient towards the musical
philanderer, summing him up as ‘ not too bad in
opera, but horridly squalid in real life.’ If Wag-
ner meant I anny to represent ah average ordinary
man he needn’t have libelled this poor old world
by peopling it only with such extreme female
types as Venus and Elisabeth. Life is bother-
some enough without making it out worse than it
is, as it certainly would be if all women were
either prudes or prodigals.
“How easily the illusion of the stage is
destroyed, and how careless some actors are about
the little things that go to preserve it ! In
‘ Tannhauser,’ Wolfram has several numbers to
Sing ii accompanying himself, by pretence, on a
small harp. The harp music really comes from
the orchestra, of course, and all Wolfram has to
do is to hold the small harp and move his fingers
across the strings. One night last week, all
through the opera, Max Eugene, who played
Wolfram, held his harp upside-down. When'the
instrument in the orchestra ran up the scale from
bass to treble, Eugene’s fingers ran down his
dummy harp from treble to bass. So persistently
did he do it that it looked positively perverse.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
To add to the horrors and troubles of the
war, bubonic plague is now reported among
British troops at Capetown.
Who is the “ R. Clive Teece ” who de-
fends the Kanaka traffic and otherwise cavorts so
Torily in Sydney Telegraph ? Is this A.M.P.
“Dick” Teece’s stripling son; or is it “Dick”
himself blossoming-out with a middle handle, after
the manner of J. Handle Carey ? Anyway the
name of “Teece” at the foot of a black-labor
manifesto won’t do the A.M.P. any good. Look
at all the thousands who t’other day voted for one
D. R. O’Sullivan for the Senate because they took
him for the other O’Sullivan.
