PERSONAL ITEMS.
The retirement of Lyne and Abbott from
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly will leave Mat. Harris
the sole representative of knightly nobility in that
chamber.
“Senator” is a grandiloquent title. For
instance, “ Senator Neild.” Conjure up a mental
picture of the man, and you will perceive no flies
on his newly-acquired handle.
Mile. Dolores (Trebelli) owns a set of
curiously-carved chessmen made out of the bones
of a departed chess-enthusiast, who bequeathed
his skeleton for that purpose.
Woollnough, M.L.A., the only parson in
politics thereabouts, lost his £25 deposit trying to
get a Tasmanian seat in the Senate —and yet he
was the most cocksure of all the aspirants.
Presbyterian parson Ronald, now M. H.R.
for South Melbourne, was born on Governor-
General Hopetoun’s Linlithgow estate, and some-
where about the same time as His Ex.—in 1861.
Yictorian solicitor Grey, so long absent
without being missing, has been arrested _ at
Suva, where he was running a sugar plantation.
What “sugar” he planted, if any, remains to be
found out.
How will members address one another in
the Federal Lower House? Will Kingston be
‘ ‘ the hon. member for one-seventh of South Aus-
tralia,” or King O’Malley the ditto for a fifth of
Raspberry-land ?
A fitting wind-up of the Federal-election
fight as it was conducted at an over-the-water
Sydney suburb was the spectacle of one defeated
candidate blubbering in full view of the crowd
when the poll was declared.
N.S.W. offered one Knight (Manning) for
a seat in the Senate —out; Victoria offered two
(Zeal and Sargood)—in; South Australia three
(Symon, Baker, Downer)—in; while Queensland,
W. A. and Tasmania offered none.
Veteran Dr. Morrison of the Scotch
College, Melbourne, now close on 80, has had 10
years added to his life by the return of no less
than five of his old boys to Australia’s first Par-
liament. Best in Senate, and Knox, Skene,
Winter-Cooke, and Carty Salmon in House of
Representatives, ai*e all Scotch-collegians.
King O’Malley to a persistent and beastly
personal interjector at one of his meetings: “I
can tell by the length of your nose that if all the
brains you have were scooped out, dried in the
sun, placed in a quill taken from the wing of a
little cock-sparrow, and blown into the eye of a
flea,’twouldn’t make the flea wink.” Collapse of
interjector. Almost vigorous enough, but too
cumbrous to be quite original.
'pld N.S.W. Agent-General Saul Samuel,
who was brimful of good stories, used to tell one of
“the Royal memory,” about which much-adver-
tised institution he entertained grave doubts. The
veteran knew the then Prince of
Wales, now Edward VII, rather
well (much better than any other
Australian who had not paid scores

Henry Neville Montagu, whose death was
chronicled the other day, did a mass of literary
work in his time. Probably his latest. definite en-
gagement was as dramatic and operatic critic to a
paper founded by Harold Stephen. He was a fine
linguist, and a keen critic of concert and other
music. For years he fathered Sydney Punch.
Forty years ago he was a literary leader in the
Sydney of that date.
Verily, man is a creature of circumstances.
While John Norton was absent from Sydney
during the first hearing of the Coningham case a
well-written descriptive literary sketch of the
proceedings appeared in his paper. Read be-
tween the lines, it was taken by many people to
indicate that Coningham was coming out top-
Anyhow, it didn’t please the R-.C. authori-
ties'; and, to make a long story short, John might
easily have been a Senator but for that free-and-
easy sketch-
Died, lately, at Williarnstown (Vic.), Cap-
tain H J. Draper, since ’B9 examiner of pilots for
that State. Deceased had been blind for years,
but his wonderful memory enabled him to carry
out his duties up to the day of his death. Draper
was mate of the Nelson when, in 1852, 22 ex-con-
victs stole aboard her, lying, off Williarnstown
overpowered and bound the few hands aboard, and
got away with 81530 z. of gold-dust. Most of this
was sold at 30s. an oz. to a receiver who got clear
ff to England.
The Duchess of York—another version,
rom the S.M. Herald’s London lady correspon-
dent :
The Duchess is undoubtedly a handsome woman,
worthy to be “ mother of our king to be.” Of fine height,
with a good carriage and fresh complexion, and sunny
blue eyes, she is sure to be much admired as she does the
round' of our countries across the seas. Once the Duchess
breaks through the iron ring of shyness which is her
bane and her trouble, she is a bright, clever, amusing
woman. But her shyness, people who know her well tell
me, is very trying ; it makes her awkward and difficult
with strangers.
Changes in the constitution of the Im-
perial Court of Final Appeal will involve the
merging into one Court of the House of Lords
and the Judicial Committee of the Privy Coun-
cil All the members of the new Court will be-
come Lords of Parliament. For the purpose of
adequately manning this Court eight new Law
Lords will be created—one from the Australian
Commonwealth (who?), one from Canada,
S’Africa, and India respectively. Of the remain-
ing four two will be English, one Scotch, and one
Irish.

Demise: Attorney Winfield Attenborough,
somewhere about the last of the Romans, in the
way of Melbourne’s old-school lawyers—a type
very different from the crack attorney of to-day.
The port-wine firm that survived the longest was
Taylor, Buckland, and Gates, in Geelong. If we
round up the big Melb. attorneys of the present,
such as Gillott, Maddock, and the rest, the differ-
ence in type is observable at once. Law does not
seem to go in such big gulps as formerly, when
the banks, squattages, and mines were always at
it with momentous cases ; the attorney was more
a general man of the world than he is now.
Attenborough was of the jovial sort, holding Ins
own well with Dick Ireland, Dick Goldsbrough,
and the stout old generation.
An ordinarily staid Fat, returned to the
Federal Parliament by the misguided Vic. elec-
tors, was utterly overwhelmed by his success, and
went off his dot publicly in Collins-st. when at
length triumph was assured. First he shook
hands with his friends tremulously and with much
dramatic flourish ; then, becoming more and more
dominated by emotion, he embraced all and sun-
dry round the neck : then he shook hands wildly
with anybody that offered, and large tears coursed
down his nose, and “ Gor bleshers” were freely
distributed. But the happy man’s most touching
manifestation was his lachrymose but happy
babbling of the phrase: “Oh, my Eliza will be
pleased—she will be pleased !” It was only when
the crowd made a song of “Oh, won’t Eliza be
pleased ! ” that the great man’s chums bpre him
into a pub. and braced him up with whiskies.
Superintendent Camphin, who in the
Coningham case professed to give friendly and
not police advice to Dr. O’Haran, has been 43
years in N.S.W. police. He joined the detective
branch close on 40 years ago, and has had a hand
in most stirring criminal events since. It was he
who unravelled the Parramatta River mystery,
and brought to the gallows Nicholls and Lester.
He also solved the mystery of the headless bodies
at Deniliquin. The only ignominious adventure
he relates tells how in a search for bushrangers in
the far back he was locked up as a bad character
by one of his own constables. The constable
would listen to no explanations, and treated his
prisoner’s assurance that he was Detective
Camphin as an attempt to hoax. The Sydney
authorities had to interfere before Camphin was
released, and even then the constable followed
him about with grave suspicion.
Surprise was expressed in some quarters
at the high position John Norton occupied on the
N.S.W. Senate poll. As an explanation. Duringthe
campaign a young girl called on the accountant
of a Govt, department. She alleged that she or
her family had a claim for a few pounds against
the dept, and could not get satisfaction. The
accountant explained at length the position of
the case, and with the idea of giving an object-
lesson in politeness to his listening staff, excelled
himself in attempting to arrive at a satisfactory
conclusion, winding up with a definite promise to
settle up one way or the other by “Tuesday week.”
The damsel thanked him and withdrew, but on
reaching the door she stopped, and, turning round,
drawled out in a most matter-of-fact tone, “I’ll
give you to Tuesday week, and then we 11 put
Norton on to yer.” Many people’s idea of
“Norton” is something of the panther tribe,
which roams round the Domain, and can be “ put
on” to anyone in a high or official position whose
ways they consider refractory.

Aboriginalities.
In Georgetown, N.Q., t’other day, a drover,
passing through to Herberton with a mob of
cattle, was married to a full-blooded lubra. The
lady was attired for the occasion in pants and
shirt. No church in Georgetown, and the Mining
Registrar tied the knot. White women are
scarce up there, and the lubra is very much in
evidence.
The old adage, “ Dog won’t
eat dog,” does not apply to the Australian dingo.
As an instance, the case of three dingo pups,
which, upon being taken from their mother’s lair,
were placed for the night with the pups of a tame
slut. In the morning, the dingo pups had eaten
the head off one tame pup, and the legs off two of
the others.

After pouring a little of the liquor on the counter

“Jack” : Re “ Dunnarg ” oil the wild
state of some back-block families (8., 2/3/’l). I
once knew of a family on the Macquarie, about
half-a day’s tramp from Hill End, who were all
(bar the old man) as wild :is warrigals. Lived by
getting a little alluvial gold, and the old man took
tlie bit of “dust” to Hill End monthly to ex-
change for rations. On one of these visits he told
the local publican that he would bring a couple of
his daughters up and have them “ broken-iu to
service.” Next trip the old man started with the
daughters, but when they caught sight of the
town they bolted. Some time afterwards lie did
manage to get one up to town, strapped-down,
in a cart ; and she was eventually tamed. Many
such families can be found even now in N.S.W.
If you want any information from them you’ve
got to run them down or log them in their huts.
And yet we send missionaries to China !

“ Louis M.” : The dance discoursed on by
“ Alick ” (B. 2/3/’01) was indeed an exceptional
one. Bush “hops,” as a rule, are lamentably
“proper” ([ refer to those occurring in back-
block townships). There are generally about six
different “sets” represented —and intermixing,
even at charity balls, is rarely indulged in. All
present appear oppressed with the responsibility
of maintaining the barrier. Should Mr. A dance
with Miss B, Miss C will decline him as a partner
for the rest of the evening. Should he then stand
“ALAS, MY POOR BROTHER !”
A Westralian version.- With apologies to “ Bovril.”
up with Miss D, his “cake is. dough” as regards
both Miss B and Miss C, and; so, on. Before en-
tering a bush ballroom one needs just as much
coaching as for a swell soiree. Sometimes, how-
ever, etiquette is rather lax. An instance : A
gent (lately up) noticed a young lady performing
the “ Duke of Argyle ” wriggle against a con-
venient post, and allowed his eye to rest on the
fair contortionist longer than necessary ; where-
upon the lady, with asperity—“Yer needn’t look,
it’s only ther prickly ’eat ? ”

“A.C.H.” : Be cat sagacity (8., 29/12/’01).
A family which lived on the west side of Port
Augusta (separated from the town by the Gulf—
at that place from quarter to half-mile wide) had
a cat which they wished to place in another home.
So one evening puss was put in a basket, rowed
across to the township, and left in a friend’s
backyard. An hour or so was spent in the Port,
and when home was reached, the cat was sitting
in the kitchen carefully drying herself. Have
often heard of cats swimming out of water, but
never before of one voluntarily entering it.
“ D. 8.” : The high ground in
Peak Hill (N.S.W.) district is, during
the .summer months, dotted with
small heaps of clean seeds —about a
handful in each heap—laboriously
collected by a slow-moving black ant,
whose nest-entrance can be found a
few inches away. The closest obser-
vation fails to disclose the object of
so much industry, as the seed simply
lies there untouched by the ant, to
be either eaten by birds or blown or
washed away by the first storm. A
notable instance of objectless
“ thrift.”
* * *
Just before last shearing a
well-known N.S.W. squatter engaged
a new bookkeeper, and seemed much
afraid that the latter did not know
enough to charge the shearers- a
remunerative price for stores. So
the boss kept popping his hoary old
head in at the door of the store now
and then with such instructions as
“Charge 2 bob a pound for tea,”
“Sixpence a pound for sugar,” &c.,
&c. But he reached the climax with
“Don’t sell ’em any stamps. Can’t
charge any profit on those.”

“E.H.M.” : In a recent Scien-
tific American a writer—referring to the wanton
destruction of animal life —states that the ’possum
feeds extensively on “grubs, beetles, and other
insects.” Can any of your readers verify this? I
never heard of it. [The reference was probably
to the American, or true, opossum (Didelphys) a
creature differing considerably in habits from the
Australian ’possum.]
* * * *
“ Spinifex ” to The Bulletin, >from Perth
(W.A.) ;
The lately-murdered J. J. Durack did -mat belong to the
firm of Connor, Doherty, and Durack, of Fremantle. He
was, however, related to a member thereof. Durack
knew well that the blacks had a grudge against him—as
indeed the E. Kimberley niggers have against all white
men, especially squatters, who, they say, are taking their
country from them. Durack never travelled unarmed,
and had loaded firearms alongside of him when murdered.
He was a typical bushman of the pioneer stamp, and the
blacks on his stations were better treated than on any
others up that way. The blacks have for years past been
wantonly spearing hundreds of cattle and horses in E.
Kimberley country, and the Duracks have been great
losers. Ask any half-civilised nigger why he spears cattle
and he will mostly reply, “ Squatter take my country.”

“Peter K.” : Be cat-intelligence. I once
knew in Lilydale district (Vic.) a large tortoise-
shell Tom which would follow at heel and
fetch-and-carry like a dog. Also would retrieve
small birds or young rabbits shot by his owner.
Had other more common accomplishments, such
as “begging” and turning somersaults at word
of command.
* * • .
Another touching story of the affectionate
and faithful dog. Toby was bought a month or
two back by a South Melbourne man who was a
martyr to the attacks of a local push, and desired
to have a companion-in arms when he sauntered
abroad after nightfall. The dog was sold with a
certificate of character and a guarantee, and his
new owner spent three weeks winning his canine
affections, and attaching him to his person
by bonds of self-interest and sympathy.
Then he started walking the animal out, and
on the fifth night three of “the Flying Angels’
swooped on him in a dark place, and began kick-
ing his socks off. This.was Toby’s opportunity,
and he laid back his ears and dug in ; taking a
mouthful of the nearest leg, he held on and
tugged, worrying with all his energy, quite re-
gardless of the screams of the victim. And when
the pushites had fled, and the police drew nigh,
the anguished victim discovered, to his great
mortification, that it was himself the faithful
animal had got hold of and was chewing.

“Via”: Be Mandevilles “long-apple”
(B. 12/1/01)). Doesn’t this fit in better with the
custard-apple of the tropics and its full brother
the Delicious Monster ? ( Anona reticulata). This

Cross.
* * ♦ *
Maori pahs in the North Island are being
gradually stripped of their native curios, the
owners giving them away or selling them at a
price far below their value to travelling pot-
hunters who ingratiate themselves with the brown
people for the special purpose of making such
deals. Gov. Ranfurlv, who already has a fair
swag of curios., was recently, at Tokaanu, given
two very ancient whalebone meres , a number of
pieces of greenstone, a large stone axe which
eight generations ago belonged to a celebrated
tohunga, and a famous stone chisel which was
long ago used to build a noted house called Hau-
hauterangi. The Duke of York will find the
Maori region the most interesting part of his
tour, and will get cartloads of almost priceless
articles laid at his royal feet by natives.
* * * *
A snake-and-man story from Lilydale,
Vic. The man was porter at the local station,
and it was part of his daily duties to attend to the
lever for working the points, situated about 50yds.
from the platform. The end of the lever was
boxed-in in the usual way, and sometimes the
workings got clogged with dust. Putting his hand
in to clear away this dust on a warm afternoon
the porter was bitten on the finger by a black
snake, which escaped after striking. The porter’s
life was saved with difficulty, and in a week or
two he went back to work again. A few months
later he put his hand into the same box exactly as
on the former occasion, and was bitten again,
dragging out the snake hooked to his finger this
time. That porter is still living, so is the snake,
but the former had so narrow a squeak that he
now makes it a rule to dust the points with a
stick. Live and learn !
The*re has been a curious controversy in
London Daily Chronicle and the Athenaeum re
the orgin of the name of Bendigo. The Chronicle
tells the old story that the place was called after
a prize-fighter, but the other paper holds to the
bandicoot theory. The AthkN/EUM comments
“The most interesting fact about the place is that
when it grew respectable, it became ashamed of
its name, and changed it; and lately, thinking
that the earlier name was, after all, racy of Aus-
tralian life, deliberately reverted to it.” Curiously
enough, there are no members for Bendigo in Vic.
Parliament. The representatives of the place are
called members for “Sandhurst.”

“ J.A.C.” : Met about a dozen Kanakas
the other morning near Yeppoon (Q.). The usual
way is just to say “ Hullo,” and pass on, but this
time I was greeted with “Goo’ morning mistah
C , what you say, it stop holiday to-day.”
“Yes,” I answered; “it looks like it by your
clothes.” They were dressed up tip-top—starchers
and loud ties, black suits of clothes and shining
boots. “Oh yeh, nice clothes, my word,”
smilingly returned one dusky toff. “To-day we
be Australyan larrah-kin.”

“A.H.C.”: Once had a bull-terrier that
was a terror for killing poultry. Also had a fine
collection of pigeons, and one morning found the
lot missing. Couldn’t learn anything about them
although the local bobby wasted much of his
valuable time pursuing clues. Some days later,
however, I was idly watching the dog rooting in
a potato-patch, and presently saw him unearth a
pigeon. Investigated and found them all—buried
at intervals over the patch.
Scene : A woolshed in the N.YV. Shear-
ing stragglers. Enter newchum manager just up
from the South. Manager, to one of the shearers :
“Look here, my man, you are leaving half the
wool on. Shear that sheep again ! ” Shearer :
“Which blooming sheep?” Manager, after pul-
ling sheep out of pen : “ This one—see the wool
you have left on!” Shearer: “Why, that’s
one that was shore three months ago at general
shearing.” Exit manager disgusted.
* * * *
A Capetown paper tells how certain Aus-
tralian shearers, who were watching Kaffirs take
the wool off sheep, were so disgusted at the way
the heathen hacked off the wool in bits and
stowed them in a bag, that they collared the
shears, took off the wool in workmanlike fashion,
and presented a decent fleece to the owner.
Probably sheepowners in S’Africa are firmly of
opinion that white men can’t shear sheep, and
that the handling of wool is an employment only
fitted for Kanak—we mean Kaffirs.

A M.L. poet was so indignant on hearing
of the proposal to change the old Dutch-sounding
name of “ New Zealand,” that he called as fol-
lows on the spirits of the dead contingenters to
rise and resist the outrage
New Zealand ! By thy sons inurned,
And wreathed in fiery flame.
O, never by thy sons be spurned
The glamour of thy name !
“ Wreathed in fiery flame.” He is the first Jingo
poet to suggest that the departed Boer-slayers are
in Sheol.
* * * *
A couple of Hindoos recently leased a
disused produce store in a N.S. W. northern town,
and started therein an ice-cream and confec-
tionery factory. The building has only one room,
30 x 15, which serves as factory, store-room, cart-
shed, stable, kitchen, and dwelling for the manu-
facturers and up to a dozen lodgers, the horse
being kept to his corner by means of a few boards.
Yet the stuff made in this den finds ready sale.
* * * *
Drought-stricken Central Queensland ap-
parently means to grow prodigies of a unique
order in place of grass. T’other day, during an
insolvency inquiry, a lad of 15 was sworn to as the
landlord and proprietor of a hotel and store.
He had earned the money in a casual way in his
spare time, and all since leaving school.
EVOLUTION.
Iletmdg'ed behind the plough and steered the share
Through ample fields of rich, red, loamy soil,
Or reaped with shimmering blade the crops it bare,
And carted to the mart his fruits of toil,
i ear in and out his day was drear and long,
tt i- S u was his couch nofc one of down ;
lie lightened labor’s burdens with his song*,
And craved not Fortune’s smile nor feared heFfrown.
Begrimed he fought upon the sodden plain,
In tatters clad, his heart and body sore ;
He heard the shrieks of dying, saw the slain,
And learned the bloody horrors of a war.
Where harvests once had thrived and homesteads smiled,
Where full contentment reigned a year ago,
An army through the smoking ruins filed,
And left a hapless people to their woe.
And now, on his return from fields afar—
His kharki outfit all the worse for wear—
He tells his tales of prowess in the bar,
And lubricates while stupid yokels stare.
But nevermore returns he to the farm,
To till and sow and reap and garner in.
For Peace’s vict’ries have no farther charm
For one whose ears have heard the battle’s din !
“F.”, in WINDSOR (N.S.W.) GAZETTE.

“ Alohar ” ■ We had been down the
river fishing—four of us in a boat—and when
within 30 yds. of home, in the dusk of the evening
m noticed something (all from thewharf whore
some youngsters were playing. Pulled over ana
girl sinking for the second time. Every-,
body except Smith, who was hideously and won-
derfully drunk, grabbed her and pulied her out
While we were doing it Smit.i fell in. The s
of Smith has been already mentioned. He was a
heavy man, and the toil we had in fishing Smith
ont was like unto the numerous labours (I forget
ho v many) of the mythological person whose
name I can’t remember. Brown carried home the
little girl who wasn’t much the worse. Johnson
and I laboured up the street
dragging a great mass of
soaked, drunk, limp, help-
less, unpleasant Smith.
Never in all my life did I see
so much water run off
man —or even six men. Near
home we met Smith s em-
ployer, who indicated the
streaming object with the
end of his walking stick, and
asked in a contemptuous tone
what that might be. To save
Smith’s billet we had to say
that, he was suffering from
prolonged immersion, in an
effort to save a fellow-crea-
ture. Smith has the Humane
Society’s medal now, and has
been mentioned in the
papers, and thanked and
presented with a purse of
sovereigns by the mayor at
a crowded public meeting.
Also he is looked on kindly
by a nice girl with property
—the elder sister of the
child we rescued. And as
the result of one ill-con-
sidered and well-meant pre-
varication we have no medal,
no purse of sovereigns, no
fame, no public meeting, no
nice girl—nothing but the
damage done to our clothes
where Smith dripped on us.
We haven t even the satis-
faction of knowing that
Smith is aware that he is an
impostor, for he really thinks
he did it. Language fails
me. It also fails Brown.
Certainly it hasn’t failed
Johnson yet, but even he is
likely to run short of adjec-
tives soon.

Mackennal the Australian.
Sundry people and personages—Mrs., Cora Potter
among them—have been credited with the “dis-
covery” of Bertram Mackennal. Obviously the
paragraphists in these cases didn’t know the
sculptor they were talking about. He belongs to
the order of geniuses who start by discovering
themselves, and keep themselves well in view for
ever afterwards. Mackennal has the strong
artistic brain in the hard, business head, whereon
Tweedlepunch, the phrenologist, would probably
find the bump of destructiveness sticking out like
E. BERTRAM MACKENNAL , the Australian Sculptor.
Photo by Humphrey & Co., Melbourne.
a watermelon. One thinks of all sculptors as
destructive image-makers, though, in truth, it is
not they, the artists, who attack blocks of marble
with mallet and chisel. And one feels that this
sculptor, anyhow, is exceptionally virile.
The eye which looks after Mackennal’s interests
is keen and hard, the mouth firm and straight.
Somehow the clean-shaven face suggests a fighting
curate, and yet “young” rapid Mackennal is a
pleasant reminder of his late father, who was the
gentlest of gentlemanly modellers in clay. The
creator of “Oirce” is pure Scottish-Austrahan.
His mother, a shrewd lassie, came frae the land o’
cakes ; his father had a Scotch father who went
through the family estate with a most un-Cale-
donian carelessness and was an artist in naught
but spending money. The practical, brainy
bohemianism of Bertram Mackennal is explained.
He was only 18 when he got away from Mel-
bourne to London. Entered himself as a student
at the Royal. Academy, and presently decided
that Paris would suit him better. At 20 he had
the audacity to open a studio, though still a mere
struggler in the school of adversity. Mackennal
doesn’t talk much about his early career, but he
appears to have mentioned casually that he owes
a debt of gratitude to a trustful restaurant-keeper,
and has eaten at tables where the chinaware—
plates, dishes, and things—were the work of his
own hands.
Happy is life in Paris when the world is young
and Fame is understood to be always hovering
near with a laurel wreath waiting for a convenient
opportunity to crown her elect, and every aspirant
is of the elect. London offers a larger assortment
of attractions. All good Americans |nowadays
go to London when they die, whereas they for-
merly sought the banks of tb'j Seine. London has
a community of 250 artists, one kind or other, at
St. John’s Wood ; altogether things are less insu-
lar than of yore. But for the young bohemian
the charm of Paris remains unrivalled. One
can drop into a cafe, say the Cafe Versailles,
and always find interesting fellows at the
tables, men for the hero-worshippers to silently
adore—such as Rodin, the great sculptor, in his
working blouse ; Francois Coppee, the poet, writ-
ing, with his glass of absinthe in front of him ;
Dubois, the medallist—anybody and everybody.
Mackennal couldn’t have been more than 22
when he met his matrimonial fate. She was an
English girl, student in Paris, and he was an
Australian boy, so they agreed that long engage-
ments were a mistake. When Longstaff came
along on his travelling scholarship (that was in the
eighties) he found his compatriot, the sculptor, to
be a husband and a father—father of an only child
who, by the way, is now a tall girl with a well-
grown talent for drawing and decorative design-
ing.
In ’BB, or thereabouts, Mackennal returned to
Melbourne. Those were the piping times of
bank overdrafts, and at the Cafe Anglais in
Collins-street —a feeding-place which rose and fell
with the Boom—one used to meet old Macken-
nal’s son oftimes. His studio was in Swanston-
street. He didn’t benefit by the Boom. The
busts that men went in for at that period were
not of marble, or bronze, but booze. He sculped
a pair of panels for the front of Parlia-
ment House, and got a few small commis-
sions. It seemed he had made a mistake
in bringing his talent to Australia. I hen
Bernhardt struck Melbourne for a month,
and the Divine Sarah, who dabbles m clay,
herself, wanted to know what ze gifted Mac-ken-
nal meant by wasting his time on this desert land,
Mon Dim ! Why did he not fly back to the home
of Art ? The trouble was to get feathers to fly
with. Frank Stuart, and a few other admirers at
his instigation, provided the necessary plumes
(witness the charming bronze statuette ot irutn,
which the sculptor presented to them some years
later) and early in ’92 Mackennal was modelling
his famous “Circe” in the studio next to Jack
Longstaff, who then had his “ Sirens picture on
the easel. Australia was about to get known to
Paris through these two works.
Mackennal was advised by Bernhardt to assert
his nationality toujours. “Be the Australian
said she, “There are many English sculptors, but
Australia has only one.” And she talked to her
Australian friend of the value of the personal
paragraph which people read m the newspapers
how much more interesting it is than the huge
poster which stares at them from the street
hoardings ! , , ,
Circe was exhibited at the Royal Academy and
the Baris Salon. Salon critics pronounced it
Great—an Inspiration. The modelling of the
statue was not more admired than the pedestal,
so notable for its clever frontal figure of womanly
despair. It is hard to believe that the same
plaster-bronze Circe which caught Pans an
Brussels, and subsequently went the round ot
a dozen or two of British cities-the Circe now on
view at Melb. Art Gallery—did actually make its
first appearance in London with a cloth over that
same pedestal. The Royal Academy suffered
from doubts concerning
the moral character of the
bas-relief work. The com-
mittee thought it better
not to expose the public
to the possible risk of an
impure ‘impression, con-
sequently there were
some who gathered around
Circe and feasted their
imagination on the sup-
posed lewdnessof the cold,
chaste work they couldn’t
see. The sculptor got a
valuable advertisement
from those managerial
scruples. The personal
paragraph is all the better
for a little mystery, espe-
cially if the mystery is
spiced.
Whence came the story of how the Australian
met Queen Victoria in a corridor in Windsor
Castle, or somewhere, and was astounded at the
dignity of her deportment, the silvery quality of
her voice, the splendor of her artistic sympathies.
&c., See. ? The narrative represented him as
having been summoned by royal command to
submit a model of a statue for the approval of the
Sovereign, who approved in such brilliantly cr’+i
cal terms that be thought he must have told her
beforehand exactly what a critic ought to say.
Mackennal never saw the august Lady m a cor-
ridor, or listened whilst she soliloquised. Cer-
tainly he was required to forward, in the ordinary
way, photos, of the Queen’s statue for India,
and the more recent one for Ballarat, to an
officer of the Household, as per official regulation.
A public statue of the monarch must be duly
passed at Court, to prevent the possibility of a
ludicrous “ duffer ” work inciting ridicule against
the figurehead of the State.
Circe has become tiie property of Mr. Pinschof
(husband of Madame Wiedermann), who outbid
the Melb. Gal Ary trustees by planking down
£lOOO for a bronze reproduction of the model.
The present plaster-bronze goes to London to be
re-cast in metal under the sculptor’s at
a cost to himself of some £3OO or so. Herr Pin-
schof has a fine room, 80ft. long, where the
Sorceress can stretch out her arms to the best
possible advantage, which fact helped in inducing
the artist to sell to the private person rather than
the public Gallery. Besides, the importunate
private customer had previously written out a
cheque for all the smaller bronzes Mackennal was
showing. No to that Melbourne’s one enterprising
patron of art just now is Pinschof, the Austrian.
Under the circumstances it was only a fair thing
for the sculptor to make his voyage out and home
by the German boat—Fredrich der Grosse. Why
does Mackennal, the Britisher—for every Austra-
lian who lives much among foreigners is forced
into Britishness by racial feeling—why does he
patronise the German line and yet look for con-
solation in the evidences of British workmanship
marked upon steel girders, patent portholes, and
what not? Because, he says, the Friedrich der
Grosse is managed as a floating hotel, with all
due regard for the boarders.
Mackennal will probably pay another visit to
Melbourne next year. The Clarke statue is to be
erected next year, and. well the Queen V ictoria
memorial is not a commission as yet. Meanwhile
he has work waiting for him in London. All sculp-
tors of any reputation have their hands full just
now. Twenty years ago they were nowhere ; now
they are everywhere. Twenty years ago the old-
fashioned houses of the wealthy were hung with
pictures in nearly all the rooms—pictures two and
three deep, placed frame against frame for the
full length of the walls. But the architect came
along (Oscar Wilde hastened the architects
blessed appearance on the scene) to reconstruct
old houses inside, or build new ones altogether, on
a plan that created a necessity for sculptors work
whilst diminishing the market for easel paintings.
The modern oak-panelled dining-room may have
one painting, let into the wall over the fire-
place The marbled hall has none. Pictures
are shown only in the gallery—few town houses
have a gallerv -the library or the smoking-room.
Sculptor’s work is wanted in all directions. The
up-to-date door-handle even is a work of art.
Costly metal furnishings require strong designs,
and manufacturers, of course, have awakened to
the fact that the difference in cost between a first-
class design and a bad one is not worth consider-
ing when the reproduction on a wholesale scale is
the same in both cases. Painters look glum, the
. new century delights them not. But the sculptors
wear orchids in their buttonholes —occasionally—
and carol tra-la-la all the time.

One of the Cunards, when asked why their

“ Coronclla ” : The Vic. Govt, granted
£6OOO to Melbourne Working Men’s College, for
the building of new workshops, to enable the col-
lege to start new trade-classes. Now that the
workshops are built-they were finished some
months ago—the Treasurer can t find monej to
fit them up, so they stand empty and useless
whilst hundreds of students await the opening.
Meanwhile, Premier Peacook is fitting up a palace
for the Duke of York.
Dear Bulletin.— The statement (8.,
2/3/’01) that no Anglican parsons have opposed
the Boer war is not quite correct. In Maoriland,
no one protested against it more vigorously from
thebeginning than O’Brien Hoare, the most original
and, next to Bishop Julius, the cleverest Anglican
parson in M.L. But as no clergy have less influ-
ence —even with their flocks—than the Anglicans,
their views about the war, or anything else, don t
count much. —Pokaki.

BERTRAM MACKENNAL’S “ CIRCE."
Photo, by Henry Dixon & Son, London.
S.A. has been lately in the midst of its
grape season, and in several districts the crop has
panned out better than any since 1887 ; besides
which prices are good, ranging from £3 15s. to
£7 10s. per ton. The principal vineyards are
on the slopes of the hills south and east of Ade-
laide ; hundreds of acres are being every year
added to the land thus used. One grower, who
buys about as much as he grows, has just brought
up his fermenting tanks to 35,000-gallon capacity,
and lie expects to make from 100,000 to 120,000
gallons of wine this season. There are at least
three others who will make as much. A labora-
tory has now become part of every decently-
equipped vineyard.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has,
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

The Difficulty of Teaching.
Everyone engaged in teaching is aware of the-
difference there is in the capacity of pupils to ac-
quire knowledge. There is the bright child whom
it is a pleasure to teach, and the child who, no-
matter what pains are taken with him, seems to
know as much to-day as he knew yesterday, and
not one iota more. Adults are constituted in just
the same way. There is the sharp man who takes
notioe of everything and stores the information
for future use, and there is the dull man who goes
through life apparently learning nothing. ' In
fact, some people never learn. In twenty-one years
the proprietors of Warner’s Safe Cure have spent
hundreds of thousands of pounds in proclaiming
the fact that there is no necessity for anyone to
suffer from Indigestion, Dyspepsia, Blood Dis-
orders, Rheumatism, Biliousness, Anemia
Gravel, Gout, Neuralgia, Debility, or even the
dread Bright’s disease, as these diseases, one and
all, arise from a disordered condition of the
kidneys or liver, or both, and Warner’s Safe
Cure will, in every case, restore the diseased
liver and kidneys to health. Therefore a person
who continues to suffer from any of the com-
plaints enumerated either does so from choice or
belongs to the large body of people who will not
learn. Here is a letter, however, from one who did
learn, and we print it, hoping it may instruct
others :
“Balmoral House, Frankston, Vic., 2nd Feb-
ruary, 1900.—1 have much pleasure in giving my
experience of Warner’s Safe Cure. I was a
sufferer from the kidneys for over two years un-
til I tried Warner’s Safe Cure, which effectually
cured me. I can recommend it to all sufferers
fronffkidney complaints. (Signed) Edwin White
Manager, Gas Works, Frankston.”*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The canker of militarism. Premier Seddon
now has an artilleryman sentry outside his house
at Wellington. Has any Australian Premier yet
gone so far as this ?
It must, indeed, gall the Jingoes to see
how many openly-declared opponents of the
S’African war have been returned to the Federal
Legislature. In Queensland and Victoria the fact
is conspicuously noticeable. ••
“ Wait for Queensland,” Sydney Tele-
graph demanded, “the natural ally of N.S.W.”
We waited accordingly, and Q. has loudly de-
clared in favor of the party which the Tele-
graph declares is to be the ruin of N.S.W.
Simon Fraser’s position oil the Victorian
Senate poll is partially accounted for by
the fact that he is Vic. Boss Orangeman. In
Brunswick, where the Y.P. barks very loudly, he
polled the highest number of votes yet recorded in
a local election.
Premier Barton expresses doubt as to
whether it is necessary to have both a Sergeant-
at-Arms and an Usher of the Rod in the Federal
House. Members should easily push him into a
negative. Use, not show, should be the reason
for employing men. And Black Rod is not even
a show.
The democracy is slow to learn. Zeal and
Fraser, the personal pals whom David Syme has
just been allowed to pitchfork into the Senate,
are the same two politically-bigoted nincom-
poops whom he shoved into the Federal Con-
vention. And* what an exhibition they made of
themselves there!
London Daily News, which lately left the
ranks of papers which denounce the Boer Republics,
prints an auditor’s certificate, showing a most sub-
stantial increase in circulation since the change of
policy. An effective answer, among thousands of
others, to those who pretend the English feeling
is all on the war side.
The Maoriland Contingenteering Bill is
expected to amount to about a quarter of a
million. The loss of men is being felt much more
than the expenditure of the money, most of which
has stayed in the country. Public works are in
some places suspended, simply because the neces-
sary men cannot be found to carry them on.
SYME’S VICTORY!
David gets a knock or two, but comes up smiling
on the general ticket.
Mr. “Jim” M‘Glowen, who, beaten for
the South Sydney Federal seat, will still sit in
N.S.'W. Assembly, has during his nine years Par-
liamentary life been claimed as a supporter by
Parkes, Dibbs, Reid, and Lyne. Premier See
now claims him as “a loyal supporter.” “ Jim ”
says that it is Premiers who tender him loyal
support.
Melbourne pressmen characterise the ac-
commodation for the craft at Parliament House
as disgraceful, even vile, in contradistinction to
that provided for members themselves. Just so !
And it is quite in accordance witli the treatment
meted out by the Daily Suppress to legislators
when they don’t happen to belong to their own
particular party.
Ex-Speaker Abbott, whose health shows
no sign o f permanent improvement, has intimated
to his Wentworth constituents that he will cease
esent af , ter the coming dissolution,
hii i . S’ <- lfc IS , Surely an absurd arrangement to
S’™ a electorate of Wentworth at one
lie W - and a Federal electorate of
>» entuoith at the other extreme.
Either many of the Senate votes in the
mnpr^elections were wrongly counted, or invalid
nhS '; ve been fitted. In four of the States
plumping was prohibited, but dissection of the
returns shows that it would require a Reid-like
mathematician to make the totals for manv elec-
n dlv if lble L by six; e.g. (in Victoria
Delatite Caron S ° Uth ’ Ilichmond - Kyneton,
N.S.W. Premier See doesn’t worship Jns-
tice Minister Wood as ex-Premier Lyne did. So
\ ood will likely not be included in his recon-
wi™°w d Ministry. Which fact explains
” /, Vood tried to sacrifice his State portfolio on
the altar of a £4OO-a-year seat in of
Reps. His opponent, Austin Chapman, is the ideal
roads-and-bridges member and general commis-
sion agent, and beat the Minister by miles.
Kingston is never ungrateful. S.A.
National Ass. dropped Symon because he sup-
ported Kingston over the memorable Privy
Oouncil dispute, whereupon Kingston secured the
eminent counsel’s inclusion in the Labor ticket, a
coup which gained Symon thousands of votes. The
latter was, therefore, in the happy position of re-
ceiving all-round support, and headed the Senate
poll. S.A. National Ass. hasn’t had such a knock
tor years.
Baden-Powell, Alfred Milner, and pushful
Joe Chamberlain, after a lot of clandestine nego-
tiations to have their recruiting agents let loose
in Australia, ruefully back down. There is a
limit even to fulsome loyalty. If the Boers are
to be policed, let Bull and his syndicates do it.
lo their disgrace the Queensland and Tasmanian
Govts. assented to the Imperial scheme, but the
objections of the other Govts, were so strong that
the flunkey States will not be troubled.
A curiosity among election returns was
supplied by White Cliffs, the opal mining camp
in far-west N.S.W. The “gougers” voted solid
tor the two Labor candidates for the Senate ; their
other four votes they bestowed with charming
impartiality among Brown and Fealy and Flynn
and all the other impossibles. And so the figures
ran like this!—Macdonell, 326 ; Smith, 301);
S O , 51; Fealy, 57; Moriarty, 74; and so on.
<<u ’ 7 Connor got 71, though the others in the
bunches ” were nowhere.
A favorite argument with the N.S.W.
anti-Federalists of two years ago was that undei
1 ederation the Mother State would lose her penny
postage within the metropolitan and other favored
areas in order to keep her in line with Victoria.
This silly prophecy was repeated by some of the
candidates in the recent campaign. Yet one of
the last acts of the Turner Government was to
decree that on Ist April, 1901. penny-postage was
to be introduced throughout the State, thus giv-
ing to Victoria a more liberal postal tariff than
N.S.W.
The Commonwealth elections in Tasmania
brought out some lovely specimens of
splenetic parochialism. For instance,
Mines-Mimster Mulcahy, who ought to
know much better, speaking in support of
M.L.C. Piesse, on the eve of polling-day,
referred to one of the candidates as “an
adventurer and political mountebank from
the mainland.” The Mercury’s report
of the petty Minister’s concluding sen-
tence: “ Surely we can select eleven men
of our own breed and generation to repre-
sent us.” Let it be noted to Tasmania’s
credit that the audience promptly resented
Mulcahy’s sentiments, and cheered for the
“ adventurer and mountebank” all it knew.
And next day King O’Malley nearly topped
the poll, running seoond to Braddou, and
beating Mulcahy’s pet candidate “of our
own breed and generation,” out of sight.
The ancient fossils of the Flyspeck
have at last got a shock, and are going about
dazed. Keating and Clemons, two young-
sters from Launceston, ran first and second
for the six Senate seats, beating Dobson
disastrously. D. O’Keefe, ex-journalist
and publican, who got the West Coast
Labor-vote, also beat Dobson. The latter
has. to b 6 contend with fifth place, the
military Cameron being ahead of him. The
sixth seat is being battled for by ex-
Attorney-General Urquhart and a new
man ca’ed Macfarlane, with the odds in
favor of the latter. So Dobson is the only
old-time politician of Tassie’s six senators.
Nothing so dreadful has happened in Tas-
mania since Adye Douglas was a very small
boy.
About S.A.’s Commonwealth legislators :
Of S.A.’s seven representatives four —Bonython, Kings-
ton, Batchelor, and Poynton—are Protectionists, and the
balance—Glynn, Solomon, and Holder—Revenue Tariffists,
with a Protectionist reservation for Holder. The Sena-
tors are equally divided, Symon, Baker, and Charleston
being- Freetraders, and Playford, Downer, and M‘Gregor
ranging as strong Protectionists. Of the delegation five
are lawyers, three of them being in the Senate. Bony-
thon is the only one who is not an old politician. So far
as democratic legislation is concerned, eight of the 13 are
certain to vote with the people on all occasions, but
Baker, Solomon, Downer, and Glynn are hopelessly
Tory.
The average Vic. elector apparently yields
with equal readiness whether he be assiduously
seduced or disdainfully flouted. Simon Fraser
advertised lavishly and addressed electors all over
the State; ffm. Zeal advertised but meagrely
and didn’t trouble to hold a single meeting. They
PRESIDENT ZEAL, of Vic. Leg. Council,
the political troglodyte who, without having held
a meeting, was sent into the Federal Senate by a
democracy grateful for past indignities.
came in first and second on tins Senate poll.
Johnny Wallace followed Zeal’s load, but hadn’t
the luck to be included in Byrne’s batch. Purves
neither spoke nor advertised —just stuck his name
on the ballot-paper and asked to be returned “ on
his face and 17,000 of the free and independent
actually acceded to his demand.
They do say that W. C. Goddard, had he
stayed in Sydney and refrained from visiting the
Hume at all, might have beaten Lyne. It is
stated that at one big meeting, addressed by the
N.S.W. ex-Premjer’s opponent, the audience,
though much bored, listened with the patience
characteristic of country-hearers. Presently, during
a pause in the Goddardian address, and when
things were so quiet that you could have heard an
elephant drop, an old cocky, with a deep bass
voice, ejaculated out 10-ud, but apparently for his
own sole benefit: “ Well, lam d—d ! Long
Bill’s got cmother soft snap ! Talk about luck ! ”
Sydney Daily Telegraph, remarking on
the mental calibre of those returned to the Federal
Parliament, concludes that S.A. has the best lot
in the Senate, and N.S.W. in the Lower House.
For the latter State, the D.T. selects as “strong
men” Barton, Lyne, Bruce Smith, M’Millan,
Reid and O’Connor—the last-named being ap-
parently the only first-ranker in the N.S.W.
Senate representation. Which, considering that
the remaining five Senators are the Telegraph’s
own choice, and were rammed down the people’s
throats for weeks as saviours of their country, is
pretty rough on the Great N.S.W. Freetrade
party.
To the Labor Party in S. Australia the
most riling thing about the election results was
not that it got only one of the two seats in each
House that it tried for, but that places were found
for the two ex-members of the party whom the
National Ass. supported. The National Ass.
doesn’t really love either of these two, Charleston
and Poynton ; but it did want to be able to sneer
at the party’s impotence, and it succeeded fairly
well. (Also, by the way, it badly wants Charles-
ton’s Leg. Council seat —and it has a man already
selected to run for it.) But by Symon’s topping
of the poll the Ass. got a bad kick itself; for it
had left its old idol’s name off the list.
Nowhere has the force of one-man-one-vote
been felt more than in Tasmania. The fossils had
no idea the Commonwealth Constitution would
cause such an upheaval; yet they ought to have
known it was loaded. Five new men, mostly
young and vigorous, have slipped into the Senate ;
and for the House of Reps, such unfashionable
persons as King O’Malley and Norman Cameron
have the pleasure of seeing their surplus votes
counted for salvation to the fallen Fysh, the
plaintive Piesse, and the Hawful Hartnoll.
Braddon topped the poll, though at one stage of
the first count O’Malley was leading. Truly
the old order changeth. The result shows a
vast increase in Tas. democratic sentiment.
Cabled that when Judas Chamberlain was
asked from “influential colonial quarters” to
have the Jookoyork made “Prince of Austra-
lia,” he replied that “if titular dignities are con-
ferred on the colonies, Canada has the first clami.
Titular dignities on the cawlinies? Why, the
t. d. would be conferred upon York ! Brumma-
gem screwmaker’s “ front” is indeed stupendous.
If it comes to claims, what is Canada’s claim on
England compared to Australia’s? Which sent
most men to S’Africa, and which could best spare
them? Fact is, that Judas is sure of Australia,
but Canada is an uncertain factor in the Empire
game, and needs tender handling. Canada must
be placated with baubles. Australia may be
safely snubbed. That’s all 1
The Sydney Herald and Telegraph have
sized up the result of the Federal elections, esti-
mating that of 111 members 59 are Protectionists
and 52 are Freetraders—quite regardless of the
fact that in three of the States the fiscal question
was never raised, and that in no two of them is.
the public idea of Protection v. Freetrade identi-
cal. Those papers’ method is to label each mem-
ber P. or F. in some patent way of their own,
then to take off a couple or so from the Protec-
tionist total for luck, and add them on to the
other side and announce a pronounced victory for
Freetrade. The momentous issue is really—Can
the Barton Govt, carry on ? And as the Govt,
has a big majority in the Lower House, though a
somewhat smaller one in the Senate, it no doubt
can.
Among utterances of successful Federal
candidates on the declaration of the respective
polls, two stand out. Higgins, at Northern
Melb.: —
The Argus and its allies have made the most they could,
of their cry of Pro-Boer and anti-Federalism, and our
opiKuients have dragged from the slime the monster of
bigotry and sectarianism. They have made a ghastly
failure. . . You radical electors have administered a
well-deserved rebuke to those narrow and miserable souls
who thought that a man ceuld not be loyal to the Crown
without ceasing to be loyal to his ideals of truth and
justice.
Ronald, at Southern Melb. :
It was an epoch-making election, for it had shown that
dictatorial journal, the Age, that its power had gone by.
The people had risen in their thousands to protest against
the blackguardly journalism by which he had been
assailed.
The Argus reported Higgins in full, but the
treacherous Age, while giving its rival’s castiga-
tion in extenso, carefully burked the slating it got
from Ronald.
“Civil Servant” to The Bulletin:—
.1. Lionel Fegan, M.L.A., Minister for Mines in the
N.S.W. State Ministry, has been appointed, at the insti-
gation of VV. J. Lyne, Federal Under-secretary for Home
Affairs. Not ten years ago Mr. Fegan was a working
miner, and his attainment of a permanent Govt, billet at
some £lOOO a year is a remarkable tribute to his ability.
His political promotion is a peculiarly apt illustration of
the success of the turn-coat. Elected’in 1894 on the solid ;
out-and-out labor ticket, he soon kicked away the
ladder, arid was next returned as a Reidite, pure and
simple. In ’9B-99 he threw ail his influence among the
miners against Federation at each Referendum. For
Reid’s latter-day adherence to the Commonwealth Bill,
Mr. Fegan “ratted” from his party, and was the framer
of the fatal resolution which put Reid out and
Lyne in. For his apostasy Lyne rewarded him
with a portfolio, and now, while B. R. Wise cannot be
allowed a seat in the Federal Legislature, an anti-
Federalist like Fegan comes in for a prime permanent
billet of £lOOO a year. The appointment will make a,
great row ; first because the system of political “ re-
wards ” is abhorred by most Australians, and also because
the thing smacks of the Lyne-See idea of “ arranging ”
matters for personal ends regardless of public
interests. Especially will Barton incur the dis-
like of the various Public Services of the Com-
monwealth. During the Federal campaign both
Barton and O’Connor assured the public servants, in set
speeches delivered for their benefit, that in making
appointments to the Federal service, those men who had-
long and faithfully served their State Govts, would
naturally be first thought of, and that the status of
public servants would be raised by the fact that a higher
and better sphere of duty would open out before them.
The single fact of the Fegan appointment kills all these
beautiful theories. A few more rewards for political
“ foresight,” and good-bye to the hopes of a better tone
in Australia’s public life, and a higher ideal for Aus-
tralia’s public men.

ALL persons requiring advices or information of any

that requests will be attended to.

“A list of the names attached to the
Funeral wreaths was duly laid before His
Majesty.” In its mind’s eye The Bulletin can
see that list alive and crawling.
The last one hears of Queen Victoria, she
was caressing her dog. Pitt’s last words, in the
history books, are, “801 l up the map of Burope.
His actual last words are said to have been, Bet
me one of Bellamy’s pies.” Bellamy was the
House of Commons caterer.
“Miles”: Bloch, the man who says
weapons are to kill war, wrote shortly before the
S’African trouble began that surrenders would be
'more plentiful in future wars. He said that a
loss of 60 per cent., spread over a week, might not
stop an advance, but 10 per Cent, in a few minutes
would be too much for the human animal.
Magersfontein cowed the Highlanders. It is like
the "torpedo coming at % shipload of brave men.
Says Macbeth : “What man dares, I dare; who
dares do more is none.”
One Kinnear, M.P., or ex, is the author
of a chatty vol. on the Commons, up to date. He
has a deal to say about the shoal of women always
gadding about the Houses of Parliament nowa-
days, in its function of the Social Club, for genteel
females —no common women being allowed in the
precincts, though they can pay taxes for the
sweetness and light. When the Thames steamers
are passing the Terrace people shout to the mem-
bers, “Why don’t you go inside and attend to
your business?”

“The labor member must not dress like a
waiter, or spell like a Duke.” — John Burns.
H. Y. L. Brown, S.A. Govt. Geologist,
lately returned from a 700-mile ride on camel-
back into the heart of Australia, searching for
mineral phosphates. This unassuming official has
travelled over almost every foot of S.A.’s drought-
country at different times. He practically dis-
covered Tarcoola goldfield, and declares that it is
the most promising show S.A. has yet had.
Invalided Fred. B. Norton tells some droll
yarns of his sojourn in the Alfred Hospital,
Melb. In December he served three weeks as a
“ paying guest ” at the Alfred and was not very
happy. Lots of other people speak coldly of their
experiences at that fashionable curing establish-
ment within the last two or three years, but their
tales lack the descriptive humour of Norton’s.
He depicts the casualness of the attendance from
a comedian’s point of view, and perhaps he drew
on his imagination for the story of how a wardsman
came along one night with two glasses of physic.
“ Barton ! Barton ! Are you Barton?” asks the
visitor. “ That’s me, if it’s Toby Barton you’re
looking for,” murmurs the sick joker. “No, not
Barton, but another name like it,” says the glass
bearer, who is grasping them from the top. ‘‘ If
you mean Norton, that’s all right,” says Fred. B.
“Which is your medicine, Barton?” asks the
wardsman. “ The one you haven’t got your finger
in,” says Norton ; and he swallows the chosen
dose. Three minutes later comes a nurse
into his' ward crying, in an undertone—“ Norton,
you’ve taken the wrong medicine ! ” “ Well, don’t
bring the stomach-pump,” says F.8.N., “ for that
was the first drink I’ve enjoyed since I came to
the hospital.” On another occasion a nurse dis-
turbed his day-dream by excitedly ordering him
to shake up his pillow, smooth the counterpane,
smuggle away the newspapers and books that lay
on his bed, and prepare for a visit of inspection.
“Lady from Sydney going through the wards,”
explains the nurse. “Don’t let me offend her
royal eyes,” moans Norton ; “just pull thesheet
over my face and pretend that I’m making a
beautiful corpse.”


Apropos of the attempted white-washing
of Major-General Oolvile, it may be noted that
new Queen Alexandra’s Lord Chamberlain is
Baron Colvile of Culross, who is placed next to
Viscount Wolseley, Gold Stick in Waiting, at
Court functions.
Melb. Women’s Hospital benefited lately
in the sum of £6 10s. 6d. through the decease of
the beneficiary by the will of E. M‘Carthy, said
beneficiary—one Paddy—having died without
known heirs or assigns. “Paddy” was a much-
prized fox-terrier, and was life-tenant in the
estate.
There is another way in which the dual
vote was worked in Melbourne for the greater
glory of Smawlcum. Hundreds of fatted electors
who had votes for the city and for Kooyong
paired, and thus contrived to work both ballots.
The difference between Knox and Fink for Koo-
yong did not greatly agitate the Portly People,
but that between Labor-member Maloney and
Money M ‘Eacharn distressed them greatly, so
that the Stouts residing in Kooyong who wanted
to vote for Fink paired with Fats who desired to
vote for Knox, and the lot left Kooyong at that,
and rushed the city with eager votes for the
gilded ship-owner. The effect of this manoeuvre
and the dual votes that flowed in from uncon-
tested districts, like St. Kilda and the Ports, was
a mighty majority for M'Eacharn in the Lonsdale
and Latrobe divisions, where the ex-Mayor scored
2116 to Maloney’s 436.

A Fatal Spider-Web.
When a fly gets accidentally
caught in a spider’s web, the
spider calmly goes about the
work of securing his prey.
He doesn’t hurry particu-
larly ; he takes his time, and
binds first the fly’s feet, and
then his wings and his entire body. That is the
way with the dread enemy of mankind—disease.
It has a web —the web of trivial disorders neg-
lected. When a man heedlessly stumbles into
that web he must be careful. The stomach is
most liable to get out of order. Hence how im-
portant that no diseased matter clogs its opera-
tions, which would cause nausea and distress by
the taking of food. If not attended to, a thou-
sand ailments are ready to arise, caused by
impurities being carried through the whole system
by the blood. What you want is something that
will keep the digestive organs in a healthy con-
dition, and unclog, or carry away, all impure
matter. That something is Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills; they cleanse the stomach, make the
appetite healthy, the digestion perfect, the liver
active and the blood pure. Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills cure Biliousness, Indigestion, Consti-
pation, Dyspepsia, Headaches, Liver and Kidney
Diseases, Boils, Pimples and Blotches. They
are unlike other medicines—they do not weaken
—and as a cure for Female Ailments they
are unequalled. Sold by chemists and store-
keepers, Is. l£d. per bottle, or six bottles,
6s. 6d., or same will be mailed, post paid,
on receipt of price. Sole Proprietors, The
W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd. (Australasian
Dep6t) 58 Pitt Street, Sydney. There is another
web, and not a spider’s web, but the web of so-
called “nerve tonics.” Look out for that web;
do not stumble into it. Cunning weavers, they
would bind you. Once you start their use
then there is danger ahead. They may appear
to do you good while you are taking them (so doe 3
whisky), but stop their use, then comes the
re-action. They would have you bound to their
use like the spider binds the fly.

Ibe other stewards and stewardesses are the
staunchest creatures possible. They go down with
the wreck, staying the passengers with life-belts
and comforting them with basins to the last.
Albert, after the ship goes down, is seen on a raft
with a small boy, ruffian Styan, and a pint of
drinking water ; and there is considerable trouble
over the distribution of that last pint.
•*, * *
When the castaways get home, the poor
Bart, is unwelcome owing to his brother hav-
mg jumped the title. Also, he discovers to
his sorrow that the usurper has a lawful
wife already, but is nevertheless preparing to
marry Frances Ross with bigamous intent. Now,
the baronet is fond of Frances, as before related,
and for her sweet sake, one would expect him to
be annoyed with the'bad young brother. On the
contrary, he merely remarks, “So Harry has
brought a stain upon our name,” the stain being
due to irregularities in the lawful wife’s Past.
Other things happen to Albert before he
arrives at his inheritance, notably forcible
chloroforming and confinement in a private
lunatic asylum ; but at last his luck turns, and the
principal villain falls down the lift-hole at a hotel.
Harrie Ireland looks well and behaves with
fortitude as the bad brother’s wife. She even
bears the news of hubby’s violent, death pretty
calmly till Frances Ross glides forward and pro-
mises to be a sister to her, whereupon the pat-
ronised penitent expires casually. The prospect
of being a widow with a Past, and a sister-in-law
to remind her of it, is too much for the poor thing.
* • * *
Bland Holt’s wily, witty Jew and Styan’s
Bash ford are a pleasing pair of rascals; Charles
Brown’s defaulting solicitor is also engaging, and
one wishes him nothing worse than a trip to Fiji.
All the characters are-more or less well done
(rather less than more as a rule), and the staging
will keep the drama in robust health for some
time to come.
** * *
The bill at Melb. Bijou is not too bad in
its weakest spots. Moreover, its weak spots are
few, and its strong ones are numerous, and the
confiding visitor from agricultural parts, who has
put his trust in Rickards without preliminary
inquiry, gets rather more than his usual
comfort and joy for the cash investment.
Austin Rudd, a comedian of abnormal funniness,
or plain ordinary humor, just according to how he
strikes people, has returned from London and
elsewhere with anew set of songs. And Lottie
Collins is back at the Bijou for 12 nights only.
These are understood to be the positively last
nights of the red-lined Widow, so anybody who
has not yet met a relic of that description is
recommended to go and consider Lottie’s crimson
effects.

N.S.W. owned Ibex, by Gossoon from Angora,
scored the easiest of wins in A.J.C. Champagne Stakes
on Monday, which, considering the quality of the com-
pany, was remarkable. Grasspan was second, and Mr.
Bailey s (Ballarat) fine colt Hautvillers third. Favoritism
was shared by first and last named at twos, while sevens
were available about Grasspan. Ibex, since running
away with V.R.C. Maribyrnong Plate, hadn’t again
shaped in public, otherwise he would have perhaps car-
ried much more money. He first saw light at Kirkham,
and went (with his dam) for 40gs. at the stud’s dispersal.
His victory, with a 101 b. penalty up, equals the perform-
ance of Coil, Aurum, and Bobadil, but none of these won
with anything like as much in hand. Ibex’s time (1.141)
!& only Jsec. slower than Bobadil’s record (1.13}), accom-
plished three years since. All winners—Victoria’s rep.
(Hautvillers) included—were bred in N.S.W. Peculiar
occurrence all seven starters finished as their names ap-
peared in the book.

Branxton (N.S.W.) races, t’other dav, were res-
ponsible for a curious happening. Two nags* (Nicodemus
and Coree) ran three dead heats. They humped 9.12 and
6.7, as named, in Branxton Handicap (7fur.), and couldn’t
be separated. Subsequently the two came together again
(Nicodemus 10.10 and Coree 7.8) in the Final Handicap
(6fur.), and a second dead-heat resulted. Later, they
were saddled-up to decide both dead-heats in one go.
Nicodemus, as in previous two struggles, made the run-
ning, and was loudly hailed winner, but Coree, sticking
well, finished upsides with his bigger opponent. Respec-
tive owners halved the total prize-money—£ls.
* * * *
Dan O’Brien has thoroughly sickened of his
Australian-bred and other galloping nondescripts, and
is invoking the services of an auctioneer. The veteran’s
luck has gone a-missing, and he intends quitting the
game in M.L. for a spell and tripping to England shortly.
* * * *
Maoriland trotting authorities have hitherto
barred feminity from the race-track, but Otahuhu Club
has now licensed Miss Ethel May Abbott, aged 16, to ride
in races. The ambitious young lady affects bloomers and
rides astraddle. She is a follower of hounds, and has won
prizes in riding and driving competitions at shows.
* * * m
Chows’ proverbial betting luck is due to the
fact that they always plunk their money on long-price
outsiders.. At Grey (M.L.) trots recently a man refused a
ticket —said he had called for another. Noting that it
was an alleged “ no-chancer,” a grinning Chow annexed
it, and notched £34 10s. div. In another event he divd.
a “ fifteener.” Chinese bet on the principle that one good
win will more than cover losses for (say) a couple of
meetings.

In response to appeals to the “ gods” to supply
places of absentees in Sydney Gaiety A.O.’s bantam tour-
ney, t’other night, a couple of newsboys came along. One
(sans all but shirt and pants) faced the redoubtable and
stocky “Tibby” Watson, and gave him a hot tussle
throughout, while the “ nooze ” lad experienced a bad
time, albeit he served up much stoush himself. In the
seventh round the “ pick-up ” stopped, limp and pumped-
out. “Look ’ere, genTmen,” said the artful youth
between big gasps, “ I ain’t givin’ up because I’m full—
me wind’s gorn, and I’m too weak. I ain’t had a feed to-
day ; I’d’ve won only fer that.” This brought down the
house, which showered enough coin into the ring to
supply three feeds a day for a month or two.

“Jab”: The spunky, decent little “Kaffir”
should give bruising a rest. He doesn’t improve with
age, and is too manly a fellow to form a chopping-block
for the class pitted against him now. His go with Tur-
ner, at Sydney Golden Gate A.O. on Saturday night, evi-
denced beyond question that he has much deteriorated.
Still, ’twas his fight, for, with two or three exceptions, he
led every round. “ I declare it a draw,” said the referee,
but the crowd wouldn’t have it, and hooted, howled, and
shrieked its disgust.

Lightweight boxer Sturgeon runs a great busi-
ness in his boxing tent at N.S.W. Agricultural Show ; ad-
mission sixpence, to see three or four three-round spars,
the whole programme lasting about 15 min. Writer
hears that £6O was taken at the tent on Good Friday.
The most unequal pug. match made among top-
notchers for years was between black Tom Mitchell and
Jack M'Gowan, at Melb. Democratic Club t’other night.
Mitchell, tall and lank-limbed, stood over the dwarf giant
M'Gowan, whose abnormally short reach had no chance
with the Negro’s gorilla-like length of arm. Were they
evenly matched in point of skill, Mitchell’s natural advan-
tages would have proved a big handicap, but Jack isn’t an
extra-clever boxer by any means, while the other man has
few if any superiors on this section. Again, though the
battle was announced for the “lightweight champion-
ship of Australia,” it is extremely doubtful whether
either scaled under the limit (10st.); Mitchell certainly
did not, and writer doesn’t think it possible for him to
get inside 71b. of it. Yet backers beforehand laid Gto 4
on M'Gowan ! Mitchell was top dog from the jump, and
never gave the S. Melbournite a chance from beginning
to end of a most one-sided struggle, wherein all the
punching and pummelling (heaps of it) went one way till
the close of the agreed-upon journey—2o rounds.
M'Gowan has weathered many a heavy battering and
weathered the latest one too, but he never had so little
say before. No man conversant with the capabilities of
each principal would have suggested the match, and how
the public came to lay odds on M'Gowan is a puzzle.

News from ’Ome tells that Australian Jem
Richardson was recently matched with a Belfast pug
named Quinn. Richardson has been in London for three
or four years,jand is referred to always as “ of Honolulu.”
Why ? Luck has never come his way once in England—
everything has gone against him.

Latest anent Griffo shuts him out for good as a
factor in pugilism, and leaves nothing ahead but the
lunatic asylum again, gaol, or, at best, the poorhouse.
At Chicago (March 4), Griffo was gaoled for a series of
bar-smashing breaks, and is reported to have said : “I
can’t work, am through with fighting, and so don’t know
where to stow myself.”

* * * *
“ The calling in of the services of Sandow,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “to pick
out the muscular flaws in the Cambridge eight, has
allayed considerable curiosity as to what the strong
boater’s system would be. It certainly had the prime
athletic merit of common sense, and if Eugene Sandow
will give up feats of strength with his tongue—in fact,
chuck ‘ skiting ’ and stick to his muscle manufactory, he
will win confidence. The Cambridge crew was excep-
tionally beefy this year (only one man is under 12st.), and
Sandow when told the rowing faults of a man prescribed
for him thus : No. 3 was slow in ‘ dropping his hands and
getting them away.’ A course of special wrist and fore-
arm exercise was allotted him and he rapidly improved.
Another one, weak in the shin and ham muscles, did not
‘recover his slide’ quick enough; a particular exercise
likewise removed his weakness, and so on. Sandow is
really a capable anatomist with a belief that most, if not
all, faults of style in rowing and kindred pastimes, are
caused by lack of development of, perhaps, an appar-
ently insignificant muscle; and this is shrewd horse sense.”

towards the bowling invasion of England, left per Omrah
Wednesday, this week. Anticipated the full team will
total 60. Letters convey that the visit isn’t creating

those going in anticipation of a comparatively cheap holi-
day and much dining will find themselves badly left, par-
ticularly as most of the ’Ome clubs are not too well fixed
financially, and entertaining 60 visitors is no small matter.

The tragic death of the popular R. Lord
Hodgson, on the Caulfield railway-line, removes one of
the best of Victorian chessists. Several times he has
been champion of his State, and once he aspired to the
title of Australasian champion. But he was hardly first-
class, and was decisively defeated by E. N. Wallace, of
N.S.W.

When playing crib, in different
parts of Maoriland .always hear two pairs called “ Mor-
gan’s orchard.” Do you know how that term originated ?
Plugger Bill, unmindful of his years and a lame
leg, girded up his loins at the Melb. Druids’ annual out-
ing and pedalled his Red Bird first past the post in the
10-miles Austrilasian Championship and Druids’ Wheel
Race. Don Walker and Gudgeon each won a race on Red
Birds. Thus putting up a record, as every Druid event
was never before won by one machine.

“N.Q.” : The victory up here is that of
Bamford over Brown for the House of Reps.
Consider the contestantsVilliers Brown,
a shrewd business man, good speaker, with plenty
of money, smd Bamford, unknown to 50 per cent,
of the electors, middling speaker, poor except in his
desire for a White Australia. Brown st imped the
division for months. Bamford just fl tted here
and there, and then beat the Kanakaphile vote
for vote; and in Townsville (Brown’s head-
quarters) headed the poll by 300. All in the'hot-
bed of the smellful Islander. If this doesn’t con-
vince the Black and-Tan crowd that they have a
lost cause it will need a brace and bit to force the
argument in.
It is obvious that Sydney Agricultural
Show-ground will soon have to be much altered so
that a large part of the assemblage shall not be—
as at present—debarred from seeing some of the
ring-attractions, such as trotting, jumping, &c. As
things are, thousands (many of them women and
children) have to stand immovably fixed in a
dense crowd for 3 or 4 hours at a stretch, unable
all the time to see anything other than the sudden
bob-up of a rider’s head as his mount flies a fence.
The large space that might easily be used to
obviate this state of things is, at present, taken-up
by advertising-pavilions, &c. In the pony com-
petition—-jumping and otherwise—were to be seen
dwarf thoroughbreds, as well as genuine ponies.
Now a thoroughbred—though it may race as a
pony and be of pony height—is not in the true
“Show” sense of the word a pony, and should,
therefore, be excluded from pony competitions.
Other notable points were that the judging in
many of the equine events was altogether too pro-
tracted ; that the newly-erected jumps are per-
pendicular on the take-off side, instead of being
sloped-away; and that the fat cattle exhibition
was the finest yet seen in Sydney. At the com-
mencement of the trotters and pacers contest, just
as the horses were being let go, a string of about a
score of bulls was led right across the running-
track, but thougli the starter’s attention was called
to this he gave the word to go—and go they
did; luckily without a mishap, but at a
fearful risk that should never have been taken.
The trotting contests were splendid, and most of
the jumping was first-class, some of the ladies
giving highly artistic exhibitions in the saddle.
The motor-cars formed a powerful attraction,
especially to the fair sex, many of whom were
picked up and literally “flown” round the ring
at rates varying from 20 to 50 miles an hour.
Some horses were exhibited in an all but tailless
condition, and it would be advisable for Agricul-
tural Societies throughout Australasia to agree to
refuse entry of any thus closely “ docked ” horse.
The practice—which is simply a foolish copying
of an English fashion —is a cruel one in an insect-
pestered climate like ours.
