The Rand mine-owners. The S’African war,
as Tiib Bulletin has often anticipated, is going to be a
gorgeous thing for them. Cabled that Kitchener has
allowed a number of mines to. re-start operations “ sub-
ject to certain defined conditions ” —that each is only to
keep 50 stamps at work, no matter how large the battery.
(That seems a good thing for the comparatively small
Band mine with a 50-head mill; and a bad one for others,
such as the Simmer and Jack, with at least 400.) That
the miners employed shall receive the same pay and
rations as irregular troopers ; the balance of their ordi-
nary wages to be devoted to a fund to provide for the
families of Band residents killed during the war;
Johannesburg Chamber of Mines to accept these terms
and to await restoration of normal conditions. If this is
all Kitchener wants it is a monstrous tax on the miners,
while the mine-oWners are allowed to go free. The Bulle-
tin expects the proposal to be defended by the news-
papers of Australia on the ground that the Boer war was
started, and is being prolonged, entirely in the interest
of the Band miners, and the Band mine-owners had noth-
ing to do with it from start to finish ; that having started
the war they should pay for it, while the unhappy million-
aire mine-owner should not have his divs. in any way
diminished.
* * * »
If there be any Trades-hall shareholders in any
of the Melbourne banks —say the Bank of Victoria, for
instance—they might benefit their poor black-coated
brother, and at the same time demonstrate the compara-
tive dignity of manual labour, by enquiring into the
salaries question at next half-yearly meeting. Publica-
tion of the Bank of Vic.’s pay-sheet with particulars as
to age and service of each at the head office
would certainly deter a good many parents from enlist-
ing their sons in the cuff-and-collar brigade. Here are
some gruesome details. Eight distinguished officials, in-
cluding General-manager Law, who draws £2OOO a year,
are on the right side of the £3OO mark. Fifteen get
from £2OO to £3OO ; whilst seventeen other old servants,
who have served as youth and man a full 10 years in the
Collins-street sweating den, are now remunerated at the
average rate of £123 10s. per annum. The lowest salary
at present for a ten years is £7O per annum.
One middle-aged clerk is paid at the rate of £6 a year for
every year he has passed under the Bank of Victoria’s
roof. Eight junior clerks, who have devoted from sto 9
years apiece to the gentlemanly occupation of casting up
figures, are drawing £6O or £7O apiece for their twelve
months’ trouble. And some thirty other misguided
youngsters are now engaged in working their weary way
towards the £7O haven of rest. The bitter cry of the
uninfluential bank clerk deserves more sympathy than
it gets from the working classes, considering that it can
expect none at all from Fat Man in general.
* * * *
Sydney D.T.’s .London correspondent is much
shocked at a suggestion of Arnold White, M.P., with re-
gard to bankers’ unclaimed balances in the United
Kingdom. The subject is a fairly familiar one in Aus-
tralia. What has often been argued at this end of the
world White repeats, and urges that bankers should be
obliged to disgorge to the State balances not operated on
for a certain number of years. The correspondent says :
“ Mr. White’s proposition is briefly this: He would by
retrospectively suspending the operation of the statutes
of limitation with regard to bankers only extort from
them vast sums of money which at the present moment
belong to the bankers themselves. Of the disturbance to
trade which would be caused by crippling the bankers,
Mr. White recks not at all. Mr. White estimates that a
sum of 90 millions sterling could thus be raised, which
would go in relief to the general taxpaj'er. The whole
project is impracticable and iniquitous, and much as the
general taxpayer would appreciate relief from a shilling
income tax, he is not likely to give a moment’s serious
consideration to a proposition to legalise wholesale thefts
from a section of the community.”
The prevalent English idea is that these
balances are when “ the period required by the statute
of limitations has expired,” the property of the bankers !
This view has been held since the time of James 1., and
bankers, it seems, have boldly acted on it. Whatever the
legal aspect of the question may be, the social and moral
aspects are obvious enough, and these latter should, and
must certainly at no remote date, prevail. In Australia
men place their money in a bank, go to the diggings or
otherwise on the wallaby, and find a resting place where
“ the dead men lie.” It is true they ought to dispose of
their effects in due legal form. As a matter of fact,
they don’t. Most don’t. Then, as a matter of fact,
the banker grabs the pool. The man may have
relatives, but the banker makes no inquiries. It is the
dead man’s money he wants and holds. Whatever law
supports him in that, whether it dates from James I. or
Egbert, must be swept from the Statute Book. It is
mere spoliation, robbery. When, then, balances remain
untouched for a term to be fixed by Parliament, the de-
positor should be supposed to have died intestate, and
the funds be paid over to the Govt. The Govt, would
give an indemnity to the bank, so that if the depositor
reappeared the banker could have all the protection an
honest man wants. Legislation should be easily attained
on this subject, and as England seems so shocked Aus-
tralia should show how simple the reform is.
Stewart, now Q. Senator, spoke his mind
during the Boer war frenzy, and was refused a
hearing later on at Rockhampton North (his
electorate). When the Senate poll came along he
scored over all contestants. They’re cooling
down.
The Victorian Govt, did a mean thing over
the reception of the Governor-General. They
particularly wanted to get a good muster of
Mounted Rifles to form an escort, so they offered
every mounted rifleman who attended a free rail-
way pass, available from the day of the reception
to the 9th—that is, a week. They got their good
muster by this inducement, and then, immediately
after the reception, issued a countermanding
order, saying that the pass would only be good
until the following day, when all the men must
return home ! Many of the men swore and—went
back ; others swore they wouldn’t go, and didn’t.
If the Govt, wants a good show of troops when the
Yorks come they mustn’t play mean tricks of this
sort. It seems to have been done because the
Railway Dept, didn’t wish to carry any dead-
heads in Easter week; but that should have been
thought of before.
The Coningham Case.
Of course, the verdict in the Coningham case
was the only possible verdict on the evidence.
When there is no clear proof that an accused
person is guilty, the custom of affirming his legal
innocence is rooted in wisdom. A juryman
may make his own presumptions, retain his
own beliefs; but unless his presumptions and
beliefs entitle him to say that he knows, he had
better always admit his ignorance with a verdict
of “Not Guilty.” The Coningham jury’s some-
what irregular and unasked-for rider (the matter
dealt with might easily have been left for the
consideration of the Attorney-General) seems to
show that some of the jurymen were still
personally undecided. When the jury went
out of its way to add a rider stating (after
seeing all that documentary evidence) that,
in its opinion, the Coninghams were guiltless
of conspiracy, the jury showed —that is appar-
ently the only interpretation of the rider—that
some of its members were making a compromise
between belief or prejudice and reason. The
most obvious inference is that the co-respondent
had failed to entirely convince some of the jury-
men of his innocence; but as petitioner failed to
entirely convince them of guilt they took the pro-
per course by returning a verdict of “Not Guilty.”
The rider was no doubt a sop to some jurymen or
one juryman who leant to Mrs. Coningham, but
did not sufficiently believe in her to take the
responsibility of contending to the end for the
right of exercising his power to wreck; a man’s life
on the strength of an uncorroborated theory. As
petitioner failed to prove his charges, there was
nothing for it but to say “Not Guilty.” But
some juryman’s belief that the co-respondent
failed to prove his innocence beyond the shadow
of a possible doubt was apparently voiced in that
compassionate rider.
This view reflects neither upon respondent nor
co-respondent. The offence charged is essentially
an act done in secret, and might be committed in
circumstances which made it utterly impossible
for any human being apart from the two con-
cerned to prove guilt. It might be falsely charged
in circumstances which made it impossible to
prove innocence. And, upon anything short of
reasonable proof, a jury can only return a verdict
of “Not Guilty.” •
The legal practice affirms that when you can-
not prove your case on the main issue, you should
try to confuse it with a side-issue. The average
jury does not think clearly, and mixes its cate-
gories in a wonderful way. If you allege that the
adversary is a pot, and cannot prove him a
pot, the average jury will be much impressed
when you show conclusively that he is a
kettle. The side-issue at the first Coningham
trial was Mrs. Coningham’s character. It was
strongly relevant to the case, but not essential,
since the offence charged could have been as easily
committed whether Mrs. Coningham in her
youth was an angel or a demon. That side-issue
failed ; in fact, as a weapon, it inclined to turn in
counsel’s hands. The jury realised that Mrs.
Coningham might have been more sinned against,
than sinning, and they disagreed.
So at the second trial a second side-issue was
found—the issue of the letters. In the strife over
the authenticity of the letters the actual charge
was lost sight of ; and the battle went against
Coningham. That was all that was needed to
turn the first jury’s practical verdict of “ Not
Proven” into the second jury’s verdict of “Not
Guilty,” with the lingering doubt or prejudice re-
presented by the compassionate rider.
The Bulletin views the general result with
satisfaction. It is quite sure that the co-
respondent (whose general credibility was in
no way shaken during the trial, whereas
that of his opponents was effectually shaken
over and over again) could not possibly
be condemned on the evidence, or rather
on the lack of evidence; and when the
evidence is insufficient it thinks that legally and
socially a verdict of “Not Guilty ” is always to be
preferred to a verdict of “Not Proven.” The
worst feature of the case was the fact that it
ranked as a religious feud; and that numbers of
adherents on both sides showed themselves care-
less of truth or justice, guilt or innocence, so long
as their own religion won.
What was at first blush the impressive circum-
stantiality of Mrs. Coningham’s story has wilted
sadly. Men of the world who in the beginning
swallowed her “confession” whole, now quite real-
ise that the entire fabric could possibly have been
erected by any ready-witted woman of experience
possessed of Dr. O’Haran’s personal acquaintance
and autographed-photo., and enjoying for a year
or two “ the run of ” his church premises, to the ex-
tent to which it was apparently enjoyed by any of
the numerous members of the Cathedral guilds.
And when you come to coldly recall
all the circumstances, it is really wonderful
how so much time and money could have been
spent over a case where, had the pursuing woman
been in quest of a verdict of 7s. 6d. a week for the
maintenance of an illegitimate child, she could
not have got a verdict on the evidence from
any Police Magistrate who knew enough law
to be aware that under the Statute no man shall,
in bastardy, be adjudged the father of a child on
the mere say-so of the mother alone.
At Poverty Point.
Dear Bulletin, —Sydney is reputed to have the finest
organ in the world ; and while M. Wiegand played, writer
believed that it was. But when the local man climbed up
to perform he often felt fully convinced that some one
had tied a rooster in the pipes. Since Wiegand’s depar-
ture a gentle squeal seems to have settled in the
high solo-pipes, to break in upon Mendelssohn’s sweetest
harmonies on the slightest provocation. Present Town
Hall recitals appear to be run for the advertisement of
the Massey-family. A short Easter programme is filled
with no less than six compositions by the Massey-family.
Pretty rough on musical folk who come from other States
during the holiday and get Massey compositions blown at
them from the finest organ in the world:—Albert Dor-
rinoton. P.S.—I attach the programme as a curiosity: —

Mr. ROBERT CLARANCE NEWELL, Pianist,
who accompanies Mdlle. Dolores during her
Australian Tour.
Haddon Chambers’s new comedy seemingly discounts
the assumption that he was assisted by Oscar Wilde, or
some other brilliant “ghost,” in his previous success—
“ The Tyranny of Tears.” Defunct Oscar surely had
nothing to do with “ The Awakening,” which scored a hit
at the St. James’s Theatre last month. London Eeferee
says of this :—“ An interesting story is told by a
dramatist who has mastered the cunning of his craft.
The dialogue is witty and polished, and, in thought and
form, proper to the characters by whom it is spoken.
The play has been ‘ lived.’ It is life. Not, perhaps, the
very best of life, but human flesh and blood all the same.”
And the denouement, as in “ The Tyranny” aforesaid, is
bright and hopeful. “ Mr. Chambers has found his in-
spiration in the manners of the day, but he has not
drained his subject to the very dregs, and he does not vex
the playgoer’s mind by working out his problem in the
way which would please nobody but a half-a-dozen revo-
lutionary critics, and the strong-minded ladies and weak-
minded gentlemen who follow their lead.” The small but
not unimportant part of a butler in “ The Awakening” is
played by H. H. Vincent, who was stage-manager for
Williamson, Garner and Musgrove at the time when Had-
don Chambers left Australia to make his fortune.
The Trebelli family is fruitful in names. Madame
Dolores’ mother, the perhaps greater Trebelli, was ZClia
Gillebert, born in Paris, 1838. Her musical name
was formed by knocking off the G, and then spell-
ing Ulebert backwards. Then she married Signor
Bettini, and tacked on his name. Disraeli and Glad-
stone once joined in complimenting her at a party.
Said Dizzy : “ The only thing Monsieur Gladstone and I
can agree upon is admiration of your beautiful voice.”
Her London dCbut was in '62, at Her Majesty’s, as Maffio
Orsini, in “ Lucrezia Borgia.” An unfortunate pecuniary
disagreement between mother and daughter, published
over a court case, led the daughter to be not particularly
fond of the name of Trebelli.
A la Madame Dante, Haidee Heller carried on Heller’s
business.
Through the fusing of an electric wire recently at an
Australian theatre the “ snow ” on a roof caught fire.
Billee Barlow and her hubby have gone to S’Africa. It
is announced in the press that it is their present intention
to be back in Sydney in August, to pursue the appeal
they have entered against the finding in that libel-action
against The Bulletin. Meanwhile, The Bulletin has to
pay its own costs in the libel-action, which it won before
the jury, and look forward to being reimbursed at
the happy time when Billee Barlow returns. That is the
N.S.W. libel-law. Yet, if Billee Barlow had won her
action, she could, after taxation of costs, have at once
gaoled The Bulletin manager for 12 months had he
failed to pay up to the uttermost farthing; also,
she could have made him bankrupt and got his certificate
withheld for ever until she was paid. Also, if an ordinary
person owes to another £5O, and lets it be known that he
is about to leave the country, the creditor can stop him
from leaving, until he pays up or finds security. But if
you issue a writ against a newspaper and get beaten after
putting the newspaper to £lOO or £lO,OOO expenses, as
costs, you can on spending two or three guineas in filing
notice of appeal say ta-ta and sail over the sea. It is a
queer law. And this we say without imputing to Billee
Barlow any intention of not returning. But why should
The Bulletin have to trust Billee Barlow meanwhile ?
Veteran Australian basso, the brave old “Tom” Bain-
ford, who was 71 on 2nd April, is possibly the oldest
vocalist in the world still exercising his profession. He
originally came to Australia as principal bass (and part-
proprietor) of the first Christy Minstrel co. which visited
these shores, and made his first appearance here at
Sydney School of Arts. The other leading members of
the co. were Nish, the violinist; Joe Brown, jig-dancer ;
and Chas. Stewart, tenor. They were shipwrecked, en
route, at the Cape of Good Hope, and spent 72 hours on
the beach. Bainford was one of the first Dick Deadeyes
“ Pinafore ”) in Australia, and a very good one, too.
Demise : Our old friend Alfred Maltby, original of
Joskin Tubbs, in “ Pink Dominoes,” and Tutor Dawson,
in “ Betsy.” A highly-cultured man, reminding one
somewhat of Haskett Smith. Maltby’s theatrical line gave
little indication of his capabilities, and seemed to have
been determined only by his quaint face. He could stage
an archaeological sort of play as effectively as Lewis Wing-
field, being a great student. In Australia, I last remem-
ber him acting Professor Justinian Babbett, in “ A Night
Out,” at Melb. Princess, with Anson as Brutus Snap, a
crushed tragedian. Titheradge, Agnes Thomas, and the
late Annie Taylor were lesser lights in that notably
humorous performance. During his stay in Australia he
did some creditable oil-painting, and wrote the “ book ”
for the “ Firm’s ” panto, at Melb. Boyal.
Cora Urquhart Brown Potter, the actress of many
angles, is said to have slipped George Musgrove and
taken a London engagement, despite the fact that G. M.
had made “ all arrangements ” for an Australian season.
We have heard several stories of this kind lately, and if
they are true managers must be growing very lax in
the matter of agreements. It used to be the commonest
precaution of a business manager to tie up his star
with a stiff document before taking any risks.
“ Melb.” : Rodney is somewhat thin, in humor, but
pleases the audience. I did not see him at the best, he
being rather catarrhal, and not borne up by a large house.
He is evidently accustomed to rouse a good deal of fun,
and is not warmed up by a merely simpering people. His
exits seemed abrupt and almost contemptuous. Doubt-
less, he would go off with a roar at Pretoria or Durham.
Apparently, he has been awhile away from great centres,
and contracts that inexplicable provincial tinge which
mosses on the best performers under such circumstances.
He is not the born entertainer, like Bussell, Parry, or
Thatcher, the man who seems to have more in reserve than
he ever gives out; but Bodney is of the Maccabe order,
the manufactured. Mimicry is his forte. Extremely
good in Cockney, Scotch, Irish. Why no Dutch? His
changes are too few. He ought to go to London and get
one of the clever fellows there to write him an entirely
fresh entertainment.
The widow of the late W. G. Barker, the Melb. basso,
acknowledges, with gratitude, the receipt of £523 from
the Barker memorial concert, which amount includes,
presumably, a few subscriptions to the fund apart from
actual concert takings. What about the extra concerts
which were expected to produce another £5OO ? The
Bulletin hopes that the cause of widow Barker and
child has not been put into the shade by Jookoyork pre-
parations. At the present moment Melbourne needs a
lot of urging to raise money on account of anything not
connected with York’s visit.
Eleven plays are listed for the present six-weeks’
dramatic season at Melb. Majesty’s. The plays won’t run
—they’ll simply gallop.
The picturesque Kyrle Bellew is again in Australia with
an eye to his N.Q. gold-mining interests, which are said
to be promising. In his mining capacity he affects a rich
region too long neglected—the Palmer Biver. Bellew
would be worth a massive salary as leading-man with
Nance O’Neil. His Scarpia was a fine, lean, venomous
villain—it would be a lovely foil to the fair, round Nance.
“Cockaigne”: Deceased Harry Monkhouse was just
Paul Bedford over again, minus Paul’s singing capacity,
but the same Toole-y street comedian, suited to lazy Lon-
don, with a sort of Prince of Wales veneering of oleaginity.
Australia or America, or even the English provinces, dis-
rate such men amazingly. They are perceived to be
humorous lecturers, not artists to endue themselves with
a character, like Elton, who was Caliban to Monkhouse’s
Prospero in a Gaiety burlesque of the “Tempest,” which
first evinced that bluff Hal had the proper birthmark for
Lud. Harry got a good, original thing as Bouillabaise, in
“ Paul Jones,” but Snazzy had to educe the fun out of it.
Monkhouse’s best chance, however, was as the original of
Penny-in-the-Slot in the “ Mountebanks,” with Aida
Jenoure as his Carrie Moore.
“Haresfoot”: B. P. Whitworth, just passed away in
Melbourne, had a good deal of connection, off and on,
with the stage of yore. He even did utility business with
G. V. Brooke, and once corrected an impression of mine
that Brooke generally ranted. Then he showed me how
remarkably quiet Brooke was as Shylock. Whitworth
brought over a Maori co. to Australia, appearing in a
drama called “ The Pakeha Maori,” which he wrote. He
took leading characters in performances by the Melb.
Press Amateurs—Sir Harcourt Courtly in “ London
Assurance,” Anthony Absolute in “ The Bivals,’.’ Triplets
in “Masks and Faces,” and Hartmann in “Helping
Hands.”
Again the usual lie about the most popular actresses of
the moment going to the races and backing the winners for
large amounts. A regular advertising gag, played off at
race-time to gobemouche journalists and sycophantic
pen-women.
Nance O’Neil intends putting up another Ibsen play
during the present Australian season, but it is not to be
the Ibsen of our time—the Ibsen of the furious contro-
versies. She has chosen one of Henrik’s earlier efforts,
“ Lady Inger of Ostrat,” a drama written about 1855, and
one exhibiting Ibsen’s quality as a conventional dramatist,
although containing the promise and potency of the Ibsen
of “Ghosts” and “Peer Gynt.” The ordinary theatre-
goer will find “ Lady Inger ” quite intelligible, and, sav-
ing perhaps for the bedroom incident between Nils Lykke
and Elina, eminently moral, as morals are misunderstood
by the crowd. Nance has chosen this drama for the sake
of Lady Inger, a character that should suit her down to
the ground and all round; but one is saddened with
thoughts of Kingston and his effeminate methods in the
role of masterful Nils Lykke.

Some musicians were railing against the ex-musical
critic of a newspaper. “Yes,” said one, “and I have
it on the best authority that he has been deaf for a long
time past, and hardly heard a note of my last perform-
ance, which he criticised so harshly.” “Happy man!”
murmured a brother musician.
“F.C.” : Snazelle must do some hard thinking if ever
he happens into Melb. Gaiety Theatre on a Sunday even-
ing. In ’95 at the demolished Opera House he ran a
Sunday sacred concert with limelight illustrations—a
first-class return for the silver coin admission demanded,
but the dour Melb. Sabbatarians stopped him. T’other
night I dropped into the Gaiety to see Parson Edgar’s
lantern-service. Half-an-hour before starting, the place
was crowded. Pretty well every denomination seemed
to be represented, even the larrikin with the Kitchener
button and the red neckerchief, whose churches and
chapels usually wear chimneys. Many of the audience
conversed pleasantly during prayer, and most of them
remained seated during the hymn-singing. The at-
traction was a brass band of 18 instruments and a French
horn (which is a weapon, not an instrument), and some
limelight illustrations of amusing and edifying incidents
in the life of a Jew, named Daniel—a lion-tamer. These
views were primitive—to put it mildly. The lecturer— or
platitudiniser —gave some original renderings of Bible
texts, one—“ the sword hath slain its thousands and
jealousy its ten thousands ’’—being an unfair aspersion
on the character of the late lamented David. Summed
up, the crowd manifestly rushed the place to escape the
eternal monotony of walking the streets and to elude the
soul-destroying deadliness of the Melbourne Sawbath.
Yes, Snazzy should arrive at some interesting conclusions.
Henry’s dramatic co. is doing the northern towns.
Tasmania to follow.

If Premier See will call N.S.W. Parlia-
ment together he will have ample time to fix up a
.rational Sunday Observance Law, a reduction of
members of Assembly, and a reduction in the
number of Ministers. These are pressing needs.
As to a Local Govt. Act, a sure way to get it in
next Parliament is to reduce the number of mem-
bers in this. Next Parliament will then be anxious
to throw the burden of local touting off its
shoulders.
I A pretty little story of virtue triumphant
' concerns an amorous Australian legislator. Said
legislator’s wife has for admirer a gaudy civil
servant who endeavoured to strengthen his posi-
tion by pointing out to wifey that hubby’s name
was not in the division-lists for 3 nights on which
he was supposed to have been at the “House.”
Wife injudiciously pointed out discrepancy to
husband, who, however, was able to prove that
he Was in the “House,” but engaged on special
committee work. Wife penitently “gives-away”
her admirer as the source of her information, and
the legislator (who is stertorous and has three
chins) passes the story on to wifey’s athletic
brother. Result, a badly-damaged civil servant,
and resumption of domestic felicity.

Justice Owen, who presided over the
Coningham-O’Haran divorce-case, is nearing his
fourteenth year on the Supreme Court Bench,
and is expected shortly to retire. He was called
to the Irish Bar in 1859, and has been 41 years in
N.S.W. For a while he gave up law and accepted
the post of Under-Secretary in the Colonial Secre
tary’s Office, but changed his mind, and appa-
rently for the better. Which of the Judges, for
instance, could have more efficiently conducted
such a trial as the Coningbam case?
The eight-year-old granddaughter of
Charles Absolom, one of the best English slow
bowlers of Grace’s early days, is being exhibited
in Melbourne with a view to impressing the
Yorks with the capabilities of Australia. The
child poses not as a cricketer, but as a well-deve-
loped specimen of the native-born. She weighs
19st. 71b. ; and possesses a 68in. waist, a 24in. calf,
and a 22in. arm; and her name'is Beatrice Maud A.
The Maorilanders must be awarded the
championship for all-round level-headedness. By
soundly-progressive laws they have made their
fertile country hum with prosperity ; and—this is
the final test —their favorite brand is D.C.L. The
whusky of the wise. In Maoriland —the Para-
dise of Scotchmen —the brain capacity of the aver-
age male citizen is judged by the brand upon his
bottle. The whusky doesn’t make the mon alto-
gether, but it shows what manner of mon he is,
and a very little experience of business life in
Dunedin, and throughout Maoriland, convinces
one of the fact that the D.C.L. mon is the best
mon.*
What position will those members of Leg.
Assembly who stood for seats in the Federal Par-
liament and were rejected occupy at the next
local election ? They did their best to get away,
were beaten, and now creep back. Several have
already learned that their reputations are damaged
in the eyes of their old constituents, and there is a
general uneasiness that all the defeated candidates
are worse off than they would have been had they
stayed “ true to Poll.” Other men have edged in
between them and the voters, and show unwilling-
ness to draw out. These members will strongly
resist dissolving Parliament for some time to come.
They will try to hold on till they recover some-
thing of the old relations with their constituents,
if a successful recovery be possible.

“S.S.”: Simon Fraser’s Federal Senate
success is only another proof of what Money can
do. The horde loves a cheerful giver, and Fraser
has been bumping down lump sums for charitable
and patriotic objects quite recklessly of late,
figuring for thousands on several of David Syme’s
little lists. Simon’s return is not so amazing, if
you consider his outlay. Money talks, and the
power of its eloquence in the Vic. Senate elections
gives one a moral sickness. Fraser, Zeal, Sar-
good, the stupid contingent of a stupid House, at
the head of the poll, and then—close upon 17,000
votes below the lowest of them—the first demo-
cratic candidate gets a.look-in! Best, who has
the highest democratic vote, gets a little over
61,000. Simon Fraser has over 83,000. Who’d
believe there were 80,000 Bore-sympathisers in
Victoria?

A Blot on the Democracy.
‘ One man one vote ! ” The placing of Simon
Fraser, William Zeal, and F. T. Sargood at the
top of the Vic. poll for the Senate by 20,000 votes
more than was recorded for any other candidate,
is an ineffaceable blot on the democratic record.
For years past the great democratic mass in Vic-
toria has shaken its grim fist at the members of
the House of 48 Fatmen, and savagely cried
“ Wait till we get you in the open ! ” They were
in the open on Friday week, and the masses tore
their clothes in their eagerness to hoist into the
Senate three of the most ignorant, arrogant, and
uncompromising Tories in the House of Fatman.
A populace guilty of such idiocy deserves all the
48 Fatmen have ever done. That the men with
the grimy fist and the menacing cry have done
this thing is clear from an analysis of the voting
in the working men’s constituencies. Taking
Trades-Hall secretary Barrett as the class of
man the masses ought to have voted for in these
constituencies, this is how the voting works out:
Albert Park.—Barrett 976, Fraser 1194, Sargood 1100,
Zeal 1037 ; Carlton and Carlton South —Barrett 1699,
Fraser 1351, Sargood 1306, Zeal 1216; Coding-wood—
Barrett 2164, Fraser 1521, Sargood 945, Zeal 1237 ;
Brunswick—Barrett 3702, Fraser 3557, Sargood 2445,
Zeal 1915 ; Emerald Hill—Barrett 1042, Fraser 777,
Sargood 655, Zeal 633 ; Essendon and Flemington—Bar-
rett 1540, Fraser 2012, Sargood 1024, Zeal 1820 ; Fitzroy
—Barrett 2016, Fraser 1874, Sargood 1429, Zeal 1662 ;
Footscray—Barrett 1369, Fraser 1093, Sargood 680,
Zeal 760 ; Melbourne North —Barrett 1233, Fraser 947,
Sargood 876, Zeal 762 ; Melbourne South—Barrett 1052,
Fraser 813, Sargood 650, Zeal 656; Melbourne West —
Barrett 1199, Fraser 713, Sargood 640, Zeal 599 ; Port
Melbourne—Barrett 732, Fraser 571, Sargood 451, Zeal
398 ; Richmond—Barrett 2428, Fraser 1823, Sargood
1516, Zeal 1537 : Williamstown—Barrett 1050, Fraser
1101, Sargood 997, Zeal 873.
In each of these constituencies it ought not to have
been possible for Fraser, Sargood or Zeal to
have polled more than 200 votes each. That the
workers did not do their clear duty is plain from
the fact that all three obstructionists were well
up, and in some cases beat the Labor man.
It is safe to say that Sargood, Zeal and
Fraser, in the order named, have done more to
block liberal and progressive legislation than
all the rest of the 48 Fatmen put together.
Sargood is the recognised leader of the House of
Fat, while Fraser and Zeal are always to the
fore when he wants any assistance in holding
Democracy’s throat. There is no reasonable ex-
planation of the action of the Vic. workers and
Liberals in this instance. Even Trenwith, on
the night of the poll, had to admit that the vote
had astonished him. It is true Fraser and Zeal
were on the Age ticket, but the fact that they
were also on the Argus ticket along with Sargood
and several other of like kidney should have
opened the eyes of Liberals. It is ridiculous to
altogether attribute Fraser’s success to the fact
that he is the Pope of the Orange crowd. Orange-
men are not so strong in Victoria that they can
put a man at the head of the poll, with 77,000
votes to his credit. Then, neither Zeal nor Sar-
good takes any part in the color controversy. It
is equally absurd to say that money is responsible
for the result of the poll, because the workers in
the city and suburbs could have decided the issue
as between Labor and Fat had they stuck to their
own side.


Three Years
Liver Trouble.
CLEMENTS TONIC SOLELY RESPONSIBLE FOR CURE.
The Case of
Mrs. KATHERINE GARLAND.
(BY OUR SPECIAL REPORTER.)
“ Your visit has come as a pleasant
surprise to me,” was the remark with which
Mrs. Garland, of No. 4 Denham-street,
Surry Hiili, greeted the reporter from this
journal, as it saves me the trouble of
writing to you about my bad attack of liver
complaint-, from which I suffered terribly.
I am a very poor correspondent at the best
of times, preferring like most members of
my sex, to receive letters rather than write
them ; but I am so conscious of a deep
obligation to Clements Tonic for restoring
me to health that I made up my mind to
write expr<sung my gratitude, and as you
have obviated that duty 1 will gladly give
the fullest details.
“ How long did your liver trouble afflict
you ? ” asked the reporter.
“ For fully three years, and I hope it will
never come on again. Do you know, I
believe I would rather be dead than
continue like I was then. The first sgn of
my failing health was an unaccountable
fastidiousness regarding my food. I some-
times felt like eating a little, and as soon as
it was before me I should turn right against
it, and do nothing but mince and turn it
over, then finally leave it alone. There
was always a sickly, sour taste in my mouth,
my tongue being so dry and rough,
particularly in the morning, and I was oLen
seized witfi a fit of vomiting, which was
invariably followed by the act of belching
distasteful gases from the stomach. The
operation of ridding myself of the bile and
so >r w.nd from the stomach caused me no
end of discomfort and pain, and blinding
headaches were frequently experienced
after I had been retching. Heavy pains in
the shoulders, and sometimes in the chest,
often paid me more attention than was
agreeable, as they made me form the
opiniou that I was slowly falling into the
clutches of some dreadful disease. Indeed,
this idea was made all the moie likely by
the yellowish hue of my skin, and my eyes
became a dirty green colour. Liver spots
also came out on my chest. Besides the
p dns in the chest and shoulders, I would
have severe attacks in the side, and they
vere so excessively tormenting that I had
to apply hot fomentations, but they only
gave temporary relief, and made the next
attack more acute. It was next door to
impossible to ever get a brief respite from
my many ailments, for 1 could scarcely ever
get to sleep, and the little repose that did
occasionally come to me was marked by
unpleasant dreams and restlessness. On
awakening it required a great stretch of
imagination to believe I had slept at all,
so weary and fatigued would I feel. My
limbs ached with sheer weakness, and I’m
sure I was an object deserving compassion
when crawling about the house in my vain
efforts to do a little housework, only to sit
down quite exhausted and fret for hours.
When I wanted to stand up I had to do so
carefully and steadily, otherwise a fainting
giddiness would take charge of me, and my
head would whirl round like a spinning-
wheel. These faining sensations left me
dreadfully depressed and low-spirited, and
I sat or lay brooding over mv misfortunes
till I got very melancholy. There was not
an ounce of flesh on my bones. So weak and
debilitated had I become that I was almost
afraid to look in the glass.”
“You look remarkably well now, how-
ever, Mrs. Garland.”
“ Yes, thank God, I am like I used to be
bef >re my three years’ sickness But you
have noc learned all my troubles. The
worst and most terrifying thing has yet to
be described. I have not yet mentioned the
anxiety my heart used to cause me. You
do not think I look like a woman whose
heart is diseased, do you ? No ; I’m sure
I dou’t. But I was almost worried to death
with that thought when I was so ill. My
heart used to start suddenly beating with
such violence that I dreaded it stopping or
easing off, in case it would stop for ever.
But it would quieten down after nearly
scaring the life out of me, only to start
again with renewed rapidity. I have since
learned that palpitation of the heart occurs
in liver disease cases when they are very
serious, so mine must have been a very bad
case.”
“And what treatment did you receive
from your doctor?”
“ Oh, the usual—medicines and pills, and
I don’t mind telling you that the liquids
they termed medicines did me no end of
injury. Why, I used to loathe the thought
of swallowing it; the smell was offensive
and the taste was disgusting. But I
persevered with them for three years’ all to
no purpose. Then a friend told me that
Clements Tonic was the best thing for me to
take, which I did, and you can see the
result for yourself. Clements Tonic seemed
a little bitter at first, but I could soon drink
it like water, without any reluctance what-
ever. But even if it had been gall I would
have drauk it, because it brought about my
convalescence in no time. I told my doctor
I would rather have Clements Tonic than all
his stuff, as it was doing me good, and his
physic had completely failed. He replied :
* Well, I’ll not make much out of you now.’
And he spoke truthfully, for I can be
independent of all doctors now. rapidly
experienced ease from puns in the stomach,
chest and shoulders, and the easing of pains
in the head brought delightful, soothing
sleep. My appet.te came back, and I did
not notice any discomfort after eating. The
liver spots were eradicated, and my skin
recovered its usual colour. No more fears
of heart disease worried me, as the palpit-
ation disappeared with the other disagree-
able symptoms. To be as brief as possible,
I must candidly admit that C ements Tonic
is solely responsible for my cure. When my
son-iu-law was up in the mountains he wrote
saying he was sick, and I sent him a couple
of bottles of Clements Tonic to Katoomba.
He subsequently thanked me, stating that
the medicine I had forwarded had cured
him. One of my daughters has been under
treatment during the p'st twelve months
for asthmatical bronchitis (another doctor
says it is heart disease she has got). They
have not done her the least good with their
phasic, so lately she has been taking
Clements Tonic. She has had three bottles
up to now, and she is making good progress
in the right direction. I believe Clements
Tonic will make a woman of her again. My
faith in it is without limit, and I am so
grateful for its effect upon mj self and
relatives that you can publish my testimony
as much as you please.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Kathbrink Garland, of No. 4 Denhamstreet,
Surry Hills, Sydney, in the Colony of New South
Wales, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, consisting of
five folios, and consecutively numbered from one to
five and that it contains and is a true ard faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish in any
wav my statements—which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
tiue, an 1 bv virtue of the provisions of an Act inside
and passed in the ninth year of the reign of her
present Majesty, intituled “An A t for the more
effectual abolition of Oaths and Affirmations taken
and made in the various Departments of the Govern-
ment of New South Wales, and to substitute Declara-
tions in lieu thereof, and for the mppre-s on of
voluntary and Extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”

THE AGED.
According to a Vic. trooper recently re-
turned from S’Africa, amongst the ration biscuits
served to the troops were some which had been
years in store at Durban, yet were perfectly
sound and good. Edible granite !
A poser for piety. According to S.F. Call,
f, Chinese lately asked an American missionary
.'' ' ia t for you want me in your heaven and not
in your country?” The scoffer’s answer, of
course, is that the Yankee man only pretends to
believe in Heaven, but his belief in his country is
all-wool.
Compare the time-loss through strikes and
lockouts with the time-loss through such a war as
that in S’Africa, and what a wretched little total
the former amounts to ! Last year, according to
British returns, the former loss was 3,785,000 days
(2,516,416 days in 1890); while, allowing 200,000
men on service, 50,000 in transporting and equip-
ping them, and 20,000 thrown idle in S’Africa, the
time-loss through the war in the 12 months (work-
ing days only) amounted to 83,700,000 days.
This war has played the deuce with
Kipling’s reputation as an authority on swaddies.
Really Kip.’s experience of soldiers was very
limited, but he darted into the subject with such
assurance and so convincing an air of omniscience
that the British public was bull-dozed, and it took
its Rudyard for granted, and at a gulp. Kipling
has simply drugged his public with the idea that
earth holds nothing to resist a regiment of British
soldiers officered by “English gentlemen.” But
the most pertinent and convincing teaching of the
Boer battles exposes the sawdust stuffing of Kip-
ling’s grave, calm, reticent, god-like young Eng-
lish official of good family, by gad, and we find
him too often a rabbit-toothed, chinless, scatter-
brain dude, without wit enough to lead a hungry
ammunition-mule to a bag of carrots. Who from
this out will read of those inspired sucklings in
11 A Conference of the Powers,” for instance,
without grinning b ick to his ears in bitter deri-
non ?

The Expiation of Ralph
Lnttrell.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A sawmill in the he:irt of the ran pros, the air
resonant with the cracking of bullock-whips, the
g-r-r-r of the huge circular saw, the jerking jar of
the “ vertical,” the rheumatic panting of the
steam-engine, the whirr of machinery-belting, the
clanking of log-chains and cant-hooks, the distant
rhythmic echo of the feller’s axe, the cling-clang
of bullock-bells, the jangling of iron upon iron,
and vocal with shouts and objurgation ; the land-
scape vivid with red shirts and blue, and the color
of cattle and salmon-hued sawdust. It was a red,
fervid day of dust and smiting sun-glare—a blis-
tering, smoky afternoon, when the sky hung in a
dense fog about the tops of the eucalypti, and
far-away bush-fires blurred the blue over-head and
made the air dance and quiver like the fierce breath
of a blast-furnace. The westering sun seemed
suspended like a huge ball of glowing copper
above the panting earth, and cast a myriad fire-
suns into the slush-pools left by the short, sharp,
tropical storm of the morning—pools which had
been churned up in the timber-yard by the jug-
gernaut wheels of the ponderous bullock-waggons
into viscid chocolate-coloured lakes. The scent of
burning timber was in the air, mingling with the
dust of the day and the various odours of eucalyp-
ti and heaped damp saw-dust, and the port-wine
bouquet of freshly-sawn messmate. Amid such a
scene, and on such a day was Sal Gardle’s good
name and fame re-established at Wyndabit, and
Ralph Luttrell chained for life to the expiation of
a passing folly.
Now Ralph Luttrell, or, as the hands employed
at the Wyndabit steam sawmills called him, with
ironical emphasis, “Mister” Luttrell, was the
bookkeeper, manager and “boss” in general of
his uncle’s mountain estate, and an undying object
■ of curiosity and speculation to all who lived and
were maimed and died there. A city-bred young
fellow was he, of some four or five-arid-twenty
summers, as strong, tall, stalwart and well-knit as
any bush man, possessing a handsome enough face,
with determined, regular features, and a firm
mouth, brown, curly, hair, and a masterful, dare-
devil, somewhat supercilious manner —particularly
efficacious with the other sex. He was rather
respected than liked by the men, but regarded
with unbounded admiration by the girls and
the women of Wyndabit, while the schoolboys
looked up to him as their ideal of a possible
“Captain Starlight”—that hero so dear to the
hearts and fancy of the bushranger of the play-
ground.
But young Luttrell was in disgrace, and the
bush sojourn was one of probation, not of choice.
His master and employer was also his uncle, one
Richard Bradshaw, owner of the Wyndabit steam
sawmills, and also of three other great timber
depots in different parts of the country, a mam-
moth farmer, a squatter, a merchant, a member of
the colony’s Upper House, a man who fared but
seldom into the tree-clad ranges whence much of
his wealth was reaped, where men were slain and
crippled, and women left husbandless and soilless,
and children fatherless, in the ordinary course of
a day’s work ; where the metal sisterhood of
the huge saws played havoc with life and
limb; and where, moreover, his nephew,
Ralph Ruttrell, languished on hard fare,
and scant amusement, and stifled curses,
raging furiously in a reticent solitude, eating Ins
own heart out. No, the great Richard Brad-
shaw’s place of residence was a, mansion in a
suburb of the great metropolis, his nominal place
of business a palatial office in its chief commer-
cial thoroughfare, his place of recreation m its
senatorial halls ; while his reputation was just
sufficiently good to bear public auditing m the
cold print of a hostile press.
Ralph Ruttrell was the onlv son of a favorite
and favored sister of the millionaire magnate,
who designed for his nephew something pompously
announced as a “career,” and by whose nepotistic
offices the lad had obtained a post and continued
advancement in the Civil Service of the colony.
Some act of wildness, or worse, had put a summary
stop to young Luttrell’s intended course of glorj,
and lie was now tasting all the bitterness to one
wholly town-bred of exile in the bush. Rather,
to be strictly accurate, he had just completed
his two years’ term of penance and of
purgatory, and was already awaiting, with much
expectation and many plans of Spanish architec-
tural evolvement, his manumission from .Nature
and saw-mill Man, and his rehabilitation in a
society that speaks bad English only when it is
sanctioned by Fashion—and at this juncture,
when all looked so bright and fair, he was about
to receive Iris coup dr. <trace from the redoubtable
“ Bronze-wing ” (Ruttrell-christened), queen of
saws, and slayer of men and destroyer of castles
prematurely budded in Spain.
The young manager’s two years of probation
had been two years of dolor and ennui, modified
to some small extent by flirtations, clandestine
and discreet, with the prettier of the maidens ot
the bush, with almost an affaire du cceur of the
more serious kind with Sally Gardle, the prettiest,
and sister of Bill Gardle, stalwart sawyer,
strongest and smartest henchman in the ranges ot
Wvndabit, flitch-master, prime-minister to Her
Majesty, “ Bronzewing,” Queen of Saws. A
mighty man was Bill Gardle, huge, broad-
shouldered—a splendid specimen of bush virility.
Much handling of heavy flitches had given him
the biceps of a Cyclops. His spreading chest ex-
cited feelings of irritation throughout three
districts of timber-men, and his great laugh echoed
loudly in the mill eaves above the clang of the
saws. Few dared face him in a fight, and his
temper was as short and as crisp as the black-
brown curls that clustered on Ins sun-tanned
forehead. Big of limb and loud of voice,
bronzed and hard as the gums he handled, swart,
muscular, and bushy of beard and moustache.
Bill Gardle was the envy of man and the admired
of woman in all Wyndabit and its neighbourhood.
In the days of his youth lie had achieved some-
what of reputation in the role of bush Lothario.
But all that was now passed. Bill had settled
down, and could leave his tale of three orphans,
like any other timber-man, when his end came—
for the life of a benclunan is not statistically re-
corded in assurance life-tables as one of longevity.
Being now a bulwark of busli respectability, Bill
Gardle could hardly be expected to look with
kindly eyes on the faux pas of youthful folly ; in a
case where a sister was concerned it would be safe to
prophesy of him speedy action and vehement ven-
geance, and Bill was beginning to suspect. Not that
young Luttrell had given him cause for suspicion.
Richard Bradshaw’s nephew was much too clever
and acute to publish his amours for the benefit ot
the Scandal-mongers of the hamlet. Nevertheless
Bill had surprised his sister in tears more than
once—tears not to be accounted for m the kindly
home of her brother and his wife, and of the chil-
dren, upon whom the girl doted. And the burly
henchman brooded in silence, and wondered,
ilis wife, however, and some of the gossips of the
place noted what Bill did not, and heads were
shaken, some pleasurably —as will always happen
when the victim is passing pretty —but fear, so tar,
had tongue-tied comment.
Poor Sally was no bride for Cultures wooing.
Her speech was the slang of the bush, and her
accent its nerve-jarring twang, ller manners were
irritatingly brusque; her voice was loud, her
tattle interminable ; her love of scandal and small
gossip fitted with painful precision her circum-
scribed environment, and she had all the large
vanity of the lilliputian r-oul. To set against her
faults, she had charms and virtues more than
enough for abstract appraisement; but not enough
for Culture’s wife to wear in a drawing-room to
Culture's gratification, and the wife of one who
100 Its forward to a career must be able to wear a
coronet when emergency is claimant. It was not
enough that Sally's heart should be a depthless
well of truth and constancy ; that Sally should be
a sunburnt Dian, tall, lithe, graceful as a fawn ;
that she could swim the creeks like a water-nymph,
run like an antelope, climb like a dryad, and
dance like the daughter of Herodias. bally, poor
girl, like many another country girl who yields
her heart to the gay cavalier who loves and who
rides away, was foredoomed— Ralph Luttrell was
on the eve of departure for the city. Ho awaited
only the summons from his uncle. Winter lodged
in Sally’s cheerless heart —and it was summer-time.
• • • *
The sun blazed redly through the angry glare of
dust and low-hanging smoke, and licked up, here
and there, patches of moisture left by the morn-
ing’s tropic storm. Presently the mill-boys were
running with buckets of water, wherewith to
extinguish fires in the log-yard caused by sparks
from the engine igniting the fluffy bark stripped
from the messmate giants. On the mill s shingled
roof, too, was a constant splashing out of incipient
flames. The corduroy smoked and steamed
in the heat, but the timber-yard was well-nigh knee-
deep in mud and drainage, and the rutted road-
way was passable only by tender-footers. liaipn
Luttrell, vehement embodiment of energy, was
here there, everywhere —directing, commanding,
rushing from mill up to office, writing out timber
dockets, supervising the cutting of logs into their
necessary lengths, serving out oil for engine-
drivers and “vertical” foreman, and tallow tor
benchmen and tailers-out, making up _ orders for
the bullock-teams clustering in the timber-yard
and anxious to draw out well on the road before
sunset, and performing the thousand-and-one
functions that pertained to his complicated office.
One thing, however, he did not do ; he never
went near the bench presided over by the man
whose sister he had wronged, whose sister lie in-
tended to desert. .
In some saw-mills hangs a blackboard, just
above the circular saw-bench, and on this black-
board is written in chalk, after consultation with
his order-book, the clerk’s list of lengths and sizes
for the henchman’s direction during the days cut-
ting. There was such a blackboard in the null of
Wyndabit, but since young Luttrell’s liaison with
Bill Gardle’s sister it had been his wont to write
out his order-list prior to the henchman’s advent
and thus avoid speaking to the man Sometimes
with the rush of a sudden order that has instantly
to be attended to, the blackboard s d.rectioua
to the henchman are supplemented by chalked
requirements on the ends of tie '
but apart from all this, it is an understood
thing that all good, sound messmate logs, from
10ft kto 20ft., sliould be cut only into weather-
boards—for the weatherboard and the arris-rail
are things apart, the culture-productions of the
hardwood saw. A magnificent, sound
10ft. messmate, Oft. through at the butt, without
knot, or bole, or gum-vein, or shake, flawless and
impeccable, had just been ripped into flitches by
the vertical, and canted on to the greased skids
that feed the trollies that feed the wturnng cir-
cular, swiftly revolving m its wel -greased bench
Benchman Gardle was absent-minded to-day-■
was thinking dully of Sally, whom he had found
in tears in the back-kitchen as lie hastily passed
through from his dinner. Bill Gardie was brood-
ing. lie gave a short glance at the flitch trolhed
over the bench to him. “ A ten-foot. bawled the
tailer-out, and without thinking of the quality
of the flitch he was handling, with a
tailing corner-wise the black-board and catching
an inconsequent entry relatmgto ten-feet three-by-
two oblivious altogetner of weatherboards, Bill
Gardle began to slash out century-enduring
“ quartering.” Ralph . Luttrell. exercised with
the conduct of the entire mnl, knew nothing of
this eccentric behaviour on the part of the hench-
man, but went on splashing through the mud of
the timber-yard, ordering, shouting, swearing
as is the use and wont of timber- bosses the
wide bush over —the heavy water-tight knee-boots
a-cake with chocolate-coloured slime, his belt
buckled tightly about '‘is waist his red
shirt unbuttoned and open at the neck, lus hat,
broad-leaved, pushed backward from his open
brow on which the brown curls glued themselves
in the trickling sweat, lus whole body tense with
masterful energy. It was almost a .quarter-of-an-
hour ere his business led him into the mill, in time
to find the yard-man pushing away a truck of ten-
feet “quartering,” and a sudden rage shook him
at the wanton waste of Weatherboard messmate.
He bounded down in great strides, snatching from
an astonished mill-boy a piece of chalk, and mak-
ing towards the bench with white anger m his face
-and there is not an inch of room to spare
between skids and tram-track on which the
trollies run, though space enough for sudden
death. High and clear rang the song of the saw
in a cold metallic tenor, with a temale malice m it
too, and the belting with ceaseless wlurr hummed
a subdued accompaniment.
* • * *
“ Bronzewing ” was a five-footer, with a rip-cut
of twenty-seven inches. Her color gave her this
name of Ralph Luttrell’s bestowal, but she had
christened herself “Siva” vyrth much spilth of
men’s blood and some orphans tears. Baby
“Ringer,” who whirred in the sawdust pit, was a
pigmy in comparison, used only for pointing
pickets and cutting firewood for the maw of the
“ Circular ” engine ; but even the Baby had a, man
to her credit. He was merely a “ cocky, who
had brought a rheumatic-legged horse and ascrew-
pingly-ricketty dray, while the mill-hands were all
at dinner, to get him a load of face-cuts where-
with to erect a skillion. He backed his crazy
vehicle right on the crest of the saw-dust ridge,
whence to skid sheer for some twelve feet down,
where the Baby clicked and clanged at the bottom
throughout the dinner-hour-Bob, the engine-
driver, being too lazy to unslip the driving-belt.

The lion of the saws was, of course, “The Mas-

was a lion guiltless of violence save on messmates
ot Gargantuan diameter, which had to he guttered

and other sets of saws in use and abuse at Wyn-

them, and the queen of these was “Bronzewing”
—the tigress of them all.
* * * •
Close behind the tailer-out and his mate, the
flitch-man— along the narrow-gauge tramway
which carried the flitch-troll v, impatiently pushed
Ralph Luttrell, chalk in hand, a thunder cloud
lowering on his brows, his mouth trap-clamped,
and an ice-cold fire glinting in blue eyes that had
heated to steel-grey. The flitch was pushed
across the bench along the side of the saw, which
was clicking and whirring and singing and hum-
rnltig .its fateful solo, the while it threw at
the vision only an ungraspable picture of a
disc of blurred motion. But heeding no-
nothing, forgetful of danger and of “ Bronze-
wing,” forgetful of the mud - encased boots
that allowed of no leg-bending between ankle
and knee, holding in thrall even the tendons
of the hock ; forgetful of everything save anger,
Ralph Luttrell sprang upon the bench, and leant
sideways to write in mammoth letters the word
“ WEATHERBOARDS ’’—with 27in. of relentless
serrated metal disc whirling death and eternity
between his outstretched, hoot-hampered legs.
And as he leant, he slipped, and was held over
the edge of Hell by the strong hands of Bill
Gardle, the benchman, who kept his giant’s grip
firm till the engine could be stopped and “ Bronze-
wing’s ” maw cheated of the choicest morsel of
human food that ever happened on her sacrificial
altar.
• * * »
Renunciations are to be measured by the moral
and intellectual stature of the renouncer. The
Great Renunciation was a small thing to the great
soul of Gautama. Ralph Luttrell conquered his
ambition and destroyed his future and his life in
a brief moment of intensest agony, more horrible
to bear than the fear of fearful death when he
trembled with blanched cheeks and a blood-for-
saken heart over the cruel teeth of “Bronze-
wing.” The struggle lasted during one ghastly
second of keenest pain, and was attributed by ail
the terror-stricken men who surrounded him
merely to natural causes—but it wasn’t. Grasp-
ing in a grip of iron the large palm of the huge
benchman, he gasped in a husky, broken voice
only the words, pregnant to him with pathetic
meaning, ‘ ‘ I’ll marry Sal ! ”
N.S.W.
F.J. BROOMFIELD.
The late Queen’s good health and conse-
quent longevity were largely attributable,- it is
stated on high medical authority, to her remark-
ably simple daily regimen. Plain beef, it seems,
was her favorite article of diet, and whisky-and-
water her only stimulant. Beef and whisky-and-
water, and sound health into the 9th decade !
Invercargill (M.L.) is getting an un-
savoury reputation in connection with the small
girl with a story. The latest instance is that of a
man charged with rape by a nice little female of
12 or so. A young relative of hers was put in the
box, but under cross-examination confessed that be
was the real offender, and that the nice little girl
had proposed that the blame should be put on the
man. Moral as usual.
“New Century Permit,” now being circu-
lated :
This is to certify that I,
the lawful wedded wife of
do during the first twelve (12) months of this century
hereby permit my husband to go where he pleases, drink
what he pleases, and when he pleases, and I furthermore
permit him to keep and enjoy the company of any lady
or ladies he sees fit, as I know he is a good judge. I
want him to enjoy life, as he will be a long time dead.

Demise : Last week of January, Baron
Wilhelm Von Rothschild, universally known as
“Baron Willy,” grandson of the founder. One of
the family has always stuck to Frankfort, and
this devolved on Willy. Very much the most pious
of all the Rothschilds, who are mostly inclined to
scepticism, partly from a family taste for allowing
the Jesuits a hand in their education. A great
man for charity. While he would quietly give
away enormous sums, he was most exact' about
the pence. He cut off a household item of milk
for the cat. Says Willy : “If the cat gets mice,
she don’t want milk. If there are no mice, we
don’t want any cat.” King Ned, as Prince of
Wales, and his sister, the German Empress
Dowager, were very partial to him. He would
never do a fraction of business on Sabbath. He
was the great pillar of the Orthodox as against
the Reforming Jews, and it is believed the
Reformers will now taka a great start.
The Faded Flame.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out of the years has gone
The Yellow Light
That set red haloes in our hair,
And lit the wide way we might fare
Into Christ’s sight.
Oh, blinding, burning Light of Gold and Graces
Shining up paths for eager, springing feet,
What subtle gold thy nightly masking was,
And was there ever morning wand as sweet 1
Out of the years has gone
The Mystic Light:
Christ’s face is hidden, and the Cross hangs dim,
Hangs dim and shadowy, void of him,
All day, all night.
Oh, early years of utt sr passionate Faith,
Oh, tender heart strings, ever tremulous,
When Goodness was our beckoning Morning Star,
And Christ and Paradise hung over us.
Wait ! and the years will bring
One smile, one face,
A little earthly human smile ;
And Earth will dream she holds awhile
The Perfect Place.
Ah, little earthly human smile and eyes 1
The yellow Light has gone, but Heaven is here.
Ah, earthly hands that cling, and eyes that bless,
And lips that cling and bless, the way is dear 1
And soon there must return
The Golden Light,
Gone silver where it once was red,
And crystal where the gold is dead,
But very bright.
And even if Christ comes not as of yore,
And that dead faith can never more arise,
Has He not flamed His Message through that
smile,
Those eyes, that surely have seen Paradise ?
Wait, and the years will bring
Their high white light—
One that will never fade or fail,
A torch fed from the Holy Grail,
By day, by night.
And then, because you worshipped in the green
And early leafing of your days of Prayer,
Out of the long sere seasons there will spring
Meanings for Grief, and Hope for all Despair.
EASTER, 1901.
LOUISE MACK.
Waiting.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was eight o’clock. The streets were half-
emptied by the theatres and hotels, and people
hurried from the noisy trams eager to begin their
night’s pleasure. There were half-a-dozen persons
waiting at the corner. A middle-aged, worried-
looking man, two brightly-dressed women who
chatted together, a couple of dowdily-clad women
pacing wearily up and down, and a smartly-
attired young man who fidgetted with his watch-
chain and darted his head about like a shy bird at
the edge of a cornfield.
A bright moon hung with serene contempt over
the city. A cold electric light illumined the
corner and threw darkly-defined shadows behind
the waiting figures. Two noisy - voiced men
jumped off a tram and joined the gaily-dressed
women. The young man pulled out his watch,
and then glanced uneasily at the Town Hall clock.
He dug his hands deep into his pockets and
kicked impatiently at a verandah-post. “ I
wonder if she will come,” he thought, and then
his eager eyes sought with a start of hope a
woman strolling down the street. His heart
withered as the middle-aged man went briskly
forward to meet her. “She won’t come to-night.
I’ll never speak to her again. I won’t be treated
like this.”
The thought of the pleasant night on the beach
that was slipping into the purgatory of might-
have-been delights frightened him. He walked a
dozen yards, tried to engage his mind with ashop
window, and returned to look at the clock. If
she did come he would be cool and distant. His
heart went out to the two lonely women, and his
eyes ached with searching the street for the well-
known figure. He would never speak to her
again, and he went suddenly hot to think that
she might be with another man. To-night would
end everything. A vision of the cool beach, the
girl, and the pleasureof it all, swept over his brain,
and he felt his face moist with anxious sweat.
One—two—five women that might have been
the girl passed him, and he hungrily followed
them with his eyes. Their steps, as they passed
away, sounded like funeral-beats at his heart. .
She was not coming. . . . He would go away and
get drunk. There were other women, and he was
sick of deceitful love. He hated the poisonous
city ; the clanging tram-bells tortured his brain
and the people who passed seemed to look
strangely at him—the deserted, the outcast 1 Why
should he stand there to be made the mockery of a
woman ?
It was all over—a great sense of gaping loneli-
ness, and a vicious hatred for the chattering fools
who passed him, possessed his being. Another
minute and he would go—anywherd—do any-
thing—
A girl touched him on the shoulder. “Good-
night, Jim. You can’t be angry with me to-night!
Look at the clock—why, I’ve only kept you wait-
ing exactly three minutes.”
VIC.
ONLYS.
The ignorance of the Tory jingo gets
wearisome after a while. A M.L. specimen writes
thus, in an alleged poem :
For they’re tempting us to stay, with good billets and
good pay,
And grants of veldt and bullocks by the score—
Not your everlasting lease, fit for socialistic geese.
But freehold land which Britons all adore !
Ivrugeroo-geroo-geray !
Be it barren sand or clay,
Better own it any day,
Than slave for rent. . . .
This is supposed to be a really nasty knock at the
Maoriland land-settlement policy. But think
of the Briton—the only man left'in Europe bar-
ring the Portuguese who allows Bis country to be
owned by a few dozen huge landlords—the man
from a country where practically the whole nation
slaves for rent —being so particular about his free-
hold that he must shout out “ Krugeroo-geroo-
geray !”

The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the clashing ballads and verses of sailor-men's
and wharf-men’s life which IS. J. Brady has contributed
to Tub Biidlutin and the Sunday Timbs, Sydney. It is
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse*
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
‘ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing ; and the price is Os.,
post-free.
